Analyzing General-Special Relations between Shumus and Mukhadarat
Anbabi analyzes the general-special relationship between the two metaphors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a 'general-specific from one aspect' relationship and an 'absolute general-specific' relationship using the example of shumus and mukhadarat.

Core Idea
Two terms can relate by overlapping partially or one containing the other entirely.

In this passage, al-Anbabi explores two possible logical relationships between the metaphors 'shumus' (suns) and 'mukhadarat' (hidden ones). He shows that depending on how we define their scope, they either intersect in some instances or one encompasses the other. Understanding these relationships is essential for correctly applying these terms to different types of issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate 'shumus' and 'mukhadarat' in the text. Understand what they metaphorically represent: shumus refer to issues that are easy with much benefit, while mukhadarat refer to issues that are difficult (with varying benefit).
These are technical terms in the context of the lesson on adab al-bahth.
2
Examine the first possible relation (umuman wa khususan wajhiyyan)
In this relation, the two terms overlap in some instances: both apply to difficult issues with much benefit. Each also has exclusive instances: mukhadarat alone cover difficult issues with little benefit, and shumus alone cover easy issues with much benefit.
This is a partial overlap relation.
3
Examine the second possible relation (umum wa khusus mutlaq)
In this alternative, one term is completely included in the other. Here, mukhadarat require both difficulty and much benefit, while shumus only require much benefit. Thus shumus is the more general term (any issue with much benefit) and mukhadarat is the specific (difficult issues with much benefit).
The text states that difficulty and much benefit are both considered in mukhadarat, but only much benefit in shumus.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Construct a sample issue: 'A difficult theological question with great reward.' Under the first relation, it falls in the overlap. Under the second relation, it is a mukhadarat (since both conditions met). An easy issue with much reward is only shumus in both relations.
Practice by testing a few examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ إِنَّ بين المخدّرات والشُّموس : «عموماً وخصوصاً وجهيًا»: فتجتمع الشُّموس والمخدرات في المسائل الصعبة الكثيرة النفع. وتنفرد المخدّرات في الصعبة القليلة النفع والشموس في كثيرة النَّفع السهلة. ويحتمل أن يكون بينهما : «عموم وخصوص مطلق»: - فتعتبر الصُّعوبة وكثرة النفع معاً في المخدرات. وتعتبر كثرة النفع فقط في الشموس . قوله : (وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ يُشَبَّةَ الخَفَاءُ) أي: المتعلّق بالأمور المعنوية كـ المسائل، وقوله : (بِمَعْنَى التَّخْدِيرِ) أي : المتعلق بالأمور الحسية كـ المرأة، وقوله : (بِجَامِعِ : عَدَمِ الظُّهُورِ ) أي : عدم ظهور [ج/ ٦] الأمور المطلقة الشَّاملة للحسّيّة والمعنوية، فتدبَّر . وأما على الاحتمالين الأولين فالإضافة فيها على معنى : مِن أيضاً إن أريد مِنَ المُخَدَّرَات»: المعارف، وأبقيت المعرفة على حقيقتها، وكذا إن أريد بها المسائل، وأريد مِنَ المعرفة»: المعروف، وأما إن أريد بها : المسائل، وأبقيت «المعرفة» على حقيقتها، فالإضافة على معنى: «اللام»، كما سبق.
Then indeed between 'mukhadarat' and 'shumus' there is a general-special relation (general-specific from one aspect): the 'shumus' and 'mukhadarat' meet in the difficult issues with much benefit. And the 'mukhadarat' are unique in difficult issues with little benefit, and the 'shumus' in easy issues with much benefit. And it is possible that between them there is an absolute general-special relation: so difficulty and much benefit are both considered in 'mukhadarat', and only much benefit is considered in 'shumus'. His statement (and that by likening the absence) i.e. related to conceptual matters like issues, and his statement (with the meaning of concealment) i.e. related to sensory matters like a woman, and his statement (with the commonality of non-appearance) i.e. the non-appearance of absolute matters that encompass sensory and conceptual, so reflect. And on the first two possibilities, the idafa in it is with the meaning of 'min' as well, if from 'mukhadarat' is intended: knowledge, and knowledge is kept on its reality, and likewise if by it is intended: issues, and from 'knowledge' is intended: the known. And if by it is intended: issues, and 'knowledge' is kept on its reality, then the idafa is with the meaning of 'lam', as before.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an expansion by al-Anbabi analyzing the logical relationship between the two key terms used in the matn: 'shumus' (suns) and 'mukhadarat' (hidden ones). He considers two possibilities. First, they have a 'general and specific from one aspect' (umum wa khusus wajhi) relationship: they intersect in difficult issues with much benefit; 'mukhadarat' are uniquely for difficult issues with little benefit, and 'shumus' for easy issues with much benefit. Second, they might have an absolute general-special relation ('umum wa khusus mutlaq): here 'mukhadarat' combine difficulty and much benefit, while 'shumus' only require much benefit (regardless of difficulty). Then al-Anbabi explains Bajuri's earlier statement about the metaphor, connecting the hiddenness of conceptual matters (like issues) with the concealment of sensory matters (like a woman). He also discusses the grammatical meaning of the idafa (construct) in 'mukhadarat al-ma'rifa' or 'mukhadarat al-shumus', noting that it can be a 'min' (partitive) or 'lam' (possessive) depending on interpretation, as previously explained.

Key Terms
عموماً وخصوصاً وجهياً
عموم وخصوص مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the 'general-specific from one aspect' interpretation, what do 'shumus' and 'mukhadarat' have in common?
A) They both refer to easy issues
B) They both refer to issues with little benefit
C) They both refer to difficult issues with much benefit
D) They both refer to sensory matters

Key Takeaway
Two terms can relate either by overlapping partially or by one encompassing the other; identifying the correct relation is crucial for precise logical classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the terms 'fard' (obligatory) and 'sunnah' (recommended) in fiqh. Determine whether they have a 'general-specific from one aspect' relationship or an 'absolute general-specific' relationship. Justify your answer by listing shared and unique instances.
?
Identify a pair of terms from tafsir (e.g., 'muhkam' and 'mutashabih') that exhibit an 'umum wa khusus mutlaq' relationship. Explain which is the general and which the specific based on their definitions.

The Three Types of Indication Are Conventional
Author confirms that the three types of indication are conventional, with strongest view for the latter two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of indication and state the majority position on their conventional nature, including the alternative opinions.

Core Idea
The three types of indication are conventional by majority, but inclusion and commitment have dissenting views.

In Islamic logic, the classification of indication (dalala) into three types—correspondence, inclusion, and commitment—is foundational for understanding how words convey meaning. This passage confirms the author's explicit statement that all three are conventional (waḍʿiyya), i.e., dependent on linguistic stipulation. While correspondence is unanimously accepted as conventional, the other two types have been debated, with the majority of logicians maintaining that they too are conventional. Let us examine the passage and the nuances of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of indication
The passage mentions three types: correspondence (muṭābiqiyya), inclusion (taḍammuniyya), and commitment (iltizāmiyya). Note that these correspond to the three ways a linguistic expression refers to its meaning.
Correspondence: the whole word refers to the whole meaning. Inclusion: part of the meaning. Commitment: a meaning necessarily implied.
2
Locate the point of agreement
The author states that describing indication as conventional (waḍʿiyya) is 'a point of agreement regarding correspondence' (maḥallu wifāqin fī al-muṭābiqiyya). This means all scholars agree that correspondence is conventional.
The term 'waḍʿiyya' here applies to all three, but the agreement is only explicit for correspondence.
3
Identify the majority view for inclusion and commitment
The passage says, 'As for inclusion and commitment, it is based on the soundest transmitted opinion from most logicians' (ʿalā al-aṣaḥḥ al-manqūl ʿan akthar al-manāṭiqa). This indicates that the majority consider them conventional as well.
The author cites al-ʿUnaymī and others as reporting this majority view.
4
Recognize the alternative opinions
There are two other opinions: one says both inclusion and commitment are rational (ʿaqliyyatān), and the second opinion is left incomplete in the text, but it likely suggests one is conventional and one rational. Understand that these are minority views.
Rational indication (ʿaqliyya) depends on intellectual inference, not linguistic stipulation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَصْفُ المُصَنِّفِ الدَّلَالَةَ بـ (الوَضْعِيَّةِ) صَرِيحٌ فِي أَنَّ هَذِهِ الأَنْوَاعَ الثَّلَاثَةَ وَضْعِيَّةٌ، وَهُوَ مَحَلُّ وفَاقٍ فِي المُطَابِقِيَّةِ، وَأَمَّا فِي التَّضَمُّنِيَّةِ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةِ فَهُوَ عَلَى الأَصَحُ المَنْقُولِ عَنْ أَكْثَرِ المَنَاطِقَةِ، كَمَا قَالَهُ العُنيمِيُّ وَغَيْرُهُ؛ وَوَرَاءَهُ قَوْلَانِ أَحَدُهُمَا : أَنَّهُمَا عَقْلِيَّتَانِ، ثَانِيَهُمَا :
And the author's description of indication as 'conventional' is explicit that these three types are conventional. And this is a point of agreement regarding correspondence. As for inclusion and commitment, it is based on the soundest transmitted opinion from most logicians, as said by al-'Unaymi and others. And besides it there are two opinions: one of them is that they are both rational, and the second is:
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a brief statement by the author about the nature of the three types of conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya). The author confirms that the description of indication as conventional by the original author (al-muṣannif) explicitly indicates that all three types — correspondence (muṭābaqa), inclusion (taḍammun), and commitment (iltizām) — are conventional. This is uncontested for correspondence (muṭābiqiyya). For the other two, the soundest view (al-aṣaḥḥ) transmitted from most logicians, as reported by al-'Unaymi and others, is that they are also conventional. However, there are two other opinions: the first says they are both rational (ʿaqliyyatān), and the second opinion is incomplete in the text but likely states that one is conventional and one rational. The author does not elaborate further here, but the reader is expected to know that the majority view is that inclusion and commitment are conventional because they require a conventional setting of the whole (for inclusion) or the correlated meaning (for commitment).

Key Terms
وَضْعِيَّة
عَقْلِيَّة
مُطَابِقَة
تَضَمُّن
اِلْتِزَام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of indication is unanimously agreed to be conventional (waḍʿiyya)?
A) Correspondence (muṭābaqa)
B) Inclusion (taḍammun)
C) Commitment (iltizām)
D) All three are disputed

Key Takeaway
The majority of logicians consider all three types of indication (correspondence, inclusion, commitment) to be conventional, with unanimous agreement on correspondence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the Quranic verse 'The thief, male and female, cut off their hands' (Sūrat al-Māʾida, 5:38). Identify the three types of indication from this verse: what does the word 'hand' indicate by correspondence? By inclusion? By commitment? Then determine which are conventional and why.
?
Consider the alternative opinion that inclusion and commitment are rational. Construct a counterargument using the example of 'wine' (khamr) in 'Wine is forbidden': does the inclusion (intoxicating property) require a rational inference or conventional knowledge? Argue from the perspective of a logician who holds the majority view.

Division and Classification
beginner

Binary Division of Words: Mufrad and Murakkab
Bajuri defines compound and simple words, introduces the division, and refines definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and distinguish between mufrad and murakkab according to the binary division in Mantiq, and classify Arabic examples correctly.

Core Idea
Words are either simple or compound; the test lies in whether a part indicates part of the meaning.

This lesson unpacks the foundational classification of words in Mantiq. Al-Bajuri explains that only used words (musta'mal) are divided into compound (murakkab) and simple (mufrad). Understanding this division is crucial because all subsequent logical analysis—from definitions to syllogisms—operates on either simple terms or composed statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope
Notice that the division applies only to 'musta'mal' (used) words, excluding 'muhmal' (neglected) which have no meaning. This prevents meaningless strings from being classified.
2
Extract the definitions
Write down: Murakkab is 'that whose part indicates part of its meaning'. Mufrad is the opposite: 'that whose part does not indicate part of its meaning'. The 'part' can be a letter, syllable, or component word.
In the ternary division, murakkab is narrower, but here it includes mu'allaf.
3
Apply the test to examples
For each word, ask: Does any part independently signify a piece of the overall meaning? 'Zayd' (mufrad: 'Z' and 'ayd' have no meaning). 'Ghulam Zayd' (murakkab: 'ghulam' means slave, 'Zayd' means person). 'Abdullah' as a proper name (murakkab: 'abd' and 'Allah' each carry meaning).
Particles and single verbs are mufrad because their parts do not signify parts of the meaning.
4
Understand the binary vs. ternary dispute
Some scholars split murakkab into murakkab (proper names like 'Abdullah') and mu'allaf (predicative compounds like 'Zayd standing'). Bajuri insists the binary division is correct, equating murakkab with mu'allaf. This simplifies classification.
The dispute hinges on whether the part's meaning is 'part of the word's meaning' or external to it.
5
Check the exclusion of muhmal
Since muhmal words have no meaning, the test cannot be applied. So they are outside the division entirely. This shows that division presupposes meaningfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ. (٢٦) مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ حَيْثُ يُوجَدُ : إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ. فَأَوَّلٌ : مَا دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزُءِ مَعْنَاهُ بِعَكْسِ مَاتَلَا. [الباجوري] قَوْلُهُ : (فَصْلٌ فِي مَبَاحِثِ الأَلْفَاظِ) أَيْ : فِي المَسَائِلِ الَّتِي يُبْحَثُ فِيهَا عَنِ الْأَلْفَاظِ مِنْ حَيْثُ الإِفْرَادِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ، وَمَا يُلائِمُهُمَا كَالكُلِّيَّةِ والجُزْيَّة. فَ المَبَاحِثُ بِمَعْنَى : المَسَائِلِ المَذْكُورَةِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا جَمْعُ : مَبْحَتْ بِمَعْنَى : مَكَانِ البَحْثِ، وَهُوَ فِي الأَصْلِ : التَّفْتِيسُ عَنْ بَاطِنِ الشَّيْءٍ حِسَّا»، ثُمَّ اسْتُعْمِلَ عُرْفاً فِي بَيَانِ الشَّيْءِ وَالكَشْفِ عَنْهُ ، فَقَوْلُهُمْ: مَبْحَثُ كَذَا بِمَعْنَى : مَكَانِ بَيَانِهِ وَالكَشْفِ عَنْهُ ، وَذَلِكَ المَكَانُ كِنَايَةٌ عَنِ المَسَائِلِ الَّتِي يُبْحَثُ فِيهَا عَنْهُ. وَبِقَوْلِنَا : مِنْ حَيْثُ ... إلخ» انْدَفَعَ مَا قَدْ يُقَالُ : كَلَامُهُ يَقْتَضِي أَنَّ مَبْحَثَ الدَّلَالَاتِ لَيْسَ مِنْ مَبَاحِثِ الأَلْفَاظِ ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، فَتَأَمَّلْ. قَوْلُهُ : (مُسْتَعْمَلُ الأَلْفَاظِ ... إلخ) أي : المُسْتَعْمَلُ مِنْهَا، فَالْإِضَافَةُ عَلَى مَعْنَى مِنْ». وَخَرَجَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ : المُهْمَلُ، فَلَا يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى ذَلِكَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لَهُ حَتَّى يُقَالَ فِيهِ : «المُرَكَّبُ : مَا دَلَ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ، وَالمُفْرَدُ : مَا لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ ... إلخ » . قَوْلُهُ : ( حَيْثُ يُوجَدُ أَيْ : فِي أَي تَرْكِيبٍ يُوجَدُ ذَلِكَ فِيهِ، فَهِيَ حَيْثُيَّةُ إِطْلَاقٍ. قَوْلُهُ : (إِمَّا مُرَكَّبٌ، وَإِمَّا مُفْرَدُ يَعْنِي : أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْرُجُ عَنْهُمَا ، وَهَذَا مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى أَنَّ القِسْمَةَ ثُنَائِيَّةٌ. وَجَعَلَهَا بَعْضُهُمْ ثُلاثِيَّةٌ : (١) - مُفْرَدٌ، وَهُوَ : «مَا لَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى شَيْءٍ أَصْلاً». (٢) - وَمُرَكَّبٌ، وَهُوَ : مَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى مَعْنَى لَيْسَ جُزْءَ مَعْنَاهُ . (٣) - وَمُؤَلَّفٌ، وَهُوَ : مَا يَدُلُّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ، وَالحَقُّ الأَوَّلُ. - وَدَخَلَ تَحْتَ المُرَكَّبِ» : المُرَكَّبُ الإِضَافِيُّ : غُلَامُ زَيْدٍ ، وَالتَّقْيِيدِيُّ كَـ : «حَيَوَانٌ نَاطِقٌ»، وَالإِسْنَادِيُّ كَـ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ». وَتَحْتَ المُفْرَدِ : الاِسْمُ ، وَالفِعْلُ، وَالحَرْفُ . قَوْلُهُ : (فَأَوَّلٌ ... إلخ) «الفَاءُ» لِلإِنْصَاحِ ؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَفْصَحَتْ عَنْ شَرْطٍ مَحْذُوفٍ؛ وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : إِذَا أَرَدْتَ بَيَانَ هَذَيْنِ القِسْمَيْنِ، فَأَوَّلُ ... إلخ». وَأَوَّلٌ : مُبْتَدَأ، وَسَاغَ الِابْتِدَاءُ بِهِ - مَعَ كَوْنِهِ نَكِرَةٌ - ؛ لِوُقُوعِهِ فِي مَعْرَضِ التَّفْصِيلِ؛ كَذَا قِيلَ. وَيُحِثَ فِيهِ : بِأَنَّ قَوْلَهُ : «فَأَوَّلٌ ... إلخ » لَيْسَ مُفَضِّلاً ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ بَيَانُ لِلمُفَصَّلِ إِلَيْهِ، فَهُوَ لَمْ يَقَعْ فِي مَعْرَضِ التَّفْصِيلِ، وَالَّذِي وَقَعَ فِي مَعْرَضِ التَّفْصِيلِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ قَوْلُهُ: «مُسْتَعْمَلُ الْأَلْفَاظِ».
Chapter on the investigations of words. (26) The used among words wherever found: either compound or simple. So the first: that whose part indicates part of its meaning, opposite of what follows. [Al-Bajuri: His saying: (Chapter on the investigations of words) means: in the issues in which words are investigated from the aspect of individuality and composition, and what suits them like universality and particularity. So 'mabahith' means the mentioned issues; because it is the plural of 'mabhat' meaning place of investigation, and it originally means: digging into the interior of something physically, then it was used customarily for the exposition of something and uncovering it. So their saying: 'mabhat of such' means: place of its exposition and uncovering, and that place is a metaphor for the issues in which it is investigated. And by our saying 'from the aspect ... etc.' is repelled what might be said: his speech necessitates that the investigation of indications is not from the investigations of words, and it is not so, so ponder. His saying: (The used among words ... etc.) means: the used among them, so the addition is in the sense of 'from'. And excluded from that is the neglected (muhmal), so it does not divide into that; because it has no meaning such that it is said about it: 'the compound: that whose part indicates part of its meaning, and the simple: that whose part does not indicate ... etc.' His saying: (wherever found) means: in whatever composition that is found in it, so it is a reflexive 'where'-phrase. His saying: (either compound or simple) means: that it does not go beyond them, and this is based on the division being binary. And some made it ternary: (1) simple, which is: 'that whose part does not indicate anything at all.' (2) compound, which is: 'that whose part indicates a meaning that is not part of its meaning.' (3) composed, which is: 'that whose part indicates part of its meaning.' And the correct is the first. And included under 'compound' is: the genitive compound: 'ghulam Zayd', and the attributive compound like: 'rational animal', and the predicative compound like: 'Zayd is standing'. And under 'simple' are: noun, verb, and particle. His saying: (So the first ... etc.) The 'fa' is for elucidation; because it elucidates a suppressed condition; and the estimation is: when you want the exposition of these two divisions, then the first ... etc. And 'awwal' is a subject, and it is permissible to begin with it even though it is indefinite because it occurs in the context of detailing; thus it is said. And it is objected to by that his saying: 'So the first ... etc.' is not a detailer, but rather it is an exposition of what is detailed to, so it did not occur in the context of detailing, and what did occur in the context of detailing is only his saying: 'The used among words'.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the investigation of words. The matn (core text) states that used words are either compound or simple. Compound is defined as that whose part indicates part of its meaning (e.g., 'ghulam Zayd' where 'ghulam' = slave, 'Zayd' = Zayd, each indicates a part of the overall meaning). Simple is the opposite. Bajuri then explains the chapter heading: 'mabahith' (investigations) refers to the issues concerning words from the perspectives of simplicity and composition, and related matters like universality and particularity. He clarifies that 'mabahith' is the plural of 'mabhat' (place of investigation), originally meaning a physical search, then used customarily for explanation and unveiling. He notes that the phrase 'from the aspect' excludes the investigation of indications (dalalat) from the scope of this chapter, though it might seem otherwise. He then specifies that only 'used' (musta'mal) words are intended, excluding 'neglected' (muhmal) words which have no meaning. The phrase 'wherever found' makes the division unconditional, applying to any composition. Bajuri then presents a difference of opinion: some scholars divide words into three categories: simple (mufrad: part indicates nothing), compound (murakkab: part indicates something not part of the meaning, like proper names 'Abdullah'), and composed (mu'allaf: part indicates part of meaning, like 'Zayd standing'). Bajuri asserts that the correct view is the binary division, equating 'compound' with 'composed'. Under compound, he lists three types: genitive (e.g., 'ghulam Zayd'), attributive (e.g., 'rational animal'), and predicative (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). Under simple, he lists noun, verb, and particle. Finally, he discusses the grammatical analysis of the matn's phrase 'So the first', explaining the 'fa' and the indefinite subject.

Key Terms
مفرد
مركب
مؤلف
مستعمل
مهمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the binary division preferred by Bajuri, which of the following is an example of a compound (murakkab) word?
A) Zayd (زيد)
B) 'Abdullah (عبد الله) as a proper name
C) The particle 'lam' (لام)
D) The verb 'daraba' (ضرب)

Key Takeaway
The binary division of words into mufrad and murakkab is based on whether a part indicates part of the meaning; only used words are considered, and the division is a prerequisite for all further logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Division and Classification
beginner
5 min
Classifying Universals by Existence: Three and Six Kinds
Author enumerates the ancient three-fold division of universals and the later six-fold refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal into one of the six categories based on the existence of its individuals, using the taxonomy of the ancients and later scholars.

Core Idea
Universals are classified by existence: none, one, many — subdivided by possibility and finitude.

The ancient logicians gave a simple threefold division of universals based on whether any individuals exist, one exists, or many exist. Later scholars refined this into six categories, distinguishing between impossible and possible non-existence, unique and potentially non-unique singulars, and finite and infinite multitudes. This taxonomy is essential for assessing claims in kalam and fiqh about necessary, impossible, and possible beings.

Guided Path
1
Recall the threefold division
The ancients divided universals into three: (1) no existing individual, (2) exactly one existing individual, (3) many existing individuals. Memorize these three as the basic levels.
Example: 'unicorn' (nonexistent), 'sun' (one in pre-modern view), 'human' (many).
2
Understand the first subdivision
For universals with no existing individual, later scholars split into: (a) impossible for any individual to exist (e.g., combination of opposites), and (b) possible but simply nonexistent (e.g., a sea of mercury). Distinguish by whether existence is logically impossible or merely absent.
In kalam, 'sharik al-Bari' (partner of the Creator) is impossible; 'city of gold' is possible but nonexistent.
3
Understand the second subdivision
For universals with exactly one individual, split into: (a) impossible for another to exist (e.g., God/Allah), and (b) possible for another but not actual (e.g., the sun as thought unique).
For Muslims, 'ilah' (god) has only one individual for the true God; another is impossible.
4
Understand the third subdivision
For universals with many individuals, split into: (a) finite number of individuals, and (b) infinite number of individuals. The passage gives 'attribute' (sifa) of Allah as infinite — His attributes (knowledge, power) are unlimited.
Warning: Do not confuse with created attributes or blessings, which are finite.
5
Apply the taxonomy
Given any universal term, determine which of the six categories it falls into. Ask: Are there any existing individuals? If none, is it impossible? If one, can there be another? If many, are they finite or infinite?
This skill is used in usul al-fiqh when defining terms like 'riba' or 'zina' to ensure correct rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ أَقْسَامَ الْكُلِّيِّ ثَلَاثَةٌ: الْأَوَّلُ: مَا لَمْ يُوجَدْ مِنْهُ شَيْءٌ. وَالثَّانِي: مَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ فَرْدٌ وَاحِدٌ فَقَط. وَالثَّالِثُ: مَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ; كَذَا قَالَ الْأَقْدَمُونَ. وَجَعَلَهَا الْمُتَأَخِّرُونَ سِتَّةً; حَيْثُ قَسَمُوا الْأَوَّلَ إِلَى: مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وُجُودُ شَيْءٍ مِنْهُ; كَالْجَمْعِ بَيْنَ الضِّدَّيْنِ، وَمَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ; كَبَحْرٍ مِنْ زِئْبَقٍ. وَقَسَمُوا الثَّانِي إِلَى: مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وُجُودُ غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ الْفَرْدِ الَّذِي وُجِدَ مِنْهُ; كَالْإِلَهِ، وَمَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ; كَالشَّمْسِ. وَقَسَّمُوا الثَّالِثَ إِلَى: مَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ غَيْرُ مُتَنَاهِيَةٍ; كَالصِّفَةِ، فَإِنَّ أَفْرَادَهَا الَّتِي وُجِدَتْ لَا تَتَنَاهَى; لِأَنَّ مِنْهَا الصِّفَاتِ الْوُجُودِيَّةَ الْقَائِمَةَ بِذَاتِهِ تَعَالَى، وَقَدْ دَلَّ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَى أَنَّهَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا، وَاسْتِحَالَةُ وُجُودِ مَا لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهُ إِنَّمَا ثَبَتَتْ فِي حَقِّ الْحَوَادِثِ، وَلَا يَصِحُّ التَّمْثِيلُ لِذَلِكَ بِـ: نِعْمَةِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى كَمَا صَنَعَهُ بَعْضُهُمْ; لِأَنَّ الْكَلَامَ فِيمَا وُجِدَ مِنْهُ أَفْرَادٌ لَا نِهَايَةَ لَهَا، وَنِعْمَةُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لَيْسَتْ كَذَلِكَ.
And know that the divisions of the universal are three: the first: that from which nothing exists. The second: that from which only one individual exists. The third: that from which individuals exist. Thus said the ancients. And the later scholars made them six: for they divided the first into: that whose existence of anything from it is impossible, like the combination of two opposites, and that which is not impossible, like a sea of mercury. And they divided the second into: that whose existence of another individual besides that which exists from it is impossible, like the God, and that which is not impossible, like the sun. And they divided the third into: that from which infinite individuals exist, like the attribute, for its individuals that exist are infinite, because among them are the existential attributes subsisting in His essence (Exalted), and the proof has indicated that they have no limit, and the impossibility of what has no limit is established only with respect to contingent things. And it is not correct to exemplify that with the blessing of Allah Exalted, as some have done, because the discussion concerns that from which infinite individuals exist, and the blessing of Allah Exalted is not like that.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage classifies universals according to the existence of their instantiations. The ancient logicians (aqdamun) gave a threefold division: (1) universal with no existing individual (e.g., 'unicorn' if nonexistent), (2) universal with only one existing individual (e.g., 'sun' as thought to be unique, or 'God' in theology), (3) universal with many existing individuals (e.g., 'human'). The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) refined each into two, making six. For the first group (no individuals), they distinguished between what is impossible to have any individual (e.g., 'combination of opposites' in reality) and what is possible but simply nonexistent (e.g., 'sea of mercury'). For the second group (one individual), they distinguished between what cannot have another like it (e.g., 'God' — no second god is possible) and what could have another but hasn't (e.g., 'sun' — though later astronomy disproved uniqueness, the logical point remains). For the third group (many individuals), they distinguished between finite and infinite. The example of infinite is 'attribute' (sifa), like God's attributes (knowledge, power, etc.), which are infinite according to Islamic theology. A note corrects a false example: some tried to use 'God's blessings' (ni'mah) as infinite, but blessings are finite because they are created and have a limit. The passage thus provides a comprehensive taxonomy of universals by their existential status, crucial for evaluating statements about necessity, possibility, and impossibility.

Key Terms
كُلِّي
أَقْدَمُون
مُتَأَخِّرُون
يَسْتَحِيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a universal from which no individual exists and its existence is impossible?
A) The sun
B) A sea of mercury
C) Combining two opposites in one subject
D) The attribute of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The later scholars' sixfold division of universals provides a precise tool for analyzing existential claims in Islamic sciences, distinguishing the impossible from the possible and the finite from the infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the universal 'nabi' (prophet) — many individuals exist. Are they finite or infinite? Why? (Hint: The Qur'an mentions a limited number, but the exact count is unknown; however, logically they are finite as created beings.)
?
Classify the universal 'shirk' (associating partners with Allah) — can multiple individuals exist? Is it impossible or merely nonexistent in some contexts? (Answer: It is possible for multiple acts of shirk to exist, so it falls under the third division, finite many.)

The Five Relations of Words to Meanings
Author enumerates the five relations of words to meanings and expands to eight by adding three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five relations of words to meanings and distinguish which apply only to universals and which apply also to particulars.

Core Idea
Relations of words to meanings: some are universal-specific, others apply to particulars too.

In this passage, the author introduces the five fundamental relations between words and their meanings. Two of these—tawāṭu' and tashākuk—are exclusive to universal terms, while the remaining three—contrast, sharing, and synonymy—occur in particulars as well. The author also corrects the mistaken view that particulars always fall under contrast. Understanding these relations is essential for precise interpretation of texts in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universal-specific relations
tawāṭu' and tashākuk are said to be specific to universal terms. Understand why: universals apply to many individuals equally or with gradation, whereas particulars are single individuals.
2
Identify the three relations that also apply to particulars
Contrast (tabāyun), sharing (ishtirāk), and synonymy (tarāduf) can occur with particular terms as the author shows with examples.
3
Analyze the examples given
For each relation, look at the example: contrast: Zayd and Wāshiq (two distinct individuals); sharing: the name 'Zayd' for two different persons; synonymy: Zayd and Abū 'Abdillāh (same person, different names).
4
Recognize the correction of a misconception
The author points out that the claim that particulars are always from the category of contrast is refuted by the fact that ishtirāk and tarāduf also occur in particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني. ونسبة الألفاظ للمعاني خمسة أقسام بلا نقصان. أن بعض هذه النسب يختص بالكلي، وهو التواطؤ والتشاكك؛ كما هو ظاهر. وأما الباقي فهو غير مختص به، بل يكون في الجزئي أيضاً. ومثال التباين فيه: زيد وواشق. ومثال الاشتراك فيه: زيد اسماً لابن عمرو، وزيد اسماً لابن بكر. ومثال الترادف فيه: زيد وأبو عبد الله. وبهذا التحقيق يعلم رد ما قيل من أن الجزئي من قبيل المتباين، فافهم. قوله: (خمسة أقسام) بقي عليه ثلاثة، وهي: النساوي، والعموم والخصوص من وجه، والعموم والخصوص بإطلاق. فضابط الأول: أن يتحدا ما صدقاً ويختلفا مفهوماً؛ كما في: الكاتب والضاحك. وضابط الثاني: أن يجتمعا في مادة، وينفرد كل منهما في مادة أخرى؛ كما في: الإنسان والأبيض. وضابط الثالث: أن يجتمعا في مادة، وينفرد أحدهما في مادة أخرى؛ كما في: الإنسان والحيوان. قال بعض المحققين: ويمكن إدراج الأول في الترادف؛ بأن يراد به ما يشمل ما لو كان بينهما الاتحاد ما صدقا فقط. وإدراج الثاني والثالث في التخالف؛ بأن يراد به ما يشمل التباين الجزئي. انتهى بتصرف. وعليه فكلام المصنف مستوف لجملة النسب الثمانية.
A chapter on explaining the relation of words to meanings. The relation of words to meanings is five divisions without deficiency. Some of these relations are specific to the universal, namely tawāṭu' and tashākuk, as is apparent. As for the rest, they are not specific to it, but occur in the particular as well. An example of contrast in it: Zayd and Wāshiq. An example of sharing in it: Zayd as a name for Ibn 'Amr, and Zayd as a name for Ibn Bakr. An example of synonymy in it: Zayd and Abū 'Abdillāh. By this verification, the refutation of what was said that the particular is of the type of contrast is known, so understand. His statement: (five divisions) there remain three additional to it, which are: relative equality, generality and specificity from one aspect, and absolute generality and specificity. The definition of the first: that they agree in extension and differ in intension, as in 'the writer' and 'the one who laughs'. The definition of the second: that they meet in one matter, and each is exclusive in another matter, as in 'human' and 'white'. The definition of the third: that they meet in one matter, and one of them is exclusive in another matter, as in 'human' and 'animal'. Some investigators said: It is possible to include the first under synonymy, by meaning that which includes even if there is agreement in extension only; and to include the second and third under opposition, by meaning that which includes partial contrast. End, with modification. Upon this, the author's statement is comprehensive of all eight relations.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the relation of words to meanings. The author states that there are five divisions. He then explains that two of these relations—tawāṭu' and tashākuk—are specific to universal terms, while the remaining three—takhāluf, ishtirāk, and tarāduf—can also apply to particular terms. He provides examples using proper names to illustrate contrast, sharing, and synonymy in particulars. He then corrects a misconception that particulars always fall under contrast. The commentator adds that three more relations complete the total to eight: relative equality (al-nasāwī), generality and specificity from one aspect, and absolute generality and specificity. He gives logical definitions (ḍābiṭ) for each: the first requires agreement in extension but difference in intension (like 'writer' and 'laugher'); the second requires meeting in one matter while each is exclusive in another (like 'human' and 'white'); the third requires meeting in one matter while one is exclusive in another (like 'human' and 'animal'). Finally, some scholars include the first additional relation under synonymy and the other two under opposition, making the author's statement cover all eight relations.

Key Terms
التواطؤ
التشاكك
التباين
الاشتراك
الترادف

Key Terms from Source Passage

What misconception about particulars does the author correct in this passage?
A) Particulars always fall under tawāṭu'.
B) Particulars always fall under tabāyun.
C) Particulars never fall under ishtirāk.
D) Particulars are always from tashākuk.

Key Takeaway
The five relations of words to meanings are tawāṭu', tashākuk (universal-specific), tabāyun, ishtirāk, and tarāduf (apply also to particulars).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Division and Classification
intermediate
10 min
The Three Additional Relations of Words to Meanings
Author enumerates the five relations of words to meanings and expands to eight by adding three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three additional relations (al-nasāwī, al-‘umūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh, al-‘umūm wa al-khuṣūṣ muṭlaq) and provide examples for each.

Core Idea
Beyond the five, three more relations complete the picture: equality, mutual generality, and absolute generality.

The author initially stated that there are five relations, but the commentator notes that three more are missing, bringing the total to eight. These additional relations are: relative equality (al-nasāwī), generality and specificity from one aspect, and absolute generality and specificity. Each has a precise definition based on agreement or difference in extension (ṣadaq) and intension (mafhūm). Some scholars attempt to subsume these under the original five, as explained at the end of the passage.

Guided Path
1
Understand the gap: from five to eight
The author said five, but three are missing. The commentator lists them.
2
Learn the first additional relation: al-nasāwī
Definition: two words agree in extension but differ in intension. Example: 'al-kātib' and 'al-ḍāḥik' (the writer and the laugher) – they can apply to the same person but have different meanings.
3
Learn the second additional relation: al-‘umūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh
Definition: they meet in one matter, and each is exclusive in another matter. Example: 'insān' and 'abyaḍ' – a human can be white, but not all humans are white, and not all white things are human.
4
Learn the third additional relation: al-‘umūm wa al-khuṣūṣ muṭlaq
Definition: they meet in one matter, and one is exclusive in another matter. Example: 'insān' and 'ḥayawān' – all humans are animals, but not all animals are humans.
5
Examine the scholarly attempt to reduce these to the original five
Some scholars include al-nasāwī under tarāduf (synonymy) by expanding the definition of synonymy, and include the other two under takhāluf (opposition) by including partial contrast. This shows the flexibility of the classification.

Source Text

The three additional relations complete the set of eight: al-nasāwī (equal extension), ‘umūm min wajh (partial overlap), and ‘umūm muṭlaq (one includes the other).

Defining Tawatu' and Responding to the Perfection Objection
Bajuri defines tawatu' and answers an objection about differential perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tawatu' (univocal term) and determine whether an objection based on differential perfection refutes the univocality by analyzing whether the variation lies in the meaning or external attributes.

Core Idea
A univocal term has the same meaning in all individuals; differences in perfection are external, not internal.

In this lesson, you will examine a classic definition of al-tawatu' (univocal terms) from Bajuri's commentary. The passage not only defines the term but also addresses a subtle objection: if some individuals are more perfect in the attributes of the term, doesn't that make the term ambiguous (mutashakik)? You will learn how al-Qarafi's answer preserves the univocality by distinguishing the core meaning from external effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and genus
Look at the opening: 'His statement: (Tawatu') meaning agreement (tawafuq).' The genus is 'agreement' and the differentia is that the single meaning is equal in all individuals without difference or variation. Notice the example 'insan' (human) is given as a clear case.
2
Analyze the objection
The objector claims that if some individuals (like the Prophet ﷺ) have more effects and completeness in human attributes, then the term 'insan' should be mutashakik (graded) rather than univocal. The objection hinges on the fact that the term seems to apply in degrees.
The objection assumes that variation in effects implies variation in the meaning itself.
3
Examine al-Qarafi's answer
Al-Qarafi distinguishes between variation in the meaning (which would break univocality) and variation in external matters (which does not). The core meaning of 'insan' is rational animal; additional perfection in knowledge or piety is accidental to that meaning. Therefore, tawatu' is preserved.
4
Apply the distinction
Now test: for a given term, ask whether any difference among individuals is in the core meaning or in external properties. If the core meaning is identical, the term remains tawatu'.
Use Islamic examples like 'salah' (prayer): the meaning of the ritual is the same for all, though the reward varies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (تَوَاطُؤٌ) أَيْ : تَوَافُقٌ، وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ كَانَ المَعْنَى الوَاحِدُ مُسْتَوِياً فِي أَفْرَادِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اخْتِلَافِ وَتَفَاوتٍ فِيهَا ؛ كَمَا فِي الإِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّ مَعْنَاهُ لَا يَخْتَلِفُ فِي أَفْرَادِهِ. فَإِنْ قِيلَ: قَدْ يَكُونُ المُتَوَاطِئُ فِي بَعْضِ الأَفْرَادِ أَكْثَرَ آثَاراً وَأَكْمَلَ مِنْهُ فِي بَعْضٍ آخَرَ، وَهَذَا يَقْتَضِي أَنَّهُ مُتَشَاكِكٌ، وَذَلِكَ كَـ : الإِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّ بَعْضَ أَفْرَادِهِ كَـ نَبِيِّنَا عَلَيْهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ أَكْثَرُ وَأَكْمَلُ مِنْ غَيْرِهِ فِي الخَوَاصِّ الإِنْسَانِيَّةِ كَالإِدْرَاكِ . أُجِيبَ بِمَا قَالَهُ القَرَافِيُّ : مِن أَنَّ التَّفَاوتَ بِالأُمُورِ الخَارِجَةِ عَنِ المُسَمَّى غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرٍ، حَتَّى يَخْرُجَ مَا ذَكَرَ عَنِ التَّوَاطُؤِ.
His statement: (Tawatu') meaning agreement, and that is by the single meaning being equal in its individuals without difference or variation in them, as in 'human', for its meaning does not differ in its individuals. If it is said: sometimes the univocal term in some individuals has more effects and is more complete than in others, and this requires that it be ambiguous (mutashakik), such as 'human', for some of its individuals like our Prophet (peace be upon him) are more and more complete than others in human characteristics like perception. It was answered by what al-Qarafi said: that variation by matters external to the named is not considered, so that what was mentioned does not exit from tawatu'.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a definition of tawatu' (univocal) from Bajuri's commentary on the matn. He defines it as a single meaning that applies equally to all its individuals without difference or variation, giving the example of 'insan' (human). Then he presents an objection: if a term applies to individuals that have different degrees of perfection (like the Prophet being more perfect in human attributes), then it would seem to be ambiguous (tashakkuk) rather than univocal. Bajuri answers with al-Qarafi's response: the variation is in matters external to the meaning itself, such as effects and additional perfections, not in the core meaning, so it remains univocal. The key point is that for tawatu', the meaning itself is the same in all individuals; differences in accidental properties do not affect the type of expression.

Key Terms
التَّوَاطُؤ
التَّوَافُق
المُتَشَاكِك

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the perfection is in external matters, not in the core meaning.
C) Because all humans are equally perfect in human attributes.
D) Because univocal terms cannot have any variation whatsoever.

Key Takeaway
A univocal term has an identical core meaning across all individuals; differences in accidental properties or degrees of perfection do not affect its univocality.

Understanding Tashakkuk: The Ambiguous Term
Bajuri defines tashakkuk and resolves a dispute about its reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tashakkuk from tawatu' and ishtirak by analyzing whether variation in intensity is intrinsic to a single meaning.

Core Idea
Tashakkuk is not reducible to univocal or homonymous terms; it has its own logical reality.

In Islamic logic, terms are classified by how they relate to their meanings. Tashakkuk (ambiguous by analogy) is a subtle category where a single meaning applies to individuals with varying intensity. This lesson unpacks its definition, illustrates it with the example of light, and explores a classical dispute about its independent reality, ultimately affirming its distinct status.

Guided Path
1
Define tashakkuk
Identify the key elements: a single meaning that is not equal across individuals but differs in intensity. The passage gives the example of 'light', which is stronger in the sun than elsewhere.
2
Understand the etymology
The term 'tashakkuk' comes from 'shakk' (doubt). Why? Because the observer doubts whether the term is univocal (tawatu', equal) or homonymous (ishtirak, multiple meanings). This doubt arises from the variation in intensity.
3
Examine Ibn al-Tilimsani's objection
He denied the independent reality of tashakkuk. His reasoning: if the variation (the factor of intensity) is part of the meaning, then the term has multiple meanings (ishtirak); if not, it is univocal (tawatu'). Thus, no third category exists.
4
Analyze Qarafi's rebuttal
Al-Qarafi responded that the meaning is a single core covering all individuals, so it cannot be homonymous. The variation comes from matters of the same genus as the meaning (e.g., intensity is intrinsic to light), so it is not purely univocal either. Thus tashakkuk has its own reality.
Key point: the variation is not external to the meaning but an intrinsic aspect of its application.
5
Conclude the reality of tashakkuk
The passage ends with 'let it be considered', inviting reflection. Following Qarafi, the tradition accepts tashakkuk as a valid classification alongside tawatu' and ishtirak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (تَشَاكُكٌ) أَيْ : بِأَنْ يَكُونَ المَعْنَى الوَاحِدُ لَيْسَ مُسْتَوِياً فِي أَفْرَادِهِ، بَلْ مُخْتَلِفٌ وَمُتَفَاوِتُ فِيهَا ؛ كَمَا فِي النُّورِ ، فَإِنَّهُ فِي الشَّمْسِ أَقْوَى مِنْهُ فِي غَيْرِهَا . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ هَذِهِ النِّسْبَةُ بِذَلِكَ، لأَنَّ النَّاظِرَ فِي ذَلِكَ يَتَشَكَّكُ وَيَقَعُ فِي شَكٍّ، فَإِنَّهُ إِنْ نَظَرَ لِأَصْلِ المَعْنَى كَانَ مِنْ قَبِيلِ التَّوَاطُؤِ، وَإِلَّا كَانَ مِنْ قَبِيلِ الاشْتِرَاكِ، وَلِذَلِكَ أَنْكَرَ ابْنُ التِّلِمْسَانِيِّ حَقِيقَةً التَّشَاكُكِ حَيْثُ قَالَ : لَا حَقِيقَةَ لِلمُشَكَّكِ؛ لأَنَّ مَا بِهِ التَّفَاوتُ إِنْ دَخَلَ فِي التَسْمِيَةِ فَمُشْتَرَكُ، وَإِلَّا فَمُتَوَاطِئُ . وَمَنَعَهُ القَرَّافِيُّ بِمَا مُلَخَصُهُ : أَنَّ المَعْنَى هُنَا وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ القَدْرُ الشَّامِلُ لِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُشْتَرَكِ، وَالتَّفَاوتُ هُنَا بِأُمُورٍ مِنْ جِنْسِ المُسَمَّى، فَلَا يَصِحُ كَوْنُهُ مِنْ قَبِيلِ المُتَوَاطِئِ، فَتُتْبَتُ لَهُ حَقِيقَةٌ ، فَلْيُتَأمَّلْ .
His statement: (Tashakkuk) i.e., that the single meaning is not equal in its individuals, but rather differs and varies in them, as in 'light', for it is stronger in the sun than in other than it. This relationship is only called that because the observer of that becomes doubtful and falls into doubt; for if he looks at the origin of the meaning, it is of the type of tawatu', otherwise it is of the type of ishtirak. For that reason, Ibn al-Tilimsani denied the reality of tashakkuk, saying: 'There is no reality for the ambiguous term; because the factor of variation, if it enters into the naming, then it is a homonym (mushtarak), otherwise it is univocal.' Al-Qarafi refuted him with what is summarized: that the meaning here is one, and it is the common measure covering all individuals, so it cannot be of the type of homonym, and the variation here is by matters of the same genus as the named, so it cannot be of the type of univocal, so a reality is established for it. Let it be considered.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines tashakkuk (ambiguous by analogy) as a single meaning that applies to its individuals with varying intensity, like 'light' which is stronger in the sun than in a lamp. The term 'tashakkuk' is derived from doubt (shakk) because the observer is uncertain whether it belongs to tawatu' or ishtirak. Ibn al-Tilimsani argued against its independent reality: if the difference in intensity is part of the meaning, then it is ishtirak (multiple meanings); if not, it is tawatu'. Hence tashakkuk is reducible. Al-Qarafi responds that the meaning is a common core covering all individuals, but the variation comes from aspects intrinsic to the concept (like intensity of light), so it is neither fully univocal (where no variation) nor homonymous (where different meanings). Thus tashakkuk has a distinct logical status. The passage ends with 'let it be considered', inviting the reader to reflect.

Key Terms
التَّشَاكُك
المُشَكَّك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Ibn al-Tilimsani deny the reality of tashakkuk?
A) Because he thought all terms are either univocal or homonymous.
B) Because he believed variation always indicates multiple meanings.
C) Because he followed Aristotle's categories strictly.
D) Because he considered tashakkuk a subtype of tawatu'.

Key Takeaway
Tashakkuk is a valid logical category where a single meaning varies in intensity across individuals, distinct from univocal and homonymous terms.

Definitions of Takhaluf, Ishtirak, and Taraduf with Grammatical Note
Bajuri defines difference, homonymy, and synonymy, and clarifies the grammatical parsing of 'aksuhu al-taraduf'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between takhaluf, ishtirak, and taraduf, and correctly parse the phrase 'aksuhu al-taraduf' as a grammatical construction in Arabic.

Core Idea
One word, many meanings (ishtirak); many words, one meaning (taraduf); completely different meanings (takhaluf).

This lesson covers three fundamental word-meaning relationships in Arabic: takhaluf (difference), ishtirak (homonymy), and taraduf (synonymy). Bajuri defines each with examples, then clarifies a subtle grammatical point about the phrase 'aksuhu al-taraduf' to ensure correct parsing. Understanding these distinctions is essential for precise analysis of texts in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In the Arabic passage, locate the terms 'التَّخَالُف', 'الاشْتِرَاك', and 'التَّرَادُف'. Note how Bajuri introduces each one with a definition.
Each term is followed by an explanation beginning with 'أَيْ' (i.e.).
2
Differentiate by direction
Determine the direction of the relationship: For ishtirak, it is one word to many meanings. For taraduf, it is many words to one meaning. For takhaluf, it is complete opposition between meanings.
Use the examples provided: 'عَيْن' for ishtirak, 'إِنْسَان' and 'بَشَر' for taraduf, 'إِنْسَان' and 'فَرَس' for takhaluf.
3
Analyze the grammatical parsing
Bajuri warns against parsing 'عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ' as a nominal sentence (subject + predicate). Explain why that would break the parallelism with the earlier enumeration. Instead, 'عَكْسُهُ' should be treated as a conjoined element (with omitted conjunction) and 'التَّرَادُفُ' as a substitute or appositive.
The earlier pattern had each term as a substitute (بدل) for 'خَمْسَة' (five). The same structure must be maintained.
4
Apply to new examples
Given a pair of expressions, classify the relationship as takhaluf, ishtirak, or taraduf. For instance, 'قَلَم' (pen) and 'قَدِيم' (ancient) – these are ishtirak? No, they are different words with different meanings? Actually, take 'قَلَم' and 'قَطَط' (scissors)? Not ideal. Use standard examples from the lesson.
Practice by creating your own pairs and testing the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (تَخَالُفُ) أَيْ : تَبَايُنٌ كُلِّيّ؛ كَمَا فِي : مَعْنَى الإِنْسَانِ وَمَعْنَى الفَرَسِ، وَيُمْكِنُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى مَا يَشْمَلُ : التَّبَايُنَ الجُزْئِيَّ، فَيَدْخُلُ فِيهِ : العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَالعُمُومُ وَالخُصُوص بِإِطْلَاقِ، كَمَا مَرَّ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالاشْتِرَاكُ) أَي : اللَّفْظِيُّ ؛ بِأَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ؛ كَمَا فِي عَيْنٍ، فَإِنَّهَا تُطْلَقُ عَلَى البَاصِرَةِ، وَعَلَى الجَارِيَةِ، وَعَلَى الذَّهَبِ، وَعَلَى ذَاتِ الشَّيْءِ، وَعَلَى خِيَارِ الشَّيْءِ، وَعَلَى الشَّمْسِ، وَعَلَى حَرْفِ الهِجَاءِ المَخْصُوصِ ، وَعَلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ بِالوُقُوفِ عَلَى القَامُوسِ وَغَيْرِهِ. قَوْلُهُ : (عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ) أي: التَّتَابِعُ وَالتَّوَارُدُ فِي الاسْتِعْمَالِ عَلَى المَعْنَى الوَاحِدِ؛ بِأَنْ يَتَعَدَّدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَّحِدَ المَعْنَى ، كَمَا أَشَارَ لَهُ بِقَوْلِهِ : «عَكْسُهُ»؛ كَمَا فِي: «إِنْسَانٍ وَبَشَرٍ، فَإِنَّهُمَا مُتَتَابِعَانِ وَمُتَوَارِدَانِ عَلَى مَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ، وَهُوَ الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ». ثُمَّ إِنَّهُ قَدْ يَتَبَادَرُ إِلَى الوَهُم إِعْرَابُ قَوْلِهِ : عَكْسُهُ التَّرَادُفُ مُبْتَدَأَ وَخَبَراً، وَهُوَ لَا يُنَاسِبُ إِعْرَابَ قَوْلِهِ : تَوَاطُؤٌ ... إلخ » بَدَلاً مِنْ خَمْسَةٌ كَمَا فِي نَظَائِرِهِ، فَالأَحْسَنُ أَنْ يُجْعَلَ قَوْلَهُ: «عَكْسُهُ» مَعْطُوفاً عَلَى مَا قَبْلَهُ عَلَى حَذْفِ العَاطِفِ، وَقَوْلَهُ : التَّرَادُفُ بَدَلاً أَوْ عَطْفَ بَيَانِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَاللَّفْظُ ) أي : المَعْهُودُ، وَهُوَ : المُسْتَعْمَلُ .
His statement: (Takhaluf) i.e., complete difference; as in the meaning of 'human' and the meaning of 'horse'. It is possible to apply it to what includes partial difference, so it includes general and specific from one perspective, and general and specific in an absolute sense, as previously mentioned. His statement: (Al-Ishtirak) i.e., verbal homonymy; by the word being one and its meanings multiple; as in 'ayn, for it is applied to the eye, the flowing water, gold, the essence of a thing, the best of a thing, the sun, the specific letter of the alphabet, and others, as known by consulting al-Qamus and other works. His statement: (The opposite of it is al-Taraduf) i.e., succession and concurrent use upon one meaning; by the words being multiple and the meaning one, as he indicated by his saying 'the opposite of it'; as in 'insan' and 'bashar', for they are successive and concurrent upon one meaning, which is 'speaking animal'. Then it might occur to the mind that the parsing of his statement 'aksuhu al-taraduf' is subject and predicate, but that does not suit the parsing of his statement 'tawatu'... etc.' as a substitute for 'five' as in its parallels. So it is better to make his word 'aksuhu' conjoined to what precedes it with the conjunction omitted, and his word 'al-taraduf' as a substitute or explanatory apposition. His statement: (Al-Lafz) i.e., the customary one, and it is the used word.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage covers three more types of word-meaning relationships and a grammatical point. First, takhaluf (difference) is when the meanings are completely different, like 'human' and 'horse'. It can also include partial difference, such as general and specific relations. Second, ishtirak (homonymy) is when one word has multiple meanings, exemplified by 'ayn' which has many meanings. Third, taraduf (synonymy) is the opposite: multiple words with one meaning, like 'insan' and 'bashar' both meaning 'speaking animal'. Then Bajuri addresses a grammatical issue: the phrase 'aksuhu al-taraduf' might be parsed as a nominal sentence, but that would break the parallelism with the earlier enumeration (where each term is a substitute for 'five'). The proper parsing is to treat 'aksuhu' as a conjunct (with omitted conjunction) and 'al-taraduf' as a substitute or appositive. Finally, he briefly notes that 'al-lafz' (the word) refers to the commonly used word.

Key Terms
التَّخَالُف
الاشْتِرَاك
التَّرَادُف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The words 'kitab' and 'risalah' both meaning 'book'.
B) The word 'ayn' meaning both 'eye' and 'gold'.
C) The words 'insan' and 'bashar' meaning 'human'.
D) The word 'rajul' meaning 'man'.

Key Takeaway
Takhaluf, ishtirak, and taraduf describe the relationship between words and meanings, and correct grammatical parsing ensures clarity in scholarly discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the words 'al-qalam' (pen) and 'al-risalah' (letter/message), determine whether they exhibit ishtirak or taraduf, and explain your reasoning. (Note: 'al-qalam' has one meaning, 'al-risalah' has one meaning, but they are different words with different meanings – so neither? Actually, this is takhaluf? Better: 'ayn' and 'shams' – both have multiple meanings, but each word individually is ishtirak; the pair 'insan' and 'bashar' is taraduf. Provide a new pair: 'sajada' (to prostrate) and 'rak'a' (to bow) – are they synonyms? They are different acts, so takhaluf. Or 'qita' and 'harb' (war) – synonyms? Actually, I'll leave a task: 'Classify the relationship between the following pairs: (a) 'kataba' and 'sattara' (both meaning 'to write'); (b) 'qiyam' and 'julus' (standing and sitting); (c) 'shariba' (to drink) and 'akala' (to eat). Explain each.'
?
Parse the phrase 'عكس الترادف' as per Bajuri's grammatical note: Is it a nominal sentence or a conjunct? Write the proper i'rab (parsing) for each word, and justify your answer based on the passage.

Division and Classification

The Two Types of Proposition: Conditional and Categorical
Author states the two types of proposition with definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition as conditional or categorical based on whether it involves condition/dependence or simple predication.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either conditional (shartiyya) or categorical (hamliyya) in Islamic logic.

In this passage, the author divides propositions into two types: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). Before defining them, he clarifies a subtle point: the word 'thumma' (then) here does not imply temporal order, but merely textual sequence. This is a common pitfall. Then he provides the core distinction: conditional propositions involve a condition and dependence (ta'liq), while categorical propositions involve simple predication (haml). Understanding this division is essential for all later logical operations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the division
Locate where the author states that propositions are divided into two divisions: conditional and categorical. Note that 'thumma' indicates sequential mention only.
Not temporal order.
2
Understand 'thumma'
The commentator clarifies that 'thumma' here is for tartib dhikri (sequential mention), not temporal order, as confirmed by al-Mallawi.
3
Extract the definition of conditional
Conditional (shartiyya) is defined as a proposition in which judgment is made on the basis of condition and dependence (ta'liq).
4
Extract the definition of categorical
Categorical (hamliyya) is defined as a proposition in which judgment is made on the basis of predication (haml), i.e., attributing a predicate to a subject.
5
Apply the distinction
Practice identifying conditional and categorical propositions from Islamic sciences, such as kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (ثُمَّ القَضَايَا ... إلخ) «ثُمَّ» لِلتَّرْتِيبِ الذِّكْرِي فَقَط، كَمَا قَالَهُ الشَّيْخُ المَلَّوِيُّ. قَوْلُهُ : ( شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ) بَدَلَانِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : قِسْمَانِ). وَالأُوْلَى: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الشَّرْطِ وَالتَّعْلِيقِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي، وَالثَّانِيَةُ: «مَا حُكِمَ فِيهَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، وَلِذَلِكَ سُمِّيَتِ الأُوْلَى: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَالثَّانِيَةُ: «حَمْلِيَّةٌ».
Then propositions with them are two divisions: conditional and categorical. His saying: (Then propositions... etc.) 'Then' is for sequential mention only, as stated by al-Mallawi. His saying: (conditional categorical) are both substitutes for his saying: 'two divisions'. And the first: 'what is judged in it on the basis of condition and dependence' as will come, and the second: 'what is judged in it on the basis of predication', and for that the first is called 'conditional', and the second 'categorical'.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage provides the division of propositions into two types: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya), as stated in the matn. The author explains that the word 'thumma' (then) here indicates sequential mention in the text, not temporal order, as confirmed by al-Mallawi. The words 'conditional' and 'categorical' are substitutes (badal) for 'two divisions'. The definition of the first type (conditional) is: a proposition in which judgment is made on the basis of condition and dependence (ta'liq). The second type (categorical) is: a proposition in which judgment is made on the basis of predication (haml), i.e., attributing a predicate to a subject. The names are derived from these features: 'shartiyya' from 'shart' (condition) and 'hamliyya' from 'haml' (predication). Later sections will elaborate on each.

Key Terms
الشرطية
الحملية
التعليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the first type called 'shartiyya'?
A) Because it is divided into two parts.
B) Because it contains a condition and dependence.
C) Because it is always true.
D) Because it is used in proofs.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental division of propositions in Islamic logic is into conditional (based on condition/dependence) and categorical (based on predication).

Division and Classification
intermediate

The Four Types of Categorical Propositions and the Natural Proposition
Al-Bajuri classifies categorical propositions and discusses the natural proposition controversy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given categorical proposition as personal, unquantified, universal, particular, or natural, and justify its classification based on the criteria discussed by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions divide into four main types; the natural proposition sparks debate but fits best under the personal.

In this passage, al-Bajuri expands on the classification of categorical propositions (al-ḥamliyya) into four well-defined types and addresses the controversial natural proposition (al-ṭabī‘iyya). Your task is to internalize the distinguishing features of each type and understand why the natural proposition’s classification matters for logical precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four main types
Al-Bajuri enumerates: personal (shakhṣiyya), unquantified (muhmala), universal (kulliyya), and particular (juz’iyya). Note the subject and quantifier for each.
The personal has a specific individual subject; the unquantified has a universal subject but no quantifier; the universal and particular are explicitly quantified.
2
Understand the restriction on personal propositions
Al-Bajuri warns that propositions about Allah should not be called personal, because that would imply physical particularization. This shows that logical terminology must be careful with theological content.
3
Analyze the natural proposition classifications
The natural proposition (e.g., 'Animal is a genus') judges the nature itself. Learn the three opinions: it belongs to the personal (correct), to the unquantified, or is an independent type. Understand why the correct opinion considers it personal: the subject is mentally specified.
The marginal notes give further reasoning: al-Ansari compares scope, al-Shirshimi defines personal by external specifications.
4
Apply the classification to new Islamic examples
Take several Arabic propositions and determine their type. For instance, 'Every believer is righteous' is universal; 'Some scholars are generous' is particular; 'Zaid is standing' is personal; 'Man is an animal' (with 'al' for genus) is unquantified; 'Animal is a genus' is natural.
Ensure you can justify each classification using the criteria from al-Bajuri's commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها (٥٢) كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ، وَالأَوَّلُ: إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ، وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ. وَالَّذِي يَتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ فِي هَذَا المَقَامِ : أَنَّ الحَمْلِيَّةَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ : (١) - الأَوَّلُ : الشَّخْصِيَّةُ ، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا مُشَخَّصاً مُعَيَّناً ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ»، لَكِنْ يَمْتَنِعُ إِطْلَاقُ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ عَلَى نَحْوِ قَوْلِنَا : «اللَّهُ تَعَالَى قَادِرُه ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ إِيْهَامٍ تَشَخُّصِ المَوْضُوعِ تَشَخُّصاً حِسْمَانِيًّا تَعَالَى اللَّهُ عَنْهُ . (٢) - وَالثَّانِي : المُهْمَلَةُ، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيًّا، وَأُهْمِلَتْ مِنَ السُّوْرِ»؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : الإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانُ إِذَا جُعِلَتْ أَلْ لِلْجِنْسِ فِي ضِمْنِ الأَفْرَادِ بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الكُلْيَةِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ. (٣) - وَالثَّالِثُ : الكُلِّيَّةُ، وَهِيَ : المُسَوَّرَةُ بِالسُّوْرِ الكُلِّيّ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ . (٤) - وَالرَّابِعُ : الجُزْئِيَّةُ ، وَهِيَ : المُسَوَّرَةُ بِالسُّوْرِ الجُزْئِيّ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ . وَهَذَانِ القِسْمَانِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرِّحُ بِهِمَا المُصَنِّفُ، لَكِنَّهُمَا مَأخُوذَانِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : وَالسُّوْرُ كُلِّيّا وَجُزْئيا يُرَى، فَإِنَّهُ يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ : أَنَّ المُسَوَّرَةَ بِالسُّورِ الكُلِّيِّ تُسَمَّى : كُلِّيَّةَ ، وَالمُسَوَّرَةَ بِالسُّورِ الجُزْئِيِّ تُسَمَّى : جُزْئِيَّة». وَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضِ المُصَنِّفُ لِـ : الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ ، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا الطَّبِيعَةُ، بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الأَفْرَادِ»؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : «الحَيَوَانُ جِنْسٌ»، وَقَدْ جَرَى فِيهَا خِلَافٌ: - فَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ الحَقُّ - : أَنَّهَا دَاخِلَةٌ فِي الشَّخْصِيَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا مُشَخَّصُ ذِهْناً . - وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهَا دَاخِلَةٌ فِى المُهْمَلَةِ (١) . - وَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ - : إِنَّهَا قِسْمٌ مُسْتَقِل (۲)، لَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَلَا مُهْمَلَةٌ . الأنبابي من غير قوله : (الأَوَّلُ : الشَّخْصِيَّةُ وهي في حكم الكلّيَّة؛ لأنَّ الحكم في كل منهما على مصدوق اللفظ خروج شيء منه عن الحكم، بخلاف المهملة، فإنَّها في قوَّة الجزئيَّة؛ لأنَّ الحكم فيها على بعض الأفراد محقق، والزَّائد مشكوك فيه، فطرح وجعلت القضيَّة في قوَّة الجزئية، وكون المحكوم به قد يتيقن تحققه لجميع الأفراد، كما في : الإنسان كاتب بالقوَّة لا يقتضي تيقن الحكم به من المتكلم على الجميع. (1) الشرشيمي : قوله : (دَاخِلَةٌ فِي المُهْمَلَةِ لأنَّه لا يراد الحقيقة في ضمن بعض الأفراد ولا كلَّها ؛ لأنَّه لا يصدق على بعض الأفراد أنه جنس، وكذلك كل الأفراد. (۲) الشرشيمي: قوله : ( قِسْمٌ مُسْتَقِل لأنَّ المراد بـ «الشَّخصيَّة»: ما له مشخصات خارجيَّةٌ ; أي: علامات تميزه كالشكل . وَهَذَا كُلَّهُ مَبْنِيٌّ عَلَى مَا هُوَ الرَّاجِحُ مِنْ أَنَّهَا مُعْتَبَرَةٌ فِي العُلُوم (۱) ، لَا عَلَى مَا قِيلَ (٢) مِنْ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ مُعْتَبَرَةٍ فِيهَا ، فَتَامَّلُ .
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. (52) Universal and personal; and the first is either quantified or unquantified. What is obtained from the words of the author in this place: that the categorical [proposition] has four divisions: (1) The first: the personal, which is the one whose subject is specified and determinate, like your saying: 'Zaid is standing,' but it is prohibited to apply 'personal' to something like our saying: 'Allah, exalted, is powerful,' due to what it entails of suggesting that the subject is specified with a physical specification – exalted is Allah above that. (2) The second: the unquantified, which is the one whose subject is universal and is left unquantified, like your saying: 'Man is an animal' when 'al' is made for the genus within the individuals, disregarding universality and particularity. (3) The third: the universal, which is the one quantified with the universal quantifier, like your saying: 'Every man is an animal.' (4) The fourth: the particular, which is the one quantified with the particular quantifier, like your saying: 'Some animal is man.' These two divisions, even if the author did not explicitly mention them, are derived from his saying: 'The quantifier is seen as universal and particular,' for it is taken from it that the one quantified with the universal quantifier is called universal, and the one quantified with the particular quantifier is called particular. And the author did not address the natural [proposition], which is the one wherein the subject of judgment is the nature, disregarding individuals, like your saying: 'Animal is a genus.' And there is disagreement concerning it: it was said – and it is the truth – that it is included in the personal, because the subject of judgment in it is specified mentally. And it was said that it is included in the unquantified. And it was said – and it is the well-known – that it is an independent division, neither personal nor unquantified. Al-Ansari: from other than his saying: (The first: the personal, and it is in the ruling of the universal, because the judgment in each of them is about the denotation of the word, something exiting from it from the judgment, unlike the unquantified, for it has the force of the particular, because the judgment in it is realized for some individuals, and the remainder is doubtful, so it is cast and the proposition is made to have the force of the particular, and that the judgment may be certain of its realization for all individuals, as in 'Man is potentially a writer' does not necessitate certainty of the judgment by the speaker about all. Al-Shirshimi: his saying: (Included in the unquantified because the reality is not intended within some individuals nor all of them, because it is not true of some individuals that it is a genus, and likewise all individuals. Al-Shirshimi: his saying: (An independent division because by 'personal' is meant that which has external specifications, i.e., distinguishing marks like form.) And all of this is based on what is preponderant, that they are considered in the sciences, not on what is said that they are not considered therein, so reflect.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification. Al-Bajuri begins by noting that from the author's words we obtain four divisions of categorical propositions. The first is the personal proposition (shakhsiyya), which has a specific individual subject like 'Zaid is standing.' He cautions that one should not call propositions about Allah 'personal' because that would imply physical particularization. The second is the unquantified proposition (muhmala), which has a universal subject but no quantifier, like 'Man is an animal' where the definite article indicates the genus without specifying universality or particularity. The third is the universal proposition (kulliyya), quantified with 'every' or similar. The fourth is the particular proposition (juz'iyya), quantified with 'some.' Al-Bajuri notes that the last two are derived from the author's statement about the quantifier. He then addresses the natural proposition (tabi'iyya), which makes a judgment on the nature itself, like 'Animal is a genus.' Three opinions are reported: the correct one includes it in the personal because the subject is mentally specified; another includes it in the unquantified; the well-known view considers it independent. The marginal notes clarify: al-Ansari explains that the personal is like the universal in judgment scope, while the unquantified is like the particular due to doubt. Al-Shirshimi provides reasons for the other views. The passage concludes that this classification is based on the preponderant view that these proposition types are valid and used in the sciences (i.e., in syllogisms), and the student is urged to reflect.

Key Terms
الحَمْلِيَّة
الشَّخْصِيَّة
المُهْمَلَة
الكُلِّيَّة
الجُزْئِيَّة
الطَّبِيعِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The personal proposition (al-shakhsiyya)
B) The unquantified proposition (al-muhmala)
C) An independent division

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are classified into four types based on subject specificity and quantifier presence; the natural proposition's classification is debated but the correct view includes it in the personal.

Division and Classification
beginner

The Issues of a Science: Definition and Role
Author defines the issues of logic and classifies them into five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the issues (masa'il) of a science and explain their relationship to the subject of the science, distinguishing between summary knowledge and complete knowledge.

Core Idea
The issues of a science are its very content; only a summary grasp of them belongs to its principles.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of the 'issues' (masa'il) of logic, which are the propositions that form the content of the science. The commentary clarifies that these issues are the science itself, and that knowing them summarily is part of the principles. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of any science.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the commentator defines 'masa'il' as propositions whose predicate-subject relation is sought by demonstration (burhan).
2
Identify genus and differentia
The genus is 'propositions' (qadaya); the differentia is the seeking of relation by burhan. This makes the definition a proper hadd.
3
Distinguish summary from complete knowledge
Summary knowledge (tasawwur ajmali) is part of the principles; complete knowledge of all issues is the science itself, not its principles.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider an issue in grammar: 'the word after composition is either declinable or indeclinable.' This is a proposition whose predicate-subject relation is sought by demonstration in the science of grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسائله، فهي القضايا المتضمنة للأحكام التصورية والتصديقية التي يتوصل بها إلى المجهول من تصور أو تصديق. والمراد بـ تصور المسائل : معرفتها على وجه إجمالي، وفائدة تقديمها : زيادة التميز، وأما الإحاطة بجميعها فهي نفس مقاصد العلم، فلا يصح جعلها من مبادئه. قوله: (مسائله) مسائل العلم هي: القضايا التي يطلب في ذلك العلم نسبة محمولاتها إلى موضوعاتها بالبرهان. وهي نفس العلم، فلا يصح عدها من المبادئ، وإنما الذي من المبادئ: ضبطها ومعرفتها على وجه إجمالي، وذلك بأن يقال : كل مسألة في العلم لا يخلو موضوعها من خمسة أقسام : ١ - إما أن يكون عين موضوع العلم مجرداً؛ كقولنا في النحو : الكلمة بعد التركيب إما معربة، أو مبنيّة. ٢ - أو مع عرض ذاتي؛ نحو: الكلمة المعربة : إما ظاهرة الإعراب، أو مقدرته. ٣ - أو نوع موضوعها؛ نحو: الاسم : يسند، ويسند إليه، و: الأمر يفيد الوجوب. ٤ - أو نوعاً مع عرض ذاتي؛ نحو: الاسم المعرب : إما منصرف، أو غير منصرف. ٥ - أو يكون وصفاً ذاتياً للموضوع؛ كقولنا : الإعراب والبناء : إما ظاهران أو مقدَّران.
As for its issues, they are the propositions that contain conceptual and assent-based judgments by which one reaches the unknown in conception or assent. The intended meaning of 'conception of the issues' is their knowledge in a summary manner. The benefit of presenting them is increased discrimination. As for encompassing all of them, that is itself the objectives of the science, so it is not valid to make them part of its principles. His saying: (Its issues) — the issues of a science are the propositions of which the relation of their predicates to their subjects is sought in that science by demonstration. They are the science itself, so it is not valid to count them among the principles. Rather, what is among the principles is their control and knowledge in a summary manner, such that it is said: Every issue in the science, its subject is not free from five divisions: 1. Either it is the very subject of the science, unqualified, like our saying in grammar: 'The word after composition is either declinable or indeclinable.' 2. Or with an essential accident, like: 'The declinable word is either manifest in declension or estimated.' 3. Or a type of its subject, like: 'The noun functions as subject and predicate,' and 'The imperative indicates obligation.' 4. Or a type with an essential accident, like: 'The declinable noun is either fully declined or not fully declined.' 5. Or it is an essential description of the subject, like our saying: 'Declension and indeclinability are either manifest or estimated.'
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage defines the masa'il (issues) of the science of logic. The matn states that issues are propositions containing conceptual or assent-based judgments that lead to unknown conceptions or assents. Knowing them summarily (tasawwur al-masa'il) is beneficial for discrimination, but complete knowledge of them is the science itself, not its principles. The commentary elaborates: issues are propositions whose predicate-subject relations are sought by demonstration (burhan). They are the science itself. However, a summary knowledge of them is part of the principles. Then the commentary provides a fivefold classification of how the subject of an issue relates to the subject of the science, using examples from grammar (a branch of Islamic sciences). The five types are: 1) the very subject of the science, unqualified; 2) the subject with an essential accident; 3) a type of the subject; 4) a type with an essential accident; 5) an essential description of the subject. All examples are from nahw (Arabic grammar), which is an Islamic science.

Key Terms
مسائل
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The issues are the science itself.
C) The issues are only conceptual judgments.
D) The issues are unrelated to the subject of the science.

Key Takeaway
The issues of a science are its core propositions, and only a summary of them belongs to the principles.

Division and Classification
