Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory explanation from the commentary (sharh) on al-Sullam al-Murawni. The author first states that the relation of words to meanings has five divisions, derived from a simple combinatorial analysis: a word may be one or multiple, and a meaning may be one or multiple. This yields four base combinations, but the case of one word for one meaning splits into two subtypes: mutawat'i (univocal) and mushakkik (equivocal in a graded sense), making five total.

The five are: (1) tawatu' (univocal), where one word applies equally to many individuals; (2) tashkik (equivocal by gradation), where one word applies with differing intensity or priority; (3) takhaluf (mutabayin/opposite), where different words have different meanings; (4) ishtirak (homonymy), where one word has multiple unrelated meanings; (5) taraduf (synonymy), where multiple words share a single meaning.

The author then defines mutawat'i in detail: a universal whose individuals (external or mental) are equal in its meaning, with no precedence. Examples: 'insan' for humans (external), 'shams' for the sun (mental). A possible objection—that some humans are more intelligent or more perfect—is answered by clarifying that the equality is in the core concept (mafhum), not in accidental attributes. The word is called mutawat'i because tawatu' means agreement; its individuals agree without difference.

Next, the author defines mushakkik: a universal whose individuals differ in the meaning itself, either by quantity (more/less), intensity (stronger/weaker), or priority (older/more deserving). Examples: 'bayad' (whiteness) is more in snow than ivory; 'wujud' (existence) is stronger in the Necessary Existent than in possibles. An objection arises: if individuals differ, then the concept is not applied equally, so why is it still one meaning? The answer is that the difference is in the existence, not in the concept the word is placed for; the concept is one, but its application varies. The commentator then critiques a phrasing in the matn: 'aw aqwa' (or stronger) should be 'aw awla' (or more deserving) because 'stronger' is redundant with 'more intense'. Finally, a quote from al-Hafid's Sharh al-Tahdhīb notes a discussion: if intensity is based on effects, then even mutawat'i terms like 'insan' would show intensity, as some humans (e.g., the Prophet) have more perfect effects. This is left as an open point.

Key Terms
المشكك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a correct example of mushakkik where the difference is in 'being older' (aqdam)?
A) Whiteness in snow vs. ivory
B) Existence in Allah vs. creation
C) Light in lamp vs. sun
D) Prophethood in prophets vs. normal humans

Key Takeaway
Mushakkik terms have one concept that applies with degrees; precise wording matters, and the correction from 'aqwa' to 'awla' shows the tradition's care for logical accuracy.

Division and Classification
beginner

The Four Relations Between Concepts: Introduction
Author defines tabayun and enumerates four relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the four logical relations between any two concepts using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Every two concepts are related by one of four relations: total exclusion, equivalence, absolute generality-specificity, or generality-specificity from one aspect.

In this lesson, we dive into the logical taxonomy of concept relations as presented in the Bajuri-Bannani commentary. Understanding these relations is crucial for constructing precise definitions and evaluating propositions in Islamic logic. The author begins by defining 'mutual exclusivity' (at-tabāyun) as the opposite of synonymy, then divides it into four exhaustive categories.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of tabayun
Find the author's definition: 'mutual exclusivity in sense opposite of synonymy'. This is the genus for the four relations.
2
Identify the four sub-types
List them: (1) total in concept and reference, (2) only in concept with equal reference (equivalence), (3) only in concept with unequal reference one-way (absolute generality-specificity), (4) only in concept with two-way separation (generality-specificity from one aspect).
3
Match each sub-type with its example
For total: human and stone; equivalence: laughing and speaking; absolute: animal and human; from one aspect: human and black.
4
Apply the definitions to new examples
Given two terms from Islamic theology or fiqh, determine which relation holds. For instance, 'wine' and 'intoxicant' – are they equivalent? Not all intoxicants are wine, so it's absolute generality-specificity.
Intoxicant is more general than wine.

واعلم أنَّ التَّباينَ بالمَعْنَى المَذْكُورِ - أَيْ : مُقَابِلِ التَّرَادُفِ - يَقَعُ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ ; لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ كُلّيّا فِي المَفْهُومِ وَالمَصْدُوقِ بِأَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى شَيْءٍ مِمَّا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ : الإِنْسَانِ، وَ: «الحَجَرِ»، وَمِنْهُ تَبَايِنُ المُشْتَقِّ وَالمُشْتَقِّ مِنْهُ كَـ: «العِلْمِ»، و: «العَالِمِ»، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ فِي المَفْهُومِ فَقَط ، وَذَلِكَ إِمَّا مَعَ تَسَاوِيهِمَا فِي المَصْدُوقِ گ: «الصَّاحِكِ»، وَ: «النَّاطِقِ، وَإِمَّا مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّسَاوِي بِأَنْ يَفْتَرِفًا فِي بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ المَصْدُوقِ، وَالاِفْتِرَاقُ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ گ: «الحَيَوَانِ»، وَ: «الإِنْسَانِ»، فَالأَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً أَعَمُّ ، وَالأَقَلُّ أَخَصُّ ، وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ الصِّفَةُ وَالمَوْصُوفُ : السَّيْفِ ، وَ : «الصَّارِمِ، وَكَـ: «النَّاطِقِ، وَ: «الفَصِيحِ، وَإِمَّا مِنَ كَـ الجِهَتَيْنِ مَعاً وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ كَـ: الإِنْسَانِ، وَ: «الأَسْوَدِ»، وَكَـ: «الصَّارِمِ»، وَ: «المُهَنَّدِ ، وَهَذِهِ هِيَ النِّسَبُ الأَرْبَعُ الَّتِي لَا بُدَّ بَيْنَ كُلِّ مَعْقُولَيْنِ مِنْ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهَا . (قصارة) قوله : (گـ: «العِلْمِ، وَ العَالِمِ) فإنَّ معنى «العلم» كما قال القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني: «معرفة المعلوم، ومعنى العالم : الذَّات المتّصفة بالعِلم، ولا فرق بين أن يكون معنى المشتق منه قائماً بالمشتق كالمثال، أو موضوعاً لمحل عمل كالحديد للحداد، فإنَّ الحديد الذي هو مشتق منه موضوع للجسم الشَّديد الأسود، وهو محل عمل للحداد الذي هو المشتق؛ أي: يعمل منه الأشياء كالسيوف والدروع، وغير ذلك، فقولُ قدُّورة : موضوعاً لعمل على حذف مضاف؛ أي: محل عمل، أو يكون موجداً له؛ أي: يكون المشتقُ سبباً مجازيًا في وجود المشتق منه كالمال والمتموّل، فإنَّ الموجد للمال بسبب التجارة وغيرها هو المتموّل ؛ أي: صاحب المال . قوله : (وَهَذِهِ هِيَ النِّسَبُ الأَرْبَعُ ... إلخ) اعلم أنَّ هذه النِّسب إن اعتبرت بين المفردين كانت معتبرةً بينهما في الحمل؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ناطق، وبالعكس، وفي الوجود؛ أي: كلَّما الإنسان وجد الناطق، وإن اعتبرت بين القضيَّتين كانت معتبرةً في الوجود فقط دون الصدق؛ إذ لا يتصوَّر حمل القضايا على شيء. (وجد) وتعرض الشارح لذكر هذه النسب؛ لأنَّ المحلَّ لائق بها فهي فائدة حسنة، وقد أشار لها صاحب القادرية» بقوله : (1) في :(أ) مِنْهُمَا، بدلاً من مِنْهَا».
وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا : أَنَّ المَعْقُولَيْنِ ; أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ الحَاصِلَيْنِ فِي العَقْلِ [ج/ ١٥] إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ ، وَإِمَّا [ب/ ۲۲] أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَجْتَمِعَا تَارَةٌ وَيَفْتَرِقَا تَارَةً، وَالافْتِرَاقُ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، وَإِمَّا مِنْ جِهَتَيْنِ بِأَنْ يُفَارِقَ كُلِّ صَاحِبَهُ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَمْيْلَتَهَا . (قصارة) وَكُلُّ مَعْقُولَيْنِ فَاعْلَمْ قَدْ وَجَبْ بَيَنْهَمَّا بَعْضُ مِنْ أَربَعِ نِسَبْ وَهِيَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ المُطلق أَوِ الَّذِي مِنْ جِهَةٍ يُحَفَّق ثُمَّ المُسَاوَاةُ مَعَ التَّبايُن وَالحَصْرُ فِي ذَاكَ بِسَبْرِ كَائِن قوله : (وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا) اعترض السَّيِّد هذا الحصر : بنقائض الأمور الشاملة للموجودات الذهنيّة والخارجيَّة؛ نحو: «شيء» و «ممكن»، فإنَّهما متساويان ونقيضاهما وهما: «لا شيء» و: «لا ممكن مفهومان ليس بينهما واحدة مِنَ النّسب الأربع، أمَّا نفي ما عدا المساواة فظاهر، وأمَّا نفي المساواة فلأنَّها معتبرة في الوجود، ولا وجود لهما . قوله : (وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصَّ) يعني : التَّباين الكلِّيَّ ; لأنَّ قوله : «أن لا يجتمعا البتة» شامل للتناقض والتّضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة ، وأمَّا التَّباين الجزئي فمندرج تحت العموم من وجه، والعموم بإطلاق ; إذ تقول : بعض الأبيض ليس بإنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان»، فهو غيرُ مراد هنا . تنبية اعلم أنَّ المتساويين كـ «الإنسان، والناطق»، نقيضاهما متساويان أبداً كـ: «لا إنسان، ولا ناطق ; بمعنى : أنَّ كلَّ ما صدق عليه لا إنسان صدق عليه لا ناطق»، وبالعكس. وأما المتباينان فنقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا التَّباين أو العموم والخصوص من وجه: ۱ - فالأوَّل كـ : «الإنسان، ولا «ناطق فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان، وناطق» وهما متباينان أيضاً; إذ كلما ثبت أحدهما ارتفع الآخر. ۲ - والثاني كـ : «الإنسان، ولا حيوان فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، وحيوان» وبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه; لاجتماعهما في الفرس وانفراد حيوان في الإنسان، وانفراد لا إنسان في الحجر .
Chapter on clarifying the relationship of words to meanings. Know that at-tabāyun (mutual exclusivity) in the mentioned sense — i.e., opposite of at-tarāduf (synonymy) — falls into four divisions: because it may be total in concept and reference, such that neither applies to anything that the other applies to, like 'human' and 'stone'; and from it is the mutual exclusivity of the derived term and its base, like 'knowledge' and 'knower'. And it may be only in concept, and that either with equality in reference, like 'laughing' and 'speaking', or without equality, such that they differ in some individuals of reference, and the difference is either from one side, which is absolute generality and specificity, like 'animal' and 'human' — the one with more individuals is more general, the one with fewer is more specific — and from that is the attribute and the described, like 'sword' and 'sharp', and like 'speaking' and 'eloquent'. Or it may be from two sides together, which is generality and specificity from one aspect, like 'human' and 'black', and like 'sharp' and 'Indian sword'. These are the four relations which must exist between every two concepts: one of them. (Commentary: His statement: 'like knowledge and knower' — for the meaning of 'knowledge', as Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said, is 'knowledge of the known', and the meaning of 'knower' is the essence characterized by knowledge. There is no difference between the meaning of the derivative being inherent in the derivative as in the example, or being the subject of an action like iron for the blacksmith, for iron which is the base is the name for the hard black body, and it is the place of action for the blacksmith who is the derivative; i.e., he works from it things like swords and shields and so forth. So the saying of Qaddura: 'subject for action' with a deleted genitive construction, i.e., place of action, or it is an efficient cause; i.e., the derivative is a metaphorical cause of the existence of the base, like property and the possessor of property, for the one who produces property through trade and other means is the possessor of property; i.e., the owner of property. His statement: 'These are the four relations... etc.' Know that these relations, if considered between two singular terms, are considered between them in predication, like 'Every human is speaking' and its converse, and in existence; i.e., whenever human exists, speaking exists. And if considered between two propositions, they are considered only in existence, not in truth, since it is not conceivable to predicate propositions of anything. And the commentator mentioned these relations because the context is suitable for them, so it is a good benefit. And the author of 'al-Qadiriyya' alluded to them by his saying: ... And the justification of the enumeration in them is that two concepts (i.e., two meanings present in the mind) are either never compatible — and they are the mutually exclusive in the most specific sense — or never separate — and they are the equivalent — or they sometimes combine and sometimes separate — and the separation is either from one side, which is absolute generality and specificity, or from two sides, such that each separates from its companion, which is generality and specificity from one aspect. And you already know their examples. (Poetic lines from al-Sullam) Comment: His statement: 'And the justification of the enumeration in them' — al-Sayyid objected to this enumeration with the negations of things that encompass mental and external existents, like 'thing' and 'possible', for they are equivalent, but their negations, namely 'nothing' and 'not possible', are two concepts between which there is none of the four relations. As for the negation of relations other than equality, it is obvious. As for the negation of equality, it is because equality is considered in existence, and they have no existence. His statement: 'and they are the mutually exclusive in the most specific sense' means total mutual exclusivity, because his statement 'they never combine' includes contradiction, contrariety, and the opposition of privation and possession. As for partial mutual exclusivity, it falls under generality-specificity from one aspect and absolute generality-specificity, since you say 'some white is not human' and 'some animal is not human'. So it is not intended here. Alert: Know that the equivalents, like 'human' and 'speaking', their negations are always equivalent, like 'not human' and 'not speaking'; meaning that everything to which 'not human' applies, 'not speaking' applies, and vice versa. As for the mutually exclusive pair, their negations are never anything other than mutual exclusivity or generality and specificity from one aspect: First, like 'human' and 'not speaking' — they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, 'not human' and 'speaking', are also mutually exclusive, because whenever one is established, the other is removed. Second, like 'human' and 'not animal' — they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, 'not human' and 'animal', have between them generality and specificity from one aspect, because they combine in the horse, and 'animal' is unique in human, and 'not human' is unique in stone.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from the Bajuri-Bannani commentary on al-Sullam defines the four necessary relations between any two concepts (مفهومان) considered in logic. The author begins by explaining that mutual exclusivity (التباين) — the opposite of synonymy — has four possible forms. First, two concepts may be totally exclusive in both meaning (مفهوم) and referents (مصدوق), meaning neither applies to anything the other applies to; example: 'human' and 'stone'. This includes the relation between a derived term (مشتق) and its root (مشتق منه), as in 'knowledge' and 'knower'. Second, they may be exclusive only in meaning, which splits into two subcases: either they have the same referents (تساوي), like 'laughing' and 'speaking' (if all humans are both), or they do not have the same referents, meaning they differ in some individuals. This difference itself is either from one side (absolute generality-specificity: عموم وخصوص مطلق), like 'animal' and 'human', where the more general has more individuals; or from two sides (generality-specificity from one aspect: عموم وخصوص من وجه), like 'human' and 'black', where they sometimes combine and sometimes separate. The commentator then expands on the example of 'knowledge' and 'knower', citing al-Baqillani and explaining the different kinds of derivation (اشتقاق). He also notes that these relations apply to singular terms in predication and existence, but to propositions only in existence, not in truth. The passage then provides a justification for why the enumeration of relations is exhaustive: any two concepts are either always compatible (equivalence), never compatible (mutual exclusivity), or sometimes compatible and sometimes not. The latter case further divides into separation from one side (absolute generality-specificity) or from two sides (generality-specificity from one aspect). An objection by al-Sayyid regarding the negations of things like 'thing' and 'possible' is addressed: their negations 'nothing' and 'not possible' have no existence, so the relation of equality does not apply to them, thus they do not break the enumeration. Finally, a useful note explains what happens to the negations of equivalent pairs and mutually exclusive pairs. For equivalents, their negations are also equivalent. For mutually exclusive concepts, their negations are either still mutually exclusive or have generality-specificity from one aspect, and examples are given.

Key Terms
التباين
التساوي
العموم والخصوص المطلق
العموم والخصوص من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Equivalence (تساوي)
C) Absolute generality-specificity (عموم وخصوص مطلق)
D) Generality-specificity from one aspect (عموم وخصوص من وجه)

Key Takeaway
Every pair of concepts falls into one of four exhaustive relations: total mutual exclusivity, equivalence, absolute generality-specificity, or generality-specificity from one aspect.

Division and Classification

The Enumerative Justification and Negation of Relations
Justification of the four-relation enumeration and the science of negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why the four relations are exhaustive and determine the relation between the negations of any given concept pair.

Core Idea
The four relations are exhaustive because any two concepts either always combine, never combine, or sometimes combine; negative concepts without existence are outside this scheme.

This lesson builds on the classification by exploring why there are exactly four relations and what happens when we negate the terms. The commentator presents a logical justification for the enumeration and then handles an important objection raised by al-Sayyid regarding negations of concepts like 'thing' and 'possible'. Understanding this deepens our grasp of the logical structure of concepts.

Guided Path
1
Follow the enumeration justification
The text argues: any two concepts are either never compatible (total mutual exclusivity), never separate (equivalence), or sometimes combine and sometimes separate. The last case splits into one-way separation (absolute generality-specificity) or two-way separation (generality-specificity from one aspect). Thus four.
2
Identify al-Sayyid's objection
Al-Sayyid objects: the negations of equivalent concepts like 'thing' and 'possible' — i.e., 'nothing' and 'not possible' — are concepts that do not exist in reality. They are not covered by the four relations because equality requires existence.
3
Understand the response
The response clarifies that the four relations are only for concepts that exist in the mind or reality. Non-existing negative concepts are outside the scope, so the enumeration remains valid for all actual (existing) concepts.
4
Analyze negation patterns
For equivalents, their negations are also equivalent. For mutually exclusive pairs, the negations can be either mutually exclusive or have generality-specificity from one aspect. The passage provides examples: 'human' and 'not speaking' (exclusive) give 'not human' and 'speaking' (also exclusive); 'human' and 'not animal' give 'not human' and 'animal' (general-specific from one aspect).
Practice with other pairs.

فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
واعلم أنَّ التَّباينَ بالمَعْنَى المَذْكُورِ - أَيْ : مُقَابِلِ التَّرَادُفِ - يَقَعُ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ ; لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يَكُونُ كُلّيّا فِي المَفْهُومِ وَالمَصْدُوقِ بِأَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى شَيْءٍ مِمَّا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ الآخَرُ : الإِنْسَانِ، وَ: «الحَجَرِ»، وَمِنْهُ تَبَايِنُ المُشْتَقِّ وَالمُشْتَقِّ مِنْهُ كَـ: «العِلْمِ»، و: «العَالِمِ»، وَقَدْ يَكُونُ فِي المَفْهُومِ فَقَط ، وَذَلِكَ إِمَّا مَعَ تَسَاوِيهِمَا فِي المَصْدُوقِ گ: «الصَّاحِكِ»، وَ: «النَّاطِقِ، وَإِمَّا مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّسَاوِي بِأَنْ يَفْتَرِفًا فِي بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ المَصْدُوقِ، وَالاِفْتِرَاقُ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ گ: «الحَيَوَانِ»، وَ: «الإِنْسَانِ»، فَالأَكْثَرُ أَفْرَاداً أَعَمُّ ، وَالأَقَلُّ أَخَصُّ ، وَمِنْ ذَلِكَ الصِّفَةُ وَالمَوْصُوفُ : السَّيْفِ ، وَ : «الصَّارِمِ، وَكَـ: «النَّاطِقِ، وَ: «الفَصِيحِ، وَإِمَّا مِنَ كَـ الجِهَتَيْنِ مَعاً وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ كَـ: الإِنْسَانِ، وَ: «الأَسْوَدِ»، وَكَـ: «الصَّارِمِ»، وَ: «المُهَنَّدِ ، وَهَذِهِ هِيَ النِّسَبُ الأَرْبَعُ الَّتِي لَا بُدَّ بَيْنَ كُلِّ مَعْقُولَيْنِ مِنْ وَاحِدَةٍ مِنْهَا . (قصارة) قوله : (گـ: «العِلْمِ، وَ العَالِمِ) فإنَّ معنى «العلم» كما قال القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني: «معرفة المعلوم، ومعنى العالم : الذَّات المتّصفة بالعِلم، ولا فرق بين أن يكون معنى المشتق منه قائماً بالمشتق كالمثال، أو موضوعاً لمحل عمل كالحديد للحداد، فإنَّ الحديد الذي هو مشتق منه موضوع للجسم الشَّديد الأسود، وهو محل عمل للحداد الذي هو المشتق؛ أي: يعمل منه الأشياء كالسيوف والدروع، وغير ذلك، فقولُ قدُّورة : موضوعاً لعمل على حذف مضاف؛ أي: محل عمل، أو يكون موجداً له؛ أي: يكون المشتقُ سبباً مجازيًا في وجود المشتق منه كالمال والمتموّل، فإنَّ الموجد للمال بسبب التجارة وغيرها هو المتموّل ؛ أي: صاحب المال . قوله : (وَهَذِهِ هِيَ النِّسَبُ الأَرْبَعُ ... إلخ) اعلم أنَّ هذه النِّسب إن اعتبرت بين المفردين كانت معتبرةً بينهما في الحمل؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ناطق، وبالعكس، وفي الوجود؛ أي: كلَّما الإنسان وجد الناطق، وإن اعتبرت بين القضيَّتين كانت معتبرةً في الوجود فقط دون الصدق؛ إذ لا يتصوَّر حمل القضايا على شيء. (وجد) وتعرض الشارح لذكر هذه النسب؛ لأنَّ المحلَّ لائق بها فهي فائدة حسنة، وقد أشار لها صاحب القادرية» بقوله : (1) في :(أ) مِنْهُمَا، بدلاً من مِنْهَا».
وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا : أَنَّ المَعْقُولَيْنِ ; أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ الحَاصِلَيْنِ فِي العَقْلِ [ج/ ١٥] إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ ، وَإِمَّا [ب/ ۲۲] أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَجْتَمِعَا تَارَةٌ وَيَفْتَرِقَا تَارَةً، وَالافْتِرَاقُ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، وَإِمَّا مِنْ جِهَتَيْنِ بِأَنْ يُفَارِقَ كُلِّ صَاحِبَهُ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَمْيْلَتَهَا . (قصارة) وَكُلُّ مَعْقُولَيْنِ فَاعْلَمْ قَدْ وَجَبْ بَيَنْهَمَّا بَعْضُ مِنْ أَربَعِ نِسَبْ وَهِيَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ المُطلق أَوِ الَّذِي مِنْ جِهَةٍ يُحَفَّق ثُمَّ المُسَاوَاةُ مَعَ التَّبايُن وَالحَصْرُ فِي ذَاكَ بِسَبْرِ كَائِن قوله : (وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا) اعترض السَّيِّد هذا الحصر : بنقائض الأمور الشاملة للموجودات الذهنيّة والخارجيَّة؛ نحو: «شيء» و «ممكن»، فإنَّهما متساويان ونقيضاهما وهما: «لا شيء» و: «لا ممكن مفهومان ليس بينهما واحدة مِنَ النّسب الأربع، أمَّا نفي ما عدا المساواة فظاهر، وأمَّا نفي المساواة فلأنَّها معتبرة في الوجود، ولا وجود لهما . قوله : (وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصَّ) يعني : التَّباين الكلِّيَّ ; لأنَّ قوله : «أن لا يجتمعا البتة» شامل للتناقض والتّضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة ، وأمَّا التَّباين الجزئي فمندرج تحت العموم من وجه، والعموم بإطلاق ; إذ تقول : بعض الأبيض ليس بإنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان»، فهو غيرُ مراد هنا . تنبية اعلم أنَّ المتساويين كـ «الإنسان، والناطق»، نقيضاهما متساويان أبداً كـ: «لا إنسان، ولا ناطق ; بمعنى : أنَّ كلَّ ما صدق عليه لا إنسان صدق عليه لا ناطق»، وبالعكس. وأما المتباينان فنقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا التَّباين أو العموم والخصوص من وجه: ۱ - فالأوَّل كـ : «الإنسان، ولا «ناطق فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان، وناطق» وهما متباينان أيضاً; إذ كلما ثبت أحدهما ارتفع الآخر. ۲ - والثاني كـ : «الإنسان، ولا حيوان فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، وحيوان» وبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه; لاجتماعهما في الفرس وانفراد حيوان في الإنسان، وانفراد لا إنسان في الحجر .
Chapter on clarifying the relationship of words to meanings. Know that at-tabāyun (mutual exclusivity) in the mentioned sense — i.e., opposite of at-tarāduf (synonymy) — falls into four divisions: because it may be total in concept and reference, such that neither applies to anything that the other applies to, like 'human' and 'stone'; and from it is the mutual exclusivity of the derived term and its base, like 'knowledge' and 'knower'. And it may be only in concept, and that either with equality in reference, like 'laughing' and 'speaking', or without equality, such that they differ in some individuals of reference, and the difference is either from one side, which is absolute generality and specificity, like 'animal' and 'human' — the one with more individuals is more general, the one with fewer is more specific — and from that is the attribute and the described, like 'sword' and 'sharp', and like 'speaking' and 'eloquent'. Or it may be from two sides together, which is generality and specificity from one aspect, like 'human' and 'black', and like 'sharp' and 'Indian sword'. These are the four relations which must exist between every two concepts: one of them. (Commentary: His statement: 'like knowledge and knower' — for the meaning of 'knowledge', as Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said, is 'knowledge of the known', and the meaning of 'knower' is the essence characterized by knowledge. There is no difference between the meaning of the derivative being inherent in the derivative as in the example, or being the subject of an action like iron for the blacksmith, for iron which is the base is the name for the hard black body, and it is the place of action for the blacksmith who is the derivative; i.e., he works from it things like swords and shields and so forth. So the saying of Qaddura: 'subject for action' with a deleted genitive construction, i.e., place of action, or it is an efficient cause; i.e., the derivative is a metaphorical cause of the existence of the base, like property and the possessor of property, for the one who produces property through trade and other means is the possessor of property; i.e., the owner of property. His statement: 'These are the four relations... etc.' Know that these relations, if considered between two singular terms, are considered between them in predication, like 'Every human is speaking' and its converse, and in existence; i.e., whenever human exists, speaking exists. And if considered between two propositions, they are considered only in existence, not in truth, since it is not conceivable to predicate propositions of anything. And the commentator mentioned these relations because the context is suitable for them, so it is a good benefit. And the author of 'al-Qadiriyya' alluded to them by his saying: ... And the justification of the enumeration in them is that two concepts (i.e., two meanings present in the mind) are either never compatible — and they are the mutually exclusive in the most specific sense — or never separate — and they are the equivalent — or they sometimes combine and sometimes separate — and the separation is either from one side, which is absolute generality and specificity, or from two sides, such that each separates from its companion, which is generality and specificity from one aspect. And you already know their examples. (Poetic lines from al-Sullam) Comment: His statement: 'And the justification of the enumeration in them' — al-Sayyid objected to this enumeration with the negations of things that encompass mental and external existents, like 'thing' and 'possible', for they are equivalent, but their negations, namely 'nothing' and 'not possible', are two concepts between which there is none of the four relations. As for the negation of relations other than equality, it is obvious. As for the negation of equality, it is because equality is considered in existence, and they have no existence. His statement: 'and they are the mutually exclusive in the most specific sense' means total mutual exclusivity, because his statement 'they never combine' includes contradiction, contrariety, and the opposition of privation and possession. As for partial mutual exclusivity, it falls under generality-specificity from one aspect and absolute generality-specificity, since you say 'some white is not human' and 'some animal is not human'. So it is not intended here. Alert: Know that the equivalents, like 'human' and 'speaking', their negations are always equivalent, like 'not human' and 'not speaking'; meaning that everything to which 'not human' applies, 'not speaking' applies, and vice versa. As for the mutually exclusive pair, their negations are never anything other than mutual exclusivity or generality and specificity from one aspect: First, like 'human' and 'not speaking' — they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, 'not human' and 'speaking', are also mutually exclusive, because whenever one is established, the other is removed. Second, like 'human' and 'not animal' — they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, 'not human' and 'animal', have between them generality and specificity from one aspect, because they combine in the horse, and 'animal' is unique in human, and 'not human' is unique in stone.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage from the Bajuri-Bannani commentary on al-Sullam defines the four necessary relations between any two concepts (مفهومان) considered in logic. The author begins by explaining that mutual exclusivity (التباين) — the opposite of synonymy — has four possible forms. First, two concepts may be totally exclusive in both meaning (مفهوم) and referents (مصدوق), meaning neither applies to anything the other applies to; example: 'human' and 'stone'. This includes the relation between a derived term (مشتق) and its root (مشتق منه), as in 'knowledge' and 'knower'. Second, they may be exclusive only in meaning, which splits into two subcases: either they have the same referents (تساوي), like 'laughing' and 'speaking' (if all humans are both), or they do not have the same referents, meaning they differ in some individuals. This difference itself is either from one side (absolute generality-specificity: عموم وخصوص مطلق), like 'animal' and 'human', where the more general has more individuals; or from two sides (generality-specificity from one aspect: عموم وخصوص من وجه), like 'human' and 'black', where they sometimes combine and sometimes separate. The commentator then expands on the example of 'knowledge' and 'knower', citing al-Baqillani and explaining the different kinds of derivation (اشتقاق). He also notes that these relations apply to singular terms in predication and existence, but to propositions only in existence, not in truth. The passage then provides a justification for why the enumeration of relations is exhaustive: any two concepts are either always compatible (equivalence), never compatible (mutual exclusivity), or sometimes compatible and sometimes not. The latter case further divides into separation from one side (absolute generality-specificity) or from two sides (generality-specificity from one aspect). An objection by al-Sayyid regarding the negations of things like 'thing' and 'possible' is addressed: their negations 'nothing' and 'not possible' have no existence, so the relation of equality does not apply to them, thus they do not break the enumeration. Finally, a useful note explains what happens to the negations of equivalent pairs and mutually exclusive pairs. For equivalents, their negations are also equivalent. For mutually exclusive concepts, their negations are either still mutually exclusive or have generality-specificity from one aspect, and examples are given.

Key Terms
التباين
التساوي
العموم والخصوص المطلق
العموم والخصوص من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between the negations of two equivalent concepts?
A) Mutual exclusivity
B) Equivalence
C) Absolute generality-specificity
D) Generality-specificity from one aspect

Key Takeaway
The four relations are exhaustive for existing concepts; negations of equivalent pairs are equivalent, while negations of mutually exclusive pairs are either mutually exclusive or have general-specificity from one aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Determine the relation between the negations of 'human' and 'animal' (i.e., 'not human' and 'not animal') using the rules from this passage. Is the relation mutual exclusivity or generality-specificity from one aspect? Explain.
?
Consider the pair 'wajib' and 'mubah' (obligatory and permissible) in fiqh. Are they mutually exclusive? If so, what is the relation between 'not wajib' and 'not mubah'? Use the passage to answer.

Division and Classification
intermediate
8 min
The Four Relationships Between Concepts
Author explains the four nisab between concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any pair of distinct mental concepts into one of the four logical relationships (mutabayin, mutasawi, umum wa khusus mutlaq, umum wa khusus min wajh) using the extension criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
Two concepts are related by one of four relationships: never combine, never separate, or combine and separate in one or two directions.

In this passage, the commentator al-Bajuri expands on the enumeration of relationships between mental concepts. Understanding these four categories is essential for logical analysis, especially in Islamic legal theory where concepts like 'command' and 'obligation' must be precisely related.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Take a pair of distinct mental objects (ma'qulān). For example, 'human' (insān) and 'animal' (ḥayawān). The division applies only to distinct concepts; synonyms are excluded.
The passage specifies 'المعقولان' meaning the two concepts obtained in the mind.
2
Check if they can never combine in extension
Ask: Is there any instance where both concepts apply simultaneously? If not, they are mutabāyinān in the specific sense of total mutual exclusivity. This includes contradictions (e.g., 'animal' and 'not-animal'), opposites (e.g., black and white), and privation-possession (e.g., blind and sighted).
Partial mutual exclusivity is not included here; it falls under other relationships.
3
Check if they can never separate in extension
Ask: Is it the case that every instance of one is an instance of the other and vice versa? If yes, they are mutasāwiyān (equivalent). Example: 'human' and 'rational' (nāṭiq) are considered equivalent in definition.
Equivalence does not mean identity of concept; it means coextensive.
4
If they sometimes combine and sometimes separate, determine the mode of separation
If they combine in some instances and separate in others, then classify: if one concept is broader (includes all instances of the other plus more), it is 'umūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq' (absolute general-specific). If each concept has instances the other does not, it is 'umūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh' (partial overlap).
Example of absolute: 'animal' (general) and 'human' (specific). Example of partial: 'human' and 'white'.

وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا : أَنَّ المَعْقُولَيْنِ أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ الحَاصِلَيْنِ فِي العَقْلِ إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَجْتَمِعَا تَارَةٌ وَيَفْتَرِقَا تَارَةً، وَالافْتِرَاقُ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، وَإِمَّا مِنْ جِهَتَيْنِ بِأَنْ يُفَارِقَ كُلِّ صَاحِبَهُ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَمْيِلَتَهَا . قُصَارَةُ وَكُلُّ مَعْقُولَيْنِ فَاعْلَمْ قَدْ وَجَبْ بَيَنْهَمَّا بَعْضُ مِنْ أَرْبَعِ نِسَبْ وَهِيَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ المُطْلَق أَوِ الَّذِي مِنْ جِهَةٍ يُحَقَّق ثُمَّ المُسَاوَاةُ مَعَ التَّبَايُن وَالحَصْرُ فِي ذَاكَ بِسَبْرِ كَائِن قوله : (وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا) اعترض السَّيِّد هذا الحصر : بنقائض الأمور الشاملة للموجودات الذهنيّة والخارجيَّة؛ نحو: «شيء» و «ممكن»، فإنَّهما متساويان ونقيضاهما وهما: «لا شيء» و: «لا ممكن مفهومان ليس بينهما واحدة مِنَ النّسب الأربع، أمَّا نفي ما عدا المساواة فظاهر، وأمَّا نفي المساواة فلأنَّها معتبرة في الوجود، ولا وجود لهما . قوله : (وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصَّ) يعني : التَّباين الكلِّيَّ ؛ لأنَّ قوله : «أن لا يجتمعا البتة» شامل للتناقض والتّضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة ، وأمَّا التَّباين الجزئي فمندرج تحت العموم من وجه، والعموم بإطلاق ؛ إذ تقول : بعض الأبيض ليس بإنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان»، فهو غيرُ مراد هنا . تنبيه اعلم أنَّ المتساويين كـ «الإنسان، والناطق»، نقيضاهما متساويان أبداً كـ: «لا إنسان، ولا ناطق ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ كلَّ ما صدق عليه لا إنسان صدق عليه لا ناطق»، وبالعكس. وأما المتباينان فنقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا التَّباين أو العموم والخصوص من وجه: ١ - فالأوَّل كـ : «الإنسان، ولا «ناطق فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان، وناطق» وهما متباينان أيضاً؛ إذ كلما ثبت أحدهما ارتفع الآخر. ٢ - والثاني كـ : «الإنسان، ولا حيوان فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، وحيوان» وبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه؛ لاجتماعهما في الفرس وانفراد حيوان في الإنسان، وانفراد لا إنسان في الحجر . فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني فَإِنْ قِيْلَ : القِسْمَةُ غَيْرُ حَاصِرَةٍ؛ لِبَقَاءِ التَّنَاقُضِ. قُلْنَا : هُوَ دَاخِلٌّ فِي القِسْم الأَوَّلِ، وَهُوَ التَّبَايِنُ الكُلّيُّ؛ لأنَّ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ البَتَّةَ گ: حَيَوَانٍ، وَ لَا حَيَوَانِ، وَمِثْلُهُ التَّضَادُّ فِي نَحْوِ : «البَيَاضِ، وَ: السَّوَادِ»، وَالتَّضَايفُ فِي مِثْلِ: «الأبُوَّةِ، وَ البُنُوَّةِ». فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: قَوْلُكَ فِي القِسْم الثَّانِي: أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَيَّةَ يَشْمَلُ المُتَرَادِفَيْنِ نَحْوُ الإِنْسَانِ، وَ : البَشَر»، وَلَا يَصِحُ عَلَى هَذَا (١): عَدُّ التَّرَادُفِ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ التَّبَاينِ . قُلْتُ : جَعْلُ مَوْضُوعِ القِسْمَةِ المَعْقُولَيْنِ ؛ أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ المُخْتَلِفَيْنِ يَمْنَعُ شُمُول المُتَرَادِفَيْن لاتِّحَادِ مَفْهُومِهِما . قُصَارَةُ وأمَّا اللذان بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، فكذلك نقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا العموم والخصوص من وجه أو التَّباين : ١ - فالأوَّل ؛ نحو: الإنسان، والأبيض فبينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه وهو ظاهر، ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان ولا أبيض وهما كذلك ؛ لاجتماعهما في الغراب مثلاً، وانفراد الأوّل في الثلج، والثاني في الزنجي. ٢ - والثاني كـ «الحَيَوان، ولا إنسان بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه؛ لاجتماعهما في الفرس مثلاً، وانفراد الحيوان في الإنسان، ولا إنسان في الجماد؛ ونقيضاهما: «لا حيوان، وإنسان وهما متباينان ضرورةً . وأمَّا اللذان بينهما عموم وخصوص بإطلاق فيلزم أن يكون بين نقيضيهما عموم وخصوص بإطلاق كذلك، لكن على العكس؛ أي: نقيض الأعمّ أخصُّ، ونقيضُ الأخص أعم، وذلك ك: «الإنسان والحيوان»، فالإنسان أخصُّ، ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، ولا حيوان»؛ فـ «لا إنسان» أعم من لا حيوان»؛ لصدق لا إنسان على ما صدق عليه لا حيوان» من كل مباين للحيوان، وزيادته عليه بصدقه على غير الإنسان من أنواع الحيوان، وذلك واضح، وقد أشار ابن مرزوق إلى النسب الأربع التي بين النقائض بقوله : فَذُو السَّسَاوِي وَالعُمُومُ المُطلَق نَقِيضُ كُلِّ مِنْلُهُ فَحَقِّق (١) على هذا زيادة من الطبعة الأميرية فقط.
And the aspect of the enumeration (of the four relationships) is that the two mental objects—i.e., the two concepts obtaining in the mind—are either that they never combine together, and they are the two mutually exclusive (mutabayinan) in the specific sense; or that they never separate together, and they are the two equivalent (mutasawiyan); or that they combine sometimes and separate sometimes, and the separation is either from one aspect—which is general-specific absolute (umum wa khusus bi-itlaq)—or from two aspects—where each separates from its companion, which is general-specific from one aspect (umum wa khusus min wajh). And you have known their divisions. The summary: And every two concepts, know, there must be between them one of four relationships: general-specific absolute, or that which is from one aspect, then equivalence together with mutual exclusivity, and the enumeration in that is by a complete survey. His statement: (And the aspect of the enumeration in them) al-Sayyid objected to this enumeration with the negations of universal things encompassing mental and external existents, like 'thing' and 'possible', for they are equivalent, and their negations, which are 'not-thing' and 'not-possible', are two concepts between which there is none of the four relationships. As for the negation of other than equivalence, it is apparent; as for the negation of equivalence, it is because it is considered in existence, and they have no existence. His statement: (and they are the two mutually exclusive in the specific sense) means total mutual exclusivity; because his statement 'that they never combine' includes contradiction, opposition, and the privation-possession contrast. As for partial mutual exclusivity, it is included under general-specific from one aspect and general-specific absolute; since you say: 'some white is not human, and some animal is not human', so it is not intended here. A note: Know that the two equivalents, like 'human' and 'rational', their negations are ever equivalent, like 'not-human' and 'not-rational'; meaning that everything of which 'not-human' is true, 'not-rational' is true of it, and vice versa. As for the two mutually exclusive, their negations are never between them except mutual exclusivity or general-specific from one aspect: 1. The first, like 'human' and 'not-rational'; they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, which are 'not-human' and 'rational', are also mutually exclusive; since whenever one is established, the other is removed. 2. The second, like 'human' and 'not-animal'; they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, 'not-human' and 'animal', have between them general-specific from one aspect; because they combine in the horse, 'animal' is unique to human, and 'not-human' is unique to stone. A chapter on explaining the relationship of words to meanings: Then if it is said: The division is not exhaustive; because of the remaining of contradiction. We say: It is included in the first division, which is total mutual exclusivity; because the two contradictories never combine, like 'animal' and 'not-animal'; and similar is opposition in cases like 'whiteness' and 'blackness', and correlation in cases like 'fatherhood' and 'sonhood'. Then if you say: Your statement in the second division, 'that they never separate', includes synonyms, like 'human' and 'man', and it is not valid on this (basis) to count synonymy as a type of mutual exclusivity. I say: Making the subject of the division the two mental objects—i.e., the two distinct concepts—prevents the inclusion of synonyms because of the unity of their concept. Summary: And as for those two between which there is general-specific from one aspect, similarly their negations are never between them except general-specific from one aspect or mutual exclusivity: 1. The first: like 'human' and 'white' (there is general-specific from one aspect between them, which is apparent), and their negations, 'not-human' and 'not-white', are likewise; because they combine in the crow, for example, and the first is unique to snow, and the second to the black person. 2. The second: like 'animal' and 'not-human' (there is general-specific from one aspect between them; because they combine in the horse, for example, and 'animal' is unique to human, and 'not-human' to inorganic matter); and their negations, 'not-animal' and 'human', are mutually exclusive necessarily. And as for those two between which there is general-specific absolute, it follows that between their negations there is general-specific absolute likewise, but in reverse; i.e., the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general. That is like 'human' and 'animal'; 'human' is more specific, and their negations are 'not-human' and 'not-animal'; so 'not-human' is more general than 'not-animal'; because 'not-human' applies to everything of which 'not-animal' applies, from everything mutually exclusive to animal, and with an increase by applying to other than human from the types of animal. And that is clear. And Ibn Marzuq has indicated the four relationships between negations by his saying: So those of equivalence and general absolute, the negation of each is like it, so verify.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sullam, expands on the classification of relationships between mental concepts (ma'qulat) and then derives the relationships between their negations (naqaid). It begins by stating the reason for the enumeration of four relationships: two concepts either never combine (mutabayinan in the specific sense of total mutual exclusivity, including contradiction, opposition, and privation-possession), never separate (mutasawiyan), or combine sometimes and separate sometimes. The separation is further divided: from one aspect (general-specific absolute) or from two aspects (general-specific from one aspect). The author notes that partial mutual exclusivity falls under the latter two, not under the first division. Then an objection by al-Sayyid al-Sharif is addressed: the negations of universal concepts like 'thing' and 'possible' do not fall into any of the four relationships because they are non-existent; the response implies that such concepts are not considered 'mental objects' in the sense intended. Next, a key note (tanbih) explains that for two equivalent concepts (e.g., 'human' and 'rational'), their negations are also equivalent. For two mutually exclusive concepts, their negations are either mutually exclusive or general-specific from one aspect, with examples. The text then moves to a new section (Fasl) that addresses two objections: (1) that contradiction is missing from the division; the answer is that it is included under total mutual exclusivity. (2) that synonyms (e.g., 'human' and 'man') would fall under the 'never separate' category, contradicting their usual classification as a type of mutual exclusivity (since they are two words for one meaning). The response clarifies that the division is about distinct concepts, so synonyms are excluded. Finally, the passage systematically works out the relationships between negations for the remaining types: general-specific from one aspect (negations can be general-specific from one aspect or mutually exclusive) and general-specific absolute (negations are reversed: the negation of the general is specific, and vice versa), followed by a poetic summary by Ibn Marzuq. Throughout, the commentary provides concrete examples from logic and language, maintaining precision in the distinctions.

Checkpoint
Which relationship exists between the concepts 'zakat' (obligatory charity) and 'sadaqah' (voluntary charity)?
A) Mutabayinan (mutually exclusive)
B) Mutasawiyan (equivalent)
C) Umum wa khusus mutlaq (absolute general-specific)
D) Umum wa khusus min wajh (partial general-specific)

Key Takeaway
The four relationships between concepts are defined by whether they never combine, never separate, or combine and separate in one or two directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the relationship between 'salah' (obligatory prayer) and 'du'a' (supplication) based on the criteria. Are they mutually exclusive, equivalent, absolute general-specific, or partial general-specific? Explain using examples.

Division and Classification
intermediate
10 min
The Relationships Between Negations
Author explains the four nisab between concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the logical relationship between the negations of two concepts given the relationship between the original concepts, using the rules derived in the passage.

Core Idea
The relationship between negations is derived from the original: equivalence stays, mutual exclusivity can yield exclusivity or partial overlap, absolute general-specific reverses, and partial overlap can yield partial overlap or exclusivity.

This passage extends the classification of relationships to the negations (naqaid) of concepts. Understanding these inference patterns is crucial for correct logical reasoning, especially when negating terms in theological or legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original relationship
First, classify the pair of positive concepts using the four relationships. For example, 'human' and 'animal' are umum wa khusus mutlaq (absolute general-specific).
This is a prerequisite for applying the negation rules.
2
Apply the rule for equivalents
If the original concepts are equivalent (mutasawiyan), then their negations are also equivalent. Example: 'human' and 'rational' imply 'not-human' and 'not-rational' are equivalent. The passage states this explicitly in the note (tanbih).
This is the simplest case.
3
Apply the rule for mutually exclusive concepts
If the original concepts are mutually exclusive (mutabayinan in the strict sense), their negations can be either mutually exclusive or umum wa khusus min wajh (partial overlap). Example: 'human' and 'not-rational' (mutually exclusive) => negations 'not-human' and 'rational' are also mutually exclusive. 'human' and 'not-animal' (mutually exclusive) => negations 'not-human' and 'animal' are umum wa khusus min wajh.
The outcome depends on the specific concepts: check if the negations combine in some instance.
4
Apply the rule for absolute general-specific
If the originals are umum wa khusus mutlaq, then the negations are also umum wa khusus mutlaq but reversed: the negation of the general becomes the specific, and the negation of the specific becomes the general. Example: 'human' (specific) and 'animal' (general) => 'not-human' (general) and 'not-animal' (specific).
This reversal is a key insight.
5
Apply the rule for partial general-specific
If the originals are umum wa khusus min wajh, the negations can be either umum wa khusus min wajh or mutabayin. Example: 'human' and 'white' => negations 'not-human' and 'not-white' are umum wa khusus min wajh. 'animal' and 'not-human' => negations 'not-animal' and 'human' are mutabayin.
Again, the result depends on the specific concepts.

وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا : أَنَّ المَعْقُولَيْنِ أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ الحَاصِلَيْنِ فِي العَقْلِ إِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَجْتَمِعَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصُ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَتَّةَ وَهُمَا المُتَسَاوِيَانِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَجْتَمِعَا تَارَةٌ وَيَفْتَرِقَا تَارَةً، وَالافْتِرَاقُ إِمَّا مِنْ جِهَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ بِإِطْلَاقِ، وَإِمَّا مِنْ جِهَتَيْنِ بِأَنْ يُفَارِقَ كُلِّ صَاحِبَهُ وَهُوَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ مِنْ وَجْهِ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَمْيِلَتَهَا . قُصَارَةُ وَكُلُّ مَعْقُولَيْنِ فَاعْلَمْ قَدْ وَجَبْ بَيَنْهَمَّا بَعْضُ مِنْ أَرْبَعِ نِسَبْ وَهِيَ العُمُومُ وَالخُصُوصُ المُطْلَق أَوِ الَّذِي مِنْ جِهَةٍ يُحَقَّق ثُمَّ المُسَاوَاةُ مَعَ التَّبَايُن وَالحَصْرُ فِي ذَاكَ بِسَبْرِ كَائِن قوله : (وَوَجْهُ الحَصْرِ فِيهَا) اعترض السَّيِّد هذا الحصر : بنقائض الأمور الشاملة للموجودات الذهنيّة والخارجيَّة؛ نحو: «شيء» و «ممكن»، فإنَّهما متساويان ونقيضاهما وهما: «لا شيء» و: «لا ممكن مفهومان ليس بينهما واحدة مِنَ النّسب الأربع، أمَّا نفي ما عدا المساواة فظاهر، وأمَّا نفي المساواة فلأنَّها معتبرة في الوجود، ولا وجود لهما . قوله : (وَهُمَا المُتَبَايِنَانِ بِالمَعْنَى الأَخَصَّ) يعني : التَّباين الكلِّيَّ ؛ لأنَّ قوله : «أن لا يجتمعا البتة» شامل للتناقض والتّضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة ، وأمَّا التَّباين الجزئي فمندرج تحت العموم من وجه، والعموم بإطلاق ؛ إذ تقول : بعض الأبيض ليس بإنسان، وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان»، فهو غيرُ مراد هنا . تنبيه اعلم أنَّ المتساويين كـ «الإنسان، والناطق»، نقيضاهما متساويان أبداً كـ: «لا إنسان، ولا ناطق ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ كلَّ ما صدق عليه لا إنسان صدق عليه لا ناطق»، وبالعكس. وأما المتباينان فنقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا التَّباين أو العموم والخصوص من وجه: ١ - فالأوَّل كـ : «الإنسان، ولا «ناطق فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان، وناطق» وهما متباينان أيضاً؛ إذ كلما ثبت أحدهما ارتفع الآخر. ٢ - والثاني كـ : «الإنسان، ولا حيوان فهما متباينان ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، وحيوان» وبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه؛ لاجتماعهما في الفرس وانفراد حيوان في الإنسان، وانفراد لا إنسان في الحجر . فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني فَإِنْ قِيْلَ : القِسْمَةُ غَيْرُ حَاصِرَةٍ؛ لِبَقَاءِ التَّنَاقُضِ. قُلْنَا : هُوَ دَاخِلٌّ فِي القِسْم الأَوَّلِ، وَهُوَ التَّبَايِنُ الكُلّيُّ؛ لأنَّ النَّقِيضَيْنِ لَا يَجْتَمِعَانِ البَتَّةَ گ: حَيَوَانٍ، وَ لَا حَيَوَانِ، وَمِثْلُهُ التَّضَادُّ فِي نَحْوِ : «البَيَاضِ، وَ: السَّوَادِ»، وَالتَّضَايفُ فِي مِثْلِ: «الأبُوَّةِ، وَ البُنُوَّةِ». فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: قَوْلُكَ فِي القِسْم الثَّانِي: أَنْ لَا يَفْتَرِقَا البَيَّةَ يَشْمَلُ المُتَرَادِفَيْنِ نَحْوُ الإِنْسَانِ، وَ : البَشَر»، وَلَا يَصِحُ عَلَى هَذَا (١): عَدُّ التَّرَادُفِ مِنْ أَقْسَامِ التَّبَاينِ . قُلْتُ : جَعْلُ مَوْضُوعِ القِسْمَةِ المَعْقُولَيْنِ ؛ أَي : المَفْهُومَيْنِ المُخْتَلِفَيْنِ يَمْنَعُ شُمُول المُتَرَادِفَيْن لاتِّحَادِ مَفْهُومِهِما . قُصَارَةُ وأمَّا اللذان بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، فكذلك نقيضاهما لا يكون أبداً بينهما إلَّا العموم والخصوص من وجه أو التَّباين : ١ - فالأوَّل ؛ نحو: الإنسان، والأبيض فبينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه وهو ظاهر، ونقيضاهما : ( لا إنسان ولا أبيض وهما كذلك ؛ لاجتماعهما في الغراب مثلاً، وانفراد الأوّل في الثلج، والثاني في الزنجي. ٢ - والثاني كـ «الحَيَوان، ولا إنسان بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه؛ لاجتماعهما في الفرس مثلاً، وانفراد الحيوان في الإنسان، ولا إنسان في الجماد؛ ونقيضاهما: «لا حيوان، وإنسان وهما متباينان ضرورةً . وأمَّا اللذان بينهما عموم وخصوص بإطلاق فيلزم أن يكون بين نقيضيهما عموم وخصوص بإطلاق كذلك، لكن على العكس؛ أي: نقيض الأعمّ أخصُّ، ونقيضُ الأخص أعم، وذلك ك: «الإنسان والحيوان»، فالإنسان أخصُّ، ونقيضاهما : «لا إنسان، ولا حيوان»؛ فـ «لا إنسان» أعم من لا حيوان»؛ لصدق لا إنسان على ما صدق عليه لا حيوان» من كل مباين للحيوان، وزيادته عليه بصدقه على غير الإنسان من أنواع الحيوان، وذلك واضح، وقد أشار ابن مرزوق إلى النسب الأربع التي بين النقائض بقوله : فَذُو السَّسَاوِي وَالعُمُومُ المُطلَق نَقِيضُ كُلِّ مِنْلُهُ فَحَقِّق (١) على هذا زيادة من الطبعة الأميرية فقط.
And the aspect of the enumeration (of the four relationships) is that the two mental objects—i.e., the two concepts obtaining in the mind—are either that they never combine together, and they are the two mutually exclusive (mutabayinan) in the specific sense; or that they never separate together, and they are the two equivalent (mutasawiyan); or that they combine sometimes and separate sometimes, and the separation is either from one aspect—which is general-specific absolute (umum wa khusus bi-itlaq)—or from two aspects—where each separates from its companion, which is general-specific from one aspect (umum wa khusus min wajh). And you have known their divisions. The summary: And every two concepts, know, there must be between them one of four relationships: general-specific absolute, or that which is from one aspect, then equivalence together with mutual exclusivity, and the enumeration in that is by a complete survey. His statement: (And the aspect of the enumeration in them) al-Sayyid objected to this enumeration with the negations of universal things encompassing mental and external existents, like 'thing' and 'possible', for they are equivalent, and their negations, which are 'not-thing' and 'not-possible', are two concepts between which there is none of the four relationships. As for the negation of other than equivalence, it is apparent; as for the negation of equivalence, it is because it is considered in existence, and they have no existence. His statement: (and they are the two mutually exclusive in the specific sense) means total mutual exclusivity; because his statement 'that they never combine' includes contradiction, opposition, and the privation-possession contrast. As for partial mutual exclusivity, it is included under general-specific from one aspect and general-specific absolute; since you say: 'some white is not human, and some animal is not human', so it is not intended here. A note: Know that the two equivalents, like 'human' and 'rational', their negations are ever equivalent, like 'not-human' and 'not-rational'; meaning that everything of which 'not-human' is true, 'not-rational' is true of it, and vice versa. As for the two mutually exclusive, their negations are never between them except mutual exclusivity or general-specific from one aspect: 1. The first, like 'human' and 'not-rational'; they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, which are 'not-human' and 'rational', are also mutually exclusive; since whenever one is established, the other is removed. 2. The second, like 'human' and 'not-animal'; they are mutually exclusive, and their negations, 'not-human' and 'animal', have between them general-specific from one aspect; because they combine in the horse, 'animal' is unique to human, and 'not-human' is unique to stone. A chapter on explaining the relationship of words to meanings: Then if it is said: The division is not exhaustive; because of the remaining of contradiction. We say: It is included in the first division, which is total mutual exclusivity; because the two contradictories never combine, like 'animal' and 'not-animal'; and similar is opposition in cases like 'whiteness' and 'blackness', and correlation in cases like 'fatherhood' and 'sonhood'. Then if you say: Your statement in the second division, 'that they never separate', includes synonyms, like 'human' and 'man', and it is not valid on this (basis) to count synonymy as a type of mutual exclusivity. I say: Making the subject of the division the two mental objects—i.e., the two distinct concepts—prevents the inclusion of synonyms because of the unity of their concept. Summary: And as for those two between which there is general-specific from one aspect, similarly their negations are never between them except general-specific from one aspect or mutual exclusivity: 1. The first: like 'human' and 'white' (there is general-specific from one aspect between them, which is apparent), and their negations, 'not-human' and 'not-white', are likewise; because they combine in the crow, for example, and the first is unique to snow, and the second to the black person. 2. The second: like 'animal' and 'not-human' (there is general-specific from one aspect between them; because they combine in the horse, for example, and 'animal' is unique to human, and 'not-human' to inorganic matter); and their negations, 'not-animal' and 'human', are mutually exclusive necessarily. And as for those two between which there is general-specific absolute, it follows that between their negations there is general-specific absolute likewise, but in reverse; i.e., the negation of the more general is more specific, and the negation of the more specific is more general. That is like 'human' and 'animal'; 'human' is more specific, and their negations are 'not-human' and 'not-animal'; so 'not-human' is more general than 'not-animal'; because 'not-human' applies to everything of which 'not-animal' applies, from everything mutually exclusive to animal, and with an increase by applying to other than human from the types of animal. And that is clear. And Ibn Marzuq has indicated the four relationships between negations by his saying: So those of equivalence and general absolute, the negation of each is like it, so verify.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sullam, expands on the classification of relationships between mental concepts (ma'qulat) and then derives the relationships between their negations (naqaid). It begins by stating the reason for the enumeration of four relationships: two concepts either never combine (mutabayinan in the specific sense of total mutual exclusivity, including contradiction, opposition, and privation-possession), never separate (mutasawiyan), or combine sometimes and separate sometimes. The separation is further divided: from one aspect (general-specific absolute) or from two aspects (general-specific from one aspect). The author notes that partial mutual exclusivity falls under the latter two, not under the first division. Then an objection by al-Sayyid al-Sharif is addressed: the negations of universal concepts like 'thing' and 'possible' do not fall into any of the four relationships because they are non-existent; the response implies that such concepts are not considered 'mental objects' in the sense intended. Next, a key note (tanbih) explains that for two equivalent concepts (e.g., 'human' and 'rational'), their negations are also equivalent. For two mutually exclusive concepts, their negations are either mutually exclusive or general-specific from one aspect, with examples. The text then moves to a new section (Fasl) that addresses two objections: (1) that contradiction is missing from the division; the answer is that it is included under total mutual exclusivity. (2) that synonyms (e.g., 'human' and 'man') would fall under the 'never separate' category, contradicting their usual classification as a type of mutual exclusivity (since they are two words for one meaning). The response clarifies that the division is about distinct concepts, so synonyms are excluded. Finally, the passage systematically works out the relationships between negations for the remaining types: general-specific from one aspect (negations can be general-specific from one aspect or mutually exclusive) and general-specific absolute (negations are reversed: the negation of the general is specific, and vice versa), followed by a poetic summary by Ibn Marzuq. Throughout, the commentary provides concrete examples from logic and language, maintaining precision in the distinctions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the negations of 'human' (insān) and 'animal' (ḥayawān)?
A) They are mutually exclusive (mutabāyinān).
B) They are equivalent (mutasāwiyān).
C) They have general-specific absolute, but reversed.
D) They have general-specific from one aspect.

Key Takeaway
The relationship between negations is systematically derived from the relationship between concepts, with predictable transformations: equivalence stays, mutual exclusivity can vary, absolute general-specific reverses, and partial general-specific can yield either partial or mutual exclusivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given that 'hajj' (pilgrimage to Mecca) and 'umrah' (lesser pilgrimage) have the relationship of umum wa khusus min wajh (they overlap partially), determine the relationship between 'non-hajj' and 'non-umrah'. Is it necessarily the same? Provide evidence from the passage.

Division and Classification
beginner
10 min
Defining al-Mushtarak: A Word with Multiple Independent Meanings
Author defines the third division of word-meaning relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mushtarak word and verify that each meaning has a separate real placement (wad' haqiqi).

Core Idea
A homonym requires independent real placements for each meaning, not just multiple uses.

In the study of word-meaning relationships, mushtarak is a critical division. This lesson unpacks its definition and the key condition of wad' haqiqi, which distinguishes it from mere polysemy or metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Extract the defining conditions
Find in the Arabic the conditions for mushtarak: the word (lafz) is one, its meanings (musamma) are multiple, and each meaning is placed by a separate real placement (wad' haqiqi) without transfer from one to another.
The term 'wad' haqiqi' is key: it means an original lexical placement, not metaphorical or derived.
2
Apply the conditions to an example
Take the word 'ayn. According to the passage, it is placed for the eye, for a spring, and for coin, each independently. Confirm that these are separate real placements, not transfer from one meaning to another.
The author explicitly says 'without considering its transfer from one to another'.
3
Contrast with words that have one meaning
Notice that mushtarak is distinct from words like 'insan' (human) which have one meaning but apply to many individuals. The multiplicity in mushtarak is in the meanings themselves, not the individuals.
This contrast helps cement the definition.
4
Verify the definition's completeness
Check whether the definition includes all cases of mushtarak and excludes non-cases. For example, metaphorical uses do not count because they lack an independent real placement.
The passage later uses 'asad' to illustrate this exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِسْمُ الثَّالِثُ مِنَ الأَقْسَامِ الأَرْبَعَةِ عِنْدَ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ، وَهُوَ : المُشْتَرَكُ - بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ -، وَهُوَ : مَا تَعَدَّدَ مُسَمَّاهُ؛ أَيْ : مَا وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ وَضْعاً حَقِيقِيًّا ؛ بِأَنْ وُضِعَ لِمَعْنَى بِخُصُوصِهِ، ثُمَّ لِمَعْنَى آخَرَ بِخُصُوصِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ نَقْلِهِ مِنَ الْمَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ إِلَى الثَّانِي كَـ: «عَيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْبَاصِرَةِ ، وَمَوْضُوعٌ أَيْضاً لِلْجَارِيَةِ، وَكَذَا لِلنَّقْدِ، وَغَيْرِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ نَقْلِهِ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ لِآخَرَ، وَلَيْسَ مَوْضُوعاً لِمَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ هُوَ قَدْرٌ مُشْتَرَكٌ بَيْنَهَا، وَكَذَا : القِرْءُ» لِلظُّهْرِ وَالحَيْضِ ، بِخِلَافِ نَحْوِ : إِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ مُنْفَرِدٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ مُسَمَّاهُ مَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ لَا تَعَدُّدَ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ: «الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ ، وَإِنَّمَا تَعَدَّدَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ كَـ : «زَيْدِ، وَ : بَكْرٍ ، فَهُوَ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ هُوَ القَدْرُ المُشْتَرَكُ بَيْنَهَا، وَإِنَّمَا أُطْلِقَ عَلَى كُلِّ مِنْهَا إِنْسَانٌ ؛ لِوُجُودِ مُسَمَّاهُ فِيهِ، لَا لِوَضْعِهِ لَهُ . وَكَذَا : «أَسَدٌ» مُسَمَّاهُ مَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ هُوَ (۱) : الحَيَوَانُ المُفْتَرِسُ، وَالتَّعَدُّدُ فِي أَفْرَادِهِ فَقَطْ ، وَأَمَّا إِطْلَاقُهُ عَلَى الرَّجُلِ الشُّجَاعِ فَهُوَ مَجَازٌ ؛ لِعَلَاقَةِ المُشَابَهَةِ فِي الجُرْأَةِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ بِسَبَبِهِ مُشْتَرَكاً .
The third division of the four divisions according to the author: that the word is one and its meaning is multiple, and it is: the mushtarak (homonym) - with fat-ha on the ra' - and it is: that whose referent (musamma) is multiple; i.e., that which was placed for it with a real placement (wad'an haqiqiyyan); by it being placed for a particular meaning, then for another particular meaning without considering its transfer from the first meaning to the second, like 'ayn, for it is placed for the eye, and also placed for a flowing spring, and likewise for coin, and others without considering its transfer from one to another, and it is not placed for a single meaning that is a common ground between them. And likewise 'qur'' for purity and menstruation. Unlike something like 'insan', for it is mufrad (single), because its referent is one meaning with no multiplicity in it, and it is 'the rational animal', and its individuals are multiple like Zayd and Bakr. So it is placed for one meaning which is the common ground between them, and it is only applied to each of them as 'insan' due to the existence of its referent in them, not by its being placed for them. And likewise 'asad': its referent is one meaning, which is the beast of prey, and multiplicity is only in its individuals. As for its application to a brave man, it is metaphorical due to the relation of similarity in boldness, so it is not on account of that a mushtarak.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This is a definitional passage introducing the third division of the four types of word-meaning relationships. The author defines al-mushtarak (homonym) as a word that has multiple distinct meanings, each by a separate real (haqiqi) placement, without any transfer from one meaning to another. For example, the word 'ayn is placed for the eye, a spring, coin, etc., independently. Similarly, qur' means both purity and menstruation. Then the author contrasts this with mufrad (single) words like 'insan' (human), which has one meaning (rational animal) but applies to many individuals (Zayd, Bakr) due to the presence of that meaning in them, not because it was independently placed for each individual. The word 'asad' (lion) is also not mushtarak because its primary meaning is the predatory animal; calling a brave man 'asad' is a metaphorical usage (majaz) based on similarity in boldness, not a separate real placement.

مشترك
وضع حقيقي
مسمى

According to the text, which of the following is an example of mushtarak (homonym)?
A) 'Insan' because it applies to many people like Zayd and Bakr.
B) 'Asad' because it is used for the animal and a brave man.
C) 'Ayn' because it refers to the eye, a spring, and coin.
D) 'Burr' and 'hintah' because they both refer to wheat.

Key Takeaway
A mushtarak word requires multiple meanings each by a separate real placement, not just multiple uses or applications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the Arabic word 'salam' (as in peace, greeting, and also a tree). Determine whether it qualifies as mushtarak according to the conditions in this passage. Provide reasoning referencing each condition.
?
Find another example of mushtarak in the Qur'an (e.g., 'umma' or 'amin') and explain why it meets the definition.

Distinguishing Mushtarak from Mufrad: One Meaning, Many Individuals
Author distinguishes mushtarak from mufrad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a mufrad word (single meaning, multiple individuals) from a mushtarak word (multiple meanings).

Core Idea
A word like 'insan' is not mushtarak—it has one meaning that applies to many individuals.

A common confusion is mistaking words with many referents for homonyms. The author clarifies that 'insan' (human) has one meaning (rational animal) but applies to all humans. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct lexical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify what makes a word mufrad
From the passage, note that 'insan' is mufrad (munfarid) because its musamma is one meaning with no multiplicity in it. The definition of insan is al-hayawan al-natiq (rational animal).
Mufrad here does not mean 'singular' in morphology, but 'having a single meaning' in this classification.
2
Observe that individuals are not meanings
The word insan applies to Zayd and Bakr, but that multiplicity is in individuals, not in the meaning. The word is placed for the single common meaning shared by all individuals.
This is the key difference from mushtarak: multiplicity in mushtarak is in the meanings themselves.
3
Apply the distinction to a new example
Consider the word 'shajar' (tree). It has one meaning (a type of plant) but applies to many individual trees. Determine if it is mufrad or mushtarak based on the passage's criteria.
This reinforces the analytical skill.
4
Contrast with a mushtarak word like 'ayn'
Compare with 'ayn' which has multiple meanings (eye, spring, coin) that are independent. This contrast highlights that the multiplicity in mufrad is not in the meaning but in the instances.
The author explicitly provides this contrast in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِسْمُ الثَّالِثُ مِنَ الأَقْسَامِ الأَرْبَعَةِ عِنْدَ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَعَدَّدَ مَعْنَاهُ، وَهُوَ : المُشْتَرَكُ - بِفَتْحِ الرَّاءِ -، وَهُوَ : مَا تَعَدَّدَ مُسَمَّاهُ؛ أَيْ : مَا وُضِعَ هُوَ لَهُ وَضْعاً حَقِيقِيًّا ؛ بِأَنْ وُضِعَ لِمَعْنَى بِخُصُوصِهِ، ثُمَّ لِمَعْنَى آخَرَ بِخُصُوصِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ نَقْلِهِ مِنَ الْمَعْنَى الأَوَّلِ إِلَى الثَّانِي كَـ: «عَيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِلْبَاصِرَةِ ، وَمَوْضُوعٌ أَيْضاً لِلْجَارِيَةِ، وَكَذَا لِلنَّقْدِ، وَغَيْرِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ اعْتِبَارِ نَقْلِهِ مِنْ وَاحِدٍ لِآخَرَ، وَلَيْسَ مَوْضُوعاً لِمَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ هُوَ قَدْرٌ مُشْتَرَكٌ بَيْنَهَا، وَكَذَا : القِرْءُ» لِلظُّهْرِ وَالحَيْضِ ، بِخِلَافِ نَحْوِ : إِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ مُنْفَرِدٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ مُسَمَّاهُ مَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ لَا تَعَدُّدَ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ: «الحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ ، وَإِنَّمَا تَعَدَّدَتْ أَفْرَادُهُ كَـ : «زَيْدِ، وَ : بَكْرٍ ، فَهُوَ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ هُوَ القَدْرُ المُشْتَرَكُ بَيْنَهَا، وَإِنَّمَا أُطْلِقَ عَلَى كُلِّ مِنْهَا إِنْسَانٌ ؛ لِوُجُودِ مُسَمَّاهُ فِيهِ، لَا لِوَضْعِهِ لَهُ . وَكَذَا : «أَسَدٌ» مُسَمَّاهُ مَعْنَى وَاحِدٌ هُوَ (۱) : الحَيَوَانُ المُفْتَرِسُ، وَالتَّعَدُّدُ فِي أَفْرَادِهِ فَقَطْ ، وَأَمَّا إِطْلَاقُهُ عَلَى الرَّجُلِ الشُّجَاعِ فَهُوَ مَجَازٌ ؛ لِعَلَاقَةِ المُشَابَهَةِ فِي الجُرْأَةِ، فَلَا يَكُونُ بِسَبَبِهِ مُشْتَرَكاً .
The third division of the four divisions according to the author: that the word is one and its meaning is multiple, and it is: the mushtarak (homonym) - with fat-ha on the ra' - and it is: that whose referent (musamma) is multiple; i.e., that which was placed for it with a real placement (wad'an haqiqiyyan); by it being placed for a particular meaning, then for another particular meaning without considering its transfer from the first meaning to the second, like 'ayn, for it is placed for the eye, and also placed for a flowing spring, and likewise for coin, and others without considering its transfer from one to another, and it is not placed for a single meaning that is a common ground between them. And likewise 'qur'' for purity and menstruation. Unlike something like 'insan', for it is mufrad (single), because its referent is one meaning with no multiplicity in it, and it is 'the rational animal', and its individuals are multiple like Zayd and Bakr. So it is placed for one meaning which is the common ground between them, and it is only applied to each of them as 'insan' due to the existence of its referent in them, not by its being placed for them. And likewise 'asad': its referent is one meaning, which is the beast of prey, and multiplicity is only in its individuals. As for its application to a brave man, it is metaphorical due to the relation of similarity in boldness, so it is not on account of that a mushtarak.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This is a definitional passage introducing the third division of the four types of word-meaning relationships. The author defines al-mushtarak (homonym) as a word that has multiple distinct meanings, each by a separate real (haqiqi) placement, without any transfer from one meaning to another. For example, the word 'ayn is placed for the eye, a spring, coin, etc., independently. Similarly, qur' means both purity and menstruation. Then the author contrasts this with mufrad (single) words like 'insan' (human), which has one meaning (rational animal) but applies to many individuals (Zayd, Bakr) due to the presence of that meaning in them, not because it was independently placed for each individual. The word 'asad' (lion) is also not mushtarak because its primary meaning is the predatory animal; calling a brave man 'asad' is a metaphorical usage (majaz) based on similarity in boldness, not a separate real placement.

Key Terms
منفرد
مسمى
فرد

B) Because its meaning is one (rational animal) and multiplicity is only in individuals.
C) Because it is a proper noun.
D) Because it cannot be used metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
A word with one meaning that applies to many individuals is mufrad, not mushtarak.

The Boundary between Mushtarak and Majaz: Metaphor Does Not Create Homonymy
Author distinguishes mushtarak from metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a mushtarak word and a word used metaphorically (majaz), recognizing that metaphorical usage does not constitute a separate real placement.

Core Idea
Calling a brave man 'asad' is metaphorical, not a second meaning—therefore it is not mushtarak.

The author uses 'asad' to illustrate a crucial boundary: metaphorical extension (majaz) does not create a homonym. Understanding this distinction prevents misclassification of words with figurative uses as mushtarak.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meaning of 'asad'
From the passage, the primary meaning of 'asad' is al-hayawan al-muftaris (the beast of prey). This is the real (haqiqi) meaning placed by wad'.
The author explicitly states: 'its referent is one meaning, which is the beast of prey'.
2
Understand the metaphorical usage
When 'asad' is applied to a brave man, it is not a second placement. Instead, it is a metaphorical transfer based on the relation of similarity (mushabaha) in boldness.
The passage says: 'its application to a brave man is metaphorical due to the relation of similarity in boldness'.
3
Distinguish from mushtarak
For a word to be mushtarak, each meaning must have an independent real placement. Since the usage for a brave man lacks such a placement, 'asad' is not mushtarak.
Compare with 'ayn', which has multiple independent real placements.
4
Apply the principle to another example
Consider the word 'yad' (hand). It can mean literally the hand, metaphorically power (e.g., 'yad Allah'), or favour. Determine which usages are haqiqi and which are majaz, and whether 'yad' is mushtarak.
This tests the ability to apply the distinction.

Source Text
