Definition of Mutaradif (Synonym)
Author defines the fourth division of word-meaning relationship (mutaradif) and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mutaradif (synonym) among word-meaning relationships in Arabic and distinguish it from mushtarak (homonym) using the conditions of multiple words and single meaning.

Core Idea
Synonyms are multiple words riding a single meaning—a relationship between words, not between word and referent.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth division of word-meaning relationships: mutaradif (synonym). Understanding this classification is essential for interpreting Arabic texts, especially in fiqh and kalam, where synonymy affects legal and theological reasoning. Let us explore the definition and its examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions for mutaradif
Notice that mutaradif requires both multiplicity of words (lafz muta'addid) and unity of meaning (ma'na wahid). The author explicitly states: 'the word is multiple and its referent is one'.
The relationship is between words, not between word and meaning.
2
Examine the given examples
Look at the three sets: wheat (burr, hintah, qamh), human (insan, bashar), lion (asad, sabu'). These are distinct word forms sharing one meaning. Reflect on why these are considered synonymous in Arabic.
In Islamic terminology, these are classic examples though slight regional/stylistic differences may exist.
3
Reflect on the etymological image
The term mutaradif derives from 'riding' (taraduf). The author says two words ride upon the single meaning, like two riders on one mount. This imagery reinforces that the meaning is the same for both words.
Understanding the etymology helps remember the concept.
4
Consider the note about Ibn al-Hajib and al-Subki
The author notes that this division is similar to that of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Subki. This tells you that the classification is standard across the tradition and widely accepted by prominent scholars.
Recognizing scholarly consensus strengthens the authority of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِسْمُ الرَّابِعُ : أَنْ يَتَعَدَّدَ اللَّفْظُ وَيَتَّحِدَ مُسَمَّاهُ، وَهُوَ : المُتَرَادِفُ، وَهُوَ : اللَّفْظُ المُتَعَدِّدُ الَّذِي اتَّحَدَ مَعْنَاهُ كَـ: «البُرِّ ، وَ الحِنْطَةِ، وَ القَمْح وَكَـ : الإِنْسَانِ، وَ البَشَرِ»، وَكَـ: «الأَسَدِ، وَ السَّبُعِ . سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لِتَرَادُفِ اللَّفْظَيْنِ عَلَى المَعْنَى الوَاحِدِ، كَأَنَّهُ مَرْكُوبٌ لَهُمَا ، وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّهُ نِسْبَةٌ بَيْنَ الأَلْفَاظِ . تَنْبِية: هَذَا التَّقْسِيمُ نَحْوُهُ لِابْنِ الحَاجِبِ وَالسُّبْكِي
The fourth division: that the word is multiple and its referent is one, and it is: the mutaradif (synonym), and it is: the multiple word whose meaning is one, like 'al-burr', 'al-hintah', and 'al-qamh' (wheat), and like 'al-insan' and 'al-bashar' (human), and like 'al-asad' and 'al-sabu'' (lion). It is named thus due to the two words riding upon the single meaning, as if the meaning is ridden by them. And you have known that it is a relationship between words. A note: this division is similar to that of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Subki.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of the four word-meaning relationships: mutaradif (synonym). It occurs when multiple words share a single referent (musamma) or meaning. Examples include 'al-burr', 'al-hintah', and 'al-qamh' all meaning wheat; 'al-insan' and 'al-bashar' both meaning human; 'al-asad' and 'al-sabu'' both meaning lion. The term 'mutaradif' is derived from the idea of two words riding on the same meaning (like two riders on one mount). The author notes that this relationship is between words, not between word and meaning. A concluding note (tanbih) indicates that this division is similar to the one found in the works of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Subki, pointing to the commonality of this classification in the tradition.

Key Terms
مترادف (mutaradif): Multiple words that have a single meaning, i.e., synonyms.
لفظ متعدد (lafz muta'addid): Multiple distinct word forms.
معنى واحد (ma'na wahid): A single meaning or concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is given as an example of mutaradif in the text?
A) 'Ayn' for eye and spring.
B) 'Insan' for rational animal.
C) 'Burr' and 'hintah' for wheat.
D) 'Asad' for lion and brave man.

Key Takeaway
Mutadif is defined as multiple words sharing a single meaning, exemplified by words for wheat, human, and lion, and it is a relationship between words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Correct Three Divisions of Lafz
Author refutes objection and presents three independent classifications of lafz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three independent criteria for classifying lafz and explain why the objection of overlap is a fallacy.

Core Idea
Words are classified by three different considerations; overlap is only apparent when you confuse the criteria.

A common objection to the division of lafz claims that categories like mutaradif and mutawati' overlap, and that the classification confuses relations among words with relations to meanings. In this passage, the commentator refutes this objection by providing three separate binary divisions based on distinct aspects: number of meanings, equality of individuals, and relation to another word. Understanding these distinctions is essential for precise terminology in Islamic logic and the sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claims
Read the first part of the passage. The objector says that the divisions overlap (e.g., mutaradif can also be mutawati' or mushakkik) and that the classification confuses the relation of words among themselves with their relation to meanings.
2
Extract the author's three-fold division
The author proposes three separate divisions: (1) by number of referents: mushtarak vs. munfarid; (2) by equality of individuals: mutawati' vs. mushakkik; (3) by relation to another word: mutaradif vs. mutabayin.
These are not to be merged into one division.
3
Analyze the refutation of overlap
The commentator argues there is no overlap because each division considers a different aspect. For example, mutaradif is considered as multiple words for one meaning, not as one word for multiple meanings. This resolves the apparent overlap.
The commentator calls the objection a fallacy (مغالطة).
4
Apply the divisions to an example
Consider the word 'ayn (عين) which means both 'eye' and 'spring'. How would it be classified under each division? It is mushtarak (one word, multiple meanings), munfarid (one word per meaning? careful), and with respect to another word, it may be mutabayin or mutaradif.
The commentator uses 'ayn to illustrate tabayun within a single word.

وَاعْتُرِضَ : بِأَنَّ الأَقْسَامَ فِيهِ مُتَدَاخِلَةٌ ، فَإِنَّ المُتَرَادِفَ يَكُونُ [١/ ٢٤] مُتَوَاطِئاً، وَيَكُونُ مُشَكِّكاً مَثَلاً ، وَكَذَا المُتباينَ، وَأَيْضاً فِيهِ تَخْلِيطُ نِسْبَةِ الأَلْفَاظِ فِيمَا بَيْنَهَا بِنِسْبَتِهَا لِلمَعَانِي، فَاللَّائِقُ أَنْ يُقَسَّمَ اللَّفْظُ ثَلَاثَ تَقْسِيمَاتٍ :
أَحَدُهَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ تَعَدُّدِ مُسَمَّاهُ وَوَحْدَتِهِ ، يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى مُشْتَرَكِ، وَإِلَى مُنْفَرِدٍ . ثَانِيهَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ تَسَاوِي أَفْرَادِهِ فِي مَعْنَاهُ وَاخْتِلَافِهَا، يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى مُتَوَاطِئ، وَإِلَى مُشَكِّك .
ثَالِتُهَا : بِاعْتِبَارِ نِسْبَتِهِ إِلَى لَفْظ آخَرَ ، يَنْقَسِمُ : إِلَى مُتَرَادِفٍ، وَإِلَى مُتَبَاين. وَلَا يَلِيقُ أَنْ تُجْعَلَ التَّقْسِيمَاتُ الثَّلَاثُ تَقْسِيماً وَاحِداً، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى المُوَفِّقُ .
قوله في التنبيه: (وَاعْتُرِضَ) فيه نظر، فإنَّه لا تداخل ؛ لاختلاف المدركين؛ لأنَّا لا ننظر في المترادف لمعانيه، وإنَّما ننظر لهذه الألفاظ منسوبة لهذا المعنى
قوله : (وَكَذَا المُتَبَاينَ) معناه : إذا كان كلُّ لفظ منه موضوعاً لمعنى واحد، فيصدق على كل : منهما أنَّه لفظ واحدٌ لمعنى واحد فيكون داخلاً في القسم الأوّل ؛ لأنَّه اتَّحد لفظه ومعناه، وذلك
مخل بالتقسيم .
قلت : هذا الاعتراض مغالطةٌ، وذلك أنَّ بين القسمين كمال المغايرة؛ إذ القسم الأوّل لفظ هو واحد لمعنى واحد، وهذا الثَّاني لفظ متعدّد لمعنى متعدد، ولا شك في افتراقهما بهذا الاعتبار، فلا التفات إلى القسم الأول.
قوله : (وَأَيْضاً فِيهِ تَخْلِيطُ فيه نظر؛ لأنَّ الألفاظ لا نسبة بينها باعتبار أنفسها، وإنَّما النسبة فيها باعتبار المعنى، ويوضّح لك ذلك : أنَّ التَّباين يكون مع اتحاد اللفظ كـ «عين» بالنسبة إلى معانيها، وكذا التّرادف فهذا اللفظ باعتبار ما وضع له يرادف الآخر، وهلم جرا، وعليه: فكان من حقِّ المصنف أن يذكر المنفرد؛ قاله شيخنا ابن منصور .
And an objection was raised: that the divisions in it overlap, for the synonymous (mutaradif) may be univocal (mutawati') and may be analogical (mushakkik) for example, and likewise the distinct (mutabayin). Also, in it there is confusion of the relation of words among themselves with their relation to meanings. So the proper thing is that the word (lafz) be divided into three divisions: one of them: by consideration of the multiplicity of its reference and its unity, it divides into homonym (mushtarak) and single (munfarid). Second: by consideration of the equality of its individuals in its meaning and their difference, it divides into univocal (mutawati') and analogical (mushakkik). Third: by consideration of its relation to another word, it divides into synonymous (mutaradif) and distinct (mutabayin). And it is not fitting that the three divisions be made into one division, and Allah the Exalted is the Grantor of success. His statement in the alert: (And an objection was raised) – there is consideration in it, for there is no overlap due to difference of perspectives, because we do not look at the synonymous (mutaradif) for its meanings; rather we look at these words as related to this meaning. His statement: (and likewise the distinct) – its meaning: if each word of them is placed for a single meaning, then each of them is true as being one word for one meaning, so it enters into the first division because its word and meaning are united, and that spoils the division. I say: this objection is a fallacy, because between the two divisions there is complete difference; since the first division is a word that is one for one meaning, and this second is a word that is multiple for multiple meanings, and there is no doubt in their separation by this consideration, so no attention is given to the first division. His statement: (And also in it there is confusion) – there is consideration in it, because words have no relation among themselves in themselves; rather the relation in them is by virtue of meaning. And that is clarified to you by: that distinctness (tabayun) may be with unity of the word, like 'ayn in relation to its meanings, and likewise synonymity (taraduf); so this word, by virtue of what it is placed for, is synonymous with the other, and so on. And upon that, it was the right of the author to mention the single (munfarid); said by our teacher Ibn Mansur.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critical commentary on the division of lafz (word). The author begins by reporting an objection: the standard division of lafz into mutaradif, mutawati', mushakkik, and mutabayin is criticized for overlap—for example, mutaradif can also be mutawati' or mushakkik, and mutabayin can also be mushtarak. The objector further claims that the classification confuses relations among words with relations to meanings. The commentator responds by proposing three separate divisions based on distinct criteria: (1) by number of meanings: mushtarak (one word, multiple meanings) vs. munfarid (one word, one meaning); (2) by equality of individuals in meaning: mutawati' (univocal) vs. mushakkik (analogical); (3) by relation to another word: mutaradif (synonym) vs. mutabayin (distinct). He insists these three should not be merged into one. He then refutes the objection in detail: the alleged overlap is a fallacy because each division considers a different aspect. For example, mutaradif is looked at as multiple words for one meaning, not as multiple meanings for one word. The commentator also addresses the claim of confusion: words have no relation in themselves; relation is through meaning. He uses the example of 'ayn (eye/spring) to show tabayun can occur with a single word having multiple meanings. He concludes that the author of the matn should have included munfarid in the first division. This passage clarifies the correct taxonomy of lafz, which is foundational for understanding subsequent discussions in logic about terms and definitions.

مُتَوَاطِئ
مُشَكِّك
مُشْتَرَك
مُنْفَرِد
مُتَبَايِن

Lafz is classified by three different considerations; apparent overlap is a fallacy arising from confusing the criteria.

Division and Classification

8 min
The Threefold Division of Compound Speech: Khabar, Talab, and Insha'
Author presents threefold division of compound speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish khabar, talab, and insha' based on the criterion of meaning actualization.

Core Idea
Khabar reports independent reality; talab demands future action; insha' creates reality in speech.

This passage introduces the foundational division of complete compound speech (kalam) into three categories: report (khabar), demand (talab), and creation (insha'). Understanding this division is crucial for analyzing propositions and arguments in logic, as only khabar can be true or false and forms the building blocks of proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching category: kalam (complete compound speech)
The passage divides kalam, which is a complete sentence conveying a meaning. Note that this division applies only to compound utterances, not single words.
Kalam in logic is equivalent to a proposition (qadiyya).
2
Understand the criterion: how meaning relates to utterance
The key question: does the meaning exist in the external world independently of the utterance? If yes, it is khabar. If the meaning is only brought into existence by the utterance, then it is either insha' (coincident with utterance time) or talab (posterior to utterance time).
This criterion is based on temporal relation and ontological status.
3
Analyze examples for each type
Khabar: 'Zayd is standing' – the standing of Zayd exists regardless of the statement. Insha': 'I sold' – the sale is effected by the utterance. Talab: 'Stand!' – the commanded action is to occur after the command.
Insha' is also called 'tanbih' (alert) because it alerts the listener to the speaker's intention.
4
Recognize the subtypes within talab
The matn mentions that talab includes command (amr) with superiority, its opposite (supplication, du'a), and request (iltimas) when the speaker and hearer are equal. This shows that talab covers a range of directives.
This subtletly is important for later discussions in usul al-fiqh and grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٣٦) أَمْرُ مَعَ اسْتِعْلَا، وَعَكْسُهُ: «دُعَا»
قوله: (وَاللَّفْظُ : إِمَّا طَلَبٌ ... إلخ) أي : اللفظ المركب، فحذف صفته لقرينة دلالة
سياق التقسيم على ذلك ؛ لأنَّ الطَّلب والخبر إنَّما يكونان في المركَّبات.

فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ لَهُمْ فِي تَقْسِيمِ الكَلَامِ إِلَى خَبَرٍ وَغَيْرِهِ طَرِيقَتَيْنِ (۲) : -
(١) - إِحْدَاهُمَا : أَنَّ القِسْمَةَ ثُلاثِيَّةٌ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : خَبَرٍ، وَطَلَبٍ، وَإِنْشَاء. وَوَجْهُ ذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الكَلَامَ إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَحَقَّقَ مَدْلُولُهُ فِي الخَارج بِدُوْنِهِ وَهُوَ الخَبَرُ كَـ : «زَيْدٍ قَائِم»، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَتَحَقَّقَ مَدْلُولُهُ إِلَّا بِهِ، وَهَذَا إِمَّا أَنْ يُفَارِقَ مَعْنَاهُ زَمَنَ الشَّكَلُم بِهِ وَهُوَ الإِنْشَاءُ كَـ: «بِعْتُ ، وَ أَنْكَحْتُ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَتَأَخَّرَ عَنْهُ وَهُوَ الطَّلَبُ كَـ: «قُمْ، وَ اضْرِبْ
قوله : (وَكَانَ حَقُّهُ أَنْ لَوَ أَخَرَ ... إلخ) أجيب عنِ النَّاظم: بأنَّ ما عدا الخبر، وهو مِن قبيل التَّصور، فلذلك قدَّم النَّاظم هذا الفصل. ورد : بأنَّ المقصود إنَّما هو الخبر، وغيرُ الخبر إنَّما هو تابع، فلا يلاحظ في التَّقدم، وإلى ردّ هذا الجواب أشار الشَّارح بقوله : لأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الخَبَرُ». >>
قوله: (كَمَا أَنَّ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ لَوَ قَيَّدَ اللَّفْظَ بِـ (المُرَكَّب) أشار به للاعتراض على الناظم حيث لم
يقيد اللفظ بالمركَّب.
ويجاب عنه : بأنَّه حذفه لقرينة كما تقدَّم .
قوله : (إِحْدَاهُمَا : أَنَّ القِسْمَةَ ثُلاثِيَّةٌ) أي : فيكون الطلب قسيماً للإنشاء، لا قسيماً منه، والتنبيه ،مراد، فالإنشاءُ لا أخصَّ منه ؛ يقال له : «تنبيه» ؛ لأنَّك نبهت به على مرادك، و: «إنشاء»؛ لأنَّك أنشأته ؛ أي : ابتكرته من غير أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج، وهذه الطريقة هي طريقة الإمام ومن تبعه، والطَّريقةُ الثَّانية للبيانيين، وهي المشهورة، وعليها درج شيخنا سيدي حمدون في خريدته»؛
فقال :
(35) And the word: either a demand or a report, and the first [is] three which we will mention, and in equality: 'a request occurred (36) a command with superiority, and its opposite: a supplication.' His statement: (And the word: either a demand... etc.) – i.e., the compound word, so he omitted its adjective due to a contextual indication of division; because demand and report only occur in compounds. Chapter on explaining the relationship of words to meanings. He has finished mentioning the divisions of the simple (mufrad), and this is a commencement from him in mentioning the divisions of the complete compound, which is speech (kalam). And it would have been proper for him if he had delayed this division after the definitions and made it a prelude to mentioning the propositions which are the starting points of arguments, because the intended purpose from it is only the report (khabar), since it is from which arguments are composed. Likewise, it would have been proper for him if he had qualified 'word' with 'compound', because his speech gives the impression that this is from the divisions of the simple, but it is not so. And know that they have, in the division of speech into report and other, two methods: (1) One of them: that the division is threefold, dividing into report, demand, and creation (insha'). And the reason for that is: speech either its meaning is actualized in the external without it – that is report, like 'Zayd is standing'; or its meaning is not actualized except by it – and this either its meaning leaves the time of speaking, and that is creation (insha'), like 'I sold' and 'I married'; or it is posterior to it, and that is demand (talab), like 'Stand' and 'Hit'. His statement: (And it would have been proper for him if he had delayed... etc.) – there was an answer to the author: that other than the report is of the type of conceptualization, so the author advanced this chapter. And it was refuted: that the intended purpose is only the report, and other than the report is merely a follower, so it is not considered in advancement, and to the refutation of this answer the commentator pointed with his statement: 'because the intended purpose is only the report.' His statement: (Likewise, it would have been proper for him if he had qualified the word with 'compound') – he pointed with it to an objection to the author where he did not qualify the word with 'compound'. And it is answered: that he omitted it due to a contextual indication as previously. His statement: (One of them: that the division is threefold) – i.e., then the demand becomes a counterpart of creation, not a part of it, and 'alert' (tanbih) is intended, so creation is not more specific than it; it is called 'alert' because you alert with it to your intention, and 'creation' because you originated it; i.e., you innovated it without it existing in the external. And this method is the method of the Imam and those who followed him, and the second method is of the grammarians (bayaniyun), and it is the well-known one, and upon it our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun proceeded in his 'Kharidah,' and he said:
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the division of compound speech (kalam). The commentator begins by quoting the matn line: 'And the word: either a demand or a report...' and explains that here 'word' means compound word, not simple, because demand and report only occur in compounds. He then introduces a new section (fasl) on the relationship of words to meanings. The commentator notes that the author of the matn has finished discussing the simple word (mufrad) and now begins discussing the complete compound (murakkab tamm), which is kalam (sentence). He criticizes the placement: it would have been better to postpone this division after definitions and make it an introduction to propositions, because the main purpose is the report (khabar), which is the building block of arguments (hujaj). He also objects that the author should have explicitly said 'compound word' to avoid the impression that this belongs to the division of the simple. Then he presents two methods for dividing kalam into report and other. The first method is a threefold division: report (khabar), demand (talab), and creation (insha'). The criterion: if the meaning is actualized externally without the utterance, it is khabar (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'—the state of standing exists independently); if the meaning is only actualized by the utterance, then it is either insha' (where the meaning coincides with the time of utterance, e.g., 'I sold' – the sale is effected by saying it) or talab (where the meaning is posterior to the utterance, e.g., 'Stand' – the command demands future action). The commentator then discusses the response to the criticism of the placement: some argue that non-reports are conceptual (tasawwur) so they are introduced early, but this is refuted because only the report is the goal; non-reports are secondary. He also notes that the omission of 'compound' is acceptable due to context. Finally, he explains that in the threefold division, demand is a separate counterpart to creation, not a subset; creation (insha') is also called 'tanbih' (alert) because it alerts the listener. This method is attributed to an 'Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or al-Amidi), while the second method is that of the grammarians (bayaniyun), which is more famous. The commentator references his teacher Hamdun's work 'al-Kharidah' for the second method.

Key Takeaway
The threefold division of kalam is based on whether the meaning exists independently (khabar), is created by utterance (insha'), or is demanded for the future (talab).

Critique of the Placement: Why This Division Should Come After Definitions
Commentator critiques placement of the division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the commentator's critique of the matn author's pedagogical order and evaluate the responses.

Core Idea
The report (khabar) is the ultimate goal; all else is secondary and should not precede definitions.

The commentator criticizes the matn author for placing the division of speech into demand and report before the section on definitions. He argues that since only khabar is used in constructing arguments (hujaj), the division should come later as an introduction to propositions. This lesson examines the critique and the ensuing debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn author's original order
The matn author (al-Nazim) presented the division of compound speech into talab and khabar right after discussing the simple word (mufrad). The commentator considers this premature.
2
Understand the commentator's first critique
The commentator says: 'It would have been proper for him to delay this division after the definitions and make it a prelude to mentioning the propositions (qadaya) which are the starting points of arguments. Because the intended purpose is only the report (khabar), since arguments are composed of it.'
This critique is about pedagogical sequence: definitions first, then division, then propositions.
3
Examine the response to the critique
A defender of the matn author argues that non-report parts (talab, insha') are of the type of conceptualization (tasawwur), so they are appropriately placed early. The commentator refutes this: the report is the goal; non-report is merely a follower (tabi'), so it does not warrant advancement.
The commentator's refutation is indicated by his phrase 'because the intended purpose is only the report'.
4
Analyze the second critique: missing qualifier
The commentator also objects that the matn author should have said 'compound word' (lafz murakkab) instead of just 'word' (lafz), because his speech gives the impression that this division applies to the simple word, which is false. The answer is that the author omitted it due to a contextual indication, which the commentator accepts.

(٣٥) وَاللَّفْظُ : إِمَّا طَلَبٌ أَوْ خَبَرُ وَأَوَّلٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ سَنُذْكَرُ وَفِي التَّسَاوِي فَ: «الْتِمَاسُ وَقَعَا
(٣٦) أَمْرُ مَعَ اسْتِعْلَا، وَعَكْسُهُ: «دُعَا»
قوله: (وَاللَّفْظُ : إِمَّا طَلَبٌ ... إلخ) أي : اللفظ المركب، فحذف صفته لقرينة دلالة
سياق التقسيم على ذلك ؛ لأنَّ الطَّلب والخبر إنَّما يكونان في المركَّبات.
•
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
فَرَغَ مِنْ ذِكْرِ أَقْسَامِ المُفْرَدِ، وَهَذَا شُرُوعٌ مِنْهُ فِي ذِكْرِ أَقْسَامِ المُرَكَبِ التّامّ ، وَهُوَ الكَلَامُ، وَكَانَ حَقُه أَنْ لَوْ أَخَّرَ هَذَا التَّقْسِيمَ بَعْدَ المُعَرِّفَاتِ، وَجَعَلَهُ مُقَدِّمَةٌ لِذِكْرِ القَضَايَا الَّتِي هِيَ مَبَادِئُ الحُجَج ؛ لِأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ مِنْهُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الخَبَرُ؛ إِذْ هُوَ الَّذِي تَرَكَّبَ (۱) مِنْهُ الحُجَجُ ، كَمَا أَنَّ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ لَوْ قَيَّدَ اللَّفْظَ بِـ (المُرَكَّب؛ لِأَنَّ كَلَامَهُ يُوهِمُ أَنَّ هَذَا مِنْ أَقْسَامِ الْمُفْرَدِ، وَلَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ.
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ لَهُمْ فِي تَقْسِيمِ الكَلَامِ إِلَى خَبَرٍ وَغَيْرِهِ طَرِيقَتَيْنِ (۲) : -
(١) - إِحْدَاهُمَا : أَنَّ القِسْمَةَ ثُلاثِيَّةٌ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى : خَبَرٍ، وَطَلَبٍ، وَإِنْشَاء. وَوَجْهُ ذَلِكَ : أَنَّ الكَلَامَ إِمَّا أَنْ يَتَحَقَّقَ مَدْلُولُهُ فِي الخَارج بِدُوْنِهِ وَهُوَ الخَبَرُ كَـ : «زَيْدٍ قَائِم»، وَإِمَّا أَنْ لَا يَتَحَقَّقَ مَدْلُولُهُ إِلَّا بِهِ، وَهَذَا إِمَّا أَنْ يُفَارِقَ مَعْنَاهُ زَمَنَ الشَّكَلُم بِهِ وَهُوَ الإِنْشَاءُ كَـ: «بِعْتُ ، وَ أَنْكَحْتُ ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَتَأَخَّرَ عَنْهُ وَهُوَ الطَّلَبُ كَـ: «قُمْ، وَ اضْرِبْ
قوله : (وَكَانَ حَقُّهُ أَنْ لَوَ أَخَرَ ... إلخ) أجيب عنِ النَّاظم: بأنَّ ما عدا الخبر، وهو مِن قبيل التَّصور، فلذلك قدَّم النَّاظم هذا الفصل. ورد : بأنَّ المقصود إنَّما هو الخبر، وغيرُ الخبر إنَّما هو تابع، فلا يلاحظ في التَّقدم، وإلى ردّ هذا الجواب أشار الشَّارح بقوله : لأَنَّ المَقْصُودَ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الخَبَرُ». >>
قوله: (كَمَا أَنَّ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ لَوَ قَيَّدَ اللَّفْظَ بِـ (المُرَكَّب) أشار به للاعتراض على الناظم حيث لم
يقيد اللفظ بالمركَّب.
ويجاب عنه : بأنَّه حذفه لقرينة كما تقدَّم .
قوله : (إِحْدَاهُمَا : أَنَّ القِسْمَةَ ثُلاثِيَّةٌ) أي : فيكون الطلب قسيماً للإنشاء، لا قسيماً منه، والتنبيه ،مراد، فالإنشاءُ لا أخصَّ منه ؛ يقال له : «تنبيه» ؛ لأنَّك نبهت به على مرادك، و: «إنشاء»؛ لأنَّك أنشأته ؛ أي : ابتكرته من غير أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج، وهذه الطريقة هي طريقة الإمام ومن تبعه، والطَّريقةُ الثَّانية للبيانيين، وهي المشهورة، وعليها درج شيخنا سيدي حمدون في خريدته»؛
فقال :
(35) And the word: either a demand or a report, and the first [is] three which we will mention, and in equality: 'a request occurred (36) a command with superiority, and its opposite: a supplication.' His statement: (And the word: either a demand... etc.) – i.e., the compound word, so he omitted its adjective due to a contextual indication of division; because demand and report only occur in compounds. Chapter on explaining the relationship of words to meanings. He has finished mentioning the divisions of the simple (mufrad), and this is a commencement from him in mentioning the divisions of the complete compound, which is speech (kalam). And it would have been proper for him if he had delayed this division after the definitions and made it a prelude to mentioning the propositions which are the starting points of arguments, because the intended purpose from it is only the report (khabar), since it is from which arguments are composed. Likewise, it would have been proper for him if he had qualified 'word' with 'compound', because his speech gives the impression that this is from the divisions of the simple, but it is not so. And know that they have, in the division of speech into report and other, two methods: (1) One of them: that the division is threefold, dividing into report, demand, and creation (insha'). And the reason for that is: speech either its meaning is actualized in the external without it – that is report, like 'Zayd is standing'; or its meaning is not actualized except by it – and this either its meaning leaves the time of speaking, and that is creation (insha'), like 'I sold' and 'I married'; or it is posterior to it, and that is demand (talab), like 'Stand' and 'Hit'. His statement: (And it would have been proper for him if he had delayed... etc.) – there was an answer to the author: that other than the report is of the type of conceptualization, so the author advanced this chapter. And it was refuted: that the intended purpose is only the report, and other than the report is merely a follower, so it is not considered in advancement, and to the refutation of this answer the commentator pointed with his statement: 'because the intended purpose is only the report.' His statement: (Likewise, it would have been proper for him if he had qualified the word with 'compound') – he pointed with it to an objection to the author where he did not qualify the word with 'compound'. And it is answered: that he omitted it due to a contextual indication as previously. His statement: (One of them: that the division is threefold) – i.e., then the demand becomes a counterpart of creation, not a part of it, and 'alert' (tanbih) is intended, so creation is not more specific than it; it is called 'alert' because you alert with it to your intention, and 'creation' because you originated it; i.e., you innovated it without it existing in the external. And this method is the method of the Imam and those who followed him, and the second method is of the grammarians (bayaniyun), and it is the well-known one, and upon it our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun proceeded in his 'Kharidah,' and he said:
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the division of compound speech (kalam). The commentator begins by quoting the matn line: 'And the word: either a demand or a report...' and explains that here 'word' means compound word, not simple, because demand and report only occur in compounds. He then introduces a new section (fasl) on the relationship of words to meanings. The commentator notes that the author of the matn has finished discussing the simple word (mufrad) and now begins discussing the complete compound (murakkab tamm), which is kalam (sentence). He criticizes the placement: it would have been better to postpone this division after definitions and make it an introduction to propositions, because the main purpose is the report (khabar), which is the building block of arguments (hujaj). He also objects that the author should have explicitly said 'compound word' to avoid the impression that this belongs to the division of the simple. Then he presents two methods for dividing kalam into report and other. The first method is a threefold division: report (khabar), demand (talab), and creation (insha'). The criterion: if the meaning is actualized externally without the utterance, it is khabar (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'—the state of standing exists independently); if the meaning is only actualized by the utterance, then it is either insha' (where the meaning coincides with the time of utterance, e.g., 'I sold' – the sale is effected by saying it) or talab (where the meaning is posterior to the utterance, e.g., 'Stand' – the command demands future action). The commentator then discusses the response to the criticism of the placement: some argue that non-reports are conceptual (tasawwur) so they are introduced early, but this is refuted because only the report is the goal; non-reports are secondary. He also notes that the omission of 'compound' is acceptable due to context. Finally, he explains that in the threefold division, demand is a separate counterpart to creation, not a subset; creation (insha') is also called 'tanbih' (alert) because it alerts the listener. This method is attributed to an 'Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or al-Amidi), while the second method is that of the grammarians (bayaniyun), which is more famous. The commentator references his teacher Hamdun's work 'al-Kharidah' for the second method.

Key Terms
خبر
طلب
إنشاء
قضية
حجة

Checkpoint

A) Because the division is too complex for beginners
B) Because only khabar is used in arguments, and it should be introduced after defining terms
C) Because the matn author forgot to include important subtypes
D) Because the division should be part of the chapter on simple words

Key Takeaway
The commentator's critique teaches that pedagogical order in logic should reflect the centrality of the proposition (khabar) as the building block of all arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Division and Classification
beginner

The Three Types of Demand: Command, Supplication, and Request
Defines the three types of demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of demand (amr, du'a', iltimas) based on the manner of demand (isti'la', khudhu', neutral) according to the passage.

Core Idea
The manner of superiority or humility, not actual status, defines command and supplication.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational classification of demand (talab) in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh. The passage distinguishes three types based on the manner in which the demand is made: command (amr) with superiority, supplication (du'a') with humility, and request (iltimas) without either. This sets the stage for later discussions on command's legal force.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of demand
Locate the terms 'amr', 'du'a', and 'iltimas' in the Arabic text. Note that they are all subcategories of talab (demand).
2
Extract the defining criterion for each
The passage explicitly ties amr to isti'la' (superiority), du'a' to khudhu' (humility), and iltimas to being free of both. Write down each definition.
Observe that the criterion is the manner (wajh) of demanding, not the actual status of the demander.
3
Test the definitions with examples
Consider a prophet conveying a divine command: he demands with isti'la' as Allah's representative, even though he is humble. This fits the definition of amr, confirming that actual high status is not required.
Similarly, a beggar's plea is du'a' regardless of his social standing.
4
Apply to a usuli scenario
Think of an imperative verb in the Quran, like 'Aqimu al-salah' (Establish prayer). According to this classification, it is amr because it is a demand with isti'la'. This classification affects later rulings on obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
الكَفِّ وَهُوَ النَّهِيُّ ، وَطَلَبَ العِلْمِ بِالمَاهِيَّةِ وَهُوَ الِاسْتِفْهَامُ ، لَكِنَّ المُقْسَمَ مِنْهُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ وَدُعَاءِ وَالتِمَاس هُوَ الأَوَّلُ ، فَالأَمْرُ هُوَ مَا كَانَ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاسْتِعْلَاءِ ؛ أَيْ : طَلَبِ العُلُوِّ ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ عالياً فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ ، أَمْ لَا.
وَالدُّعَاءُ هُوَ مَا كَانَ عَلَى وَجْهِ الخُضُوعِ، وَهُوَ ضِدُّ الاسْتِعْلَاءِ.
وَالالتِمَاسُ هُوَ مَا كَانَ مُجَرَّداً عَنْهُمَا
ثُمَّ اعْتِبَارُ اللِاسْتِعْلَاءِ فَقَطْ فِي الأَمْرِ دُونَ العُلُوِّ هُوَ قَوْلُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ وَالآمِدِيِّ، وَهُوَ أَحَدُ أَقْوَالٍ أَرْبَعَةٍ ; قَالَ ابْنُ الهُمَام فِي تَحْرِيرِهِ : وَهُوَ الحَقُّ
قوله : (هُوَ قَوْلُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ وَالآمِدِيٍّ) هو أيضاً قول الإمام الرازي وأبي الحسين مِنَ المعتزلة، كما صرح بذلك الشبكي .
قوله : (وَهُوَ أَحَدُ أَقْوَالٍ أَرْبَعَةٍ ثانيها : قيل : إنَّه لا يُعتبر فيه علوٌّ ولا استعلاء، وهو ما مشى عليه العضد والبيضاوي، وهو مذهب الأكثر، وصدَّر به السبكي، وقال قدورة: إنَّه الأصح عند علماء الأصول.
Chapter on explaining the relation of utterances to meanings. The refrain, which is the prohibition, and the request for knowledge of the essence, which is the interrogation, but the part divided into command, supplication, and request is the first. So the command is that which is in the manner of superiority, i.e., seeking high status, whether one is actually high in reality or not. The supplication is that which is in the manner of humility, and it is the opposite of superiority. The request is that which is free of both. Then the consideration of only superiority in the command without actual high status is the statement of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi, and it is one of four statements. Ibn al-Humam said in his Tahreer: It is the truth. His statement: 'It is the statement of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi' is also the statement of al-Imam al-Razi and Abu al-Husayn of the Mu'tazila, as al-Subki clarified. His statement: 'And it is one of four statements. The second: It is said that neither high status nor superiority is considered in it, and this is what al-Adud and al-Baydawi followed, and it is the doctrine of the majority, and al-Subki prefaced with it, and Qaddura said: It is the most correct according to the scholars of usul.'
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary explaining the classification of demand (talab) and the conditions of command (amr). The author begins by distinguishing between two types of demand: demand for action (which includes command, supplication, and request) and demand for knowledge (interrogation). He then defines command as a demand made with isti'la' (superiority), whether the commander is actually superior or not. Supplication is the opposite, made with humility, and request is neutral. The author then discusses the condition of isti'la' versus 'uluww (actual high status) in command, presenting the position of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi that only isti'la' matters, not actual high status. He notes that Ibn al-Humam affirms this as the truth. The commentary adds that this view is also held by al-Razi and Abu al-Husayn. The author then lists one of four opinions: the second opinion holds that neither isti'la' nor 'uluww is required, a view attributed to al-Adud, al-Baydawi, the majority, and al-Subki. The passage follows the matn's compressed definitions and expands them by citing authoritative scholars, typical of a sharh.

الأمر
الدعاء
التماس
الاستعلاء

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a command (amr) from a supplication (du'a')?
A) The presence of actual high status (uluww) in the commander.
B) The use of the imperative verb form.
C) The manner of superiority (isti'la') vs. humility (khudhu').
D) The type of action demanded.

Key Takeaway
The classification of demand into command, supplication, and request depends on the manner of expression, not the actual status of the speaker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
A beggar says to a passerby: 'Give me some money.' Is this amr or du'a'? How does the manner of demand (khudhu') make it du'a' regardless of the beggar's actual low status?

Division and Classification

The Condition of Command: Is Actual High Status Required?
Discusses four opinions on the condition of command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the four opinions on whether command requires isti'la' or 'uluww and identify the preferred view according to Ibn al-Humam.

Core Idea
Only the manner of superiority, not actual high status, is needed for a command.

After defining command as a demand with isti'la', scholars debated whether the commander must actually be superior ('uluww). This lesson presents four opinions found in the passage, with the preferred view supporting isti'la' alone. Understanding this debate is critical for determining the legal force of commands from various sources.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish isti'la' from 'uluww
Isti'la' is the manner of superiority in demanding; 'uluww is actual high status. Note that the passage explicitly separates these two concepts.
2
Identify the first opinion
The passage states that 'consideration of only superiority in the command without actual high status' is the view of Ibn al-Hajib, al-Amidi, al-Razi, and Abu al-Husayn. Ibn al-Humam declares it 'the truth.'
This is the preferred view in the passage.
3
Extract the second opinion
The second opinion is that neither 'uluww nor isti'la' is required for command. This is attributed to al-Adud, al-Baydawi, the majority, and others.
The passage notes that this is the 'doctrine of the majority' and considered 'most correct' by Qaddura.
4
Note the missing third and fourth opinions
The passage mentions there are four opinions total but only lists two. Reflect on why the author might omit the other two; perhaps they are less relevant or covered elsewhere.
This is a typical feature of such commentaries—focusing on the key opposing views.
5
Apply to a real example
Consider the command of a prophet: he is not inherently superior to all, but he conveys Allah's command with isti'la'. According to the preferred view, this is still a valid command. According to the second view, it is also valid, but without requiring any superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان نسبة الألفاظ للمعاني
الكَفِّ وَهُوَ النَّهِيُّ ، وَطَلَبَ العِلْمِ بِالمَاهِيَّةِ وَهُوَ الِاسْتِفْهَامُ ، لَكِنَّ المُقْسَمَ مِنْهُ إِلَى أَمْرٍ وَدُعَاءِ وَالتِمَاس هُوَ الأَوَّلُ ، فَالأَمْرُ هُوَ مَا كَانَ عَلَى وَجْهِ الاسْتِعْلَاءِ ؛ أَيْ : طَلَبِ العُلُوِّ ؛ سَوَاءٌ كَانَ عالياً فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ ، أَمْ لَا.
وَالدُّعَاءُ هُوَ مَا كَانَ عَلَى وَجْهِ الخُضُوعِ، وَهُوَ ضِدُّ الاسْتِعْلَاءِ.
وَالالتِمَاسُ هُوَ مَا كَانَ مُجَرَّداً عَنْهُمَا
ثُمَّ اعْتِبَارُ اللِاسْتِعْلَاءِ فَقَطْ فِي الأَمْرِ دُونَ العُلُوِّ هُوَ قَوْلُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ وَالآمِدِيِّ، وَهُوَ أَحَدُ أَقْوَالٍ أَرْبَعَةٍ ; قَالَ ابْنُ الهُمَام فِي تَحْرِيرِهِ : وَهُوَ الحَقُّ
قوله : (هُوَ قَوْلُ ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ وَالآمِدِيٍّ) هو أيضاً قول الإمام الرازي وأبي الحسين مِنَ المعتزلة، كما صرح بذلك الشبكي .
قوله : (وَهُوَ أَحَدُ أَقْوَالٍ أَرْبَعَةٍ ثانيها : قيل : إنَّه لا يُعتبر فيه علوٌّ ولا استعلاء، وهو ما مشى عليه العضد والبيضاوي، وهو مذهب الأكثر، وصدَّر به السبكي، وقال قدورة: إنَّه الأصح عند علماء الأصول.
Chapter on explaining the relation of utterances to meanings. The refrain, which is the prohibition, and the request for knowledge of the essence, which is the interrogation, but the part divided into command, supplication, and request is the first. So the command is that which is in the manner of superiority, i.e., seeking high status, whether one is actually high in reality or not. The supplication is that which is in the manner of humility, and it is the opposite of superiority. The request is that which is free of both. Then the consideration of only superiority in the command without actual high status is the statement of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi, and it is one of four statements. His statement: 'It is the statement of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi' is also the statement of al-Imam al-Razi and Abu al-Husayn of the Mu'tazila, as al-Subki clarified. His statement: 'And it is one of four statements. The second: It is said that neither high status nor superiority is considered in it, and this is what al-Adud and al-Baydawi followed, and it is the doctrine of the majority, and al-Subki prefaced with it, and Qaddura said: It is the most correct according to the scholars of usul.'

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary explaining the classification of demand (talab) and the conditions of command (amr). The author begins by distinguishing between two types of demand: demand for action (which includes command, supplication, and request) and demand for knowledge (interrogation). He then defines command as a demand made with isti'la' (superiority), whether the commander is actually superior or not. Supplication is the opposite, made with humility, and request is neutral. The author then discusses the condition of isti'la' versus 'uluww (actual high status) in command, presenting the position of Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi that only isti'la' matters, not actual high status. He notes that Ibn al-Humam affirms this as the truth. The commentary adds that this view is also held by al-Razi and Abu al-Husayn. The author then lists one of four opinions: the second opinion holds that neither isti'la' nor 'uluww is required, a view attributed to al-Adud, al-Baydawi, the majority, and al-Subki. The passage follows the matn's compressed definitions and expands them by citing authoritative scholars, typical of a sharh.

Key Terms
الأمر
الاستعلاء
العلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which scholars hold the view that only isti'la' (superiority of manner) is required for command, not actual 'uluww (high status)?
A) Al-Adud and al-Baydawi
B) Ibn al-Hajib and al-Amidi
C) The majority of usul scholars
D) Qaddura

Key Takeaway
The preferred view among the cited scholars is that isti'la' alone suffices for command, as argued by Ibn al-Hajib, al-Amidi, and endorsed by Ibn al-Humam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The second opinion states that neither isti'la' nor 'uluww is required. How would this change the analysis of the same hadith? What implications does this have for usul al-fiqh?

Classification of Composite Speech into Meaningful and Meaningless
Author classifies composite speech into significant and insignificant (muhmal).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any composite linguistic expression as either muhmal (insignificant) or musta'mal (significant) and explain the basis for this division.

Core Idea
Not all composite speech conveys meaning; some are meaningless by convention.

In logic, before analyzing the structure of arguments, we must first understand the building blocks: utterances (alfaz). Among composite utterances (al-murakkab al-lisani), a crucial division separates those that have a conventional meaning from those that do not. This passage from the Bannani commentary clarifies that the latter—called muhmal—do exist, contrary to the claim of some scholars. Understanding this division is essential because only musta'mal utterances enter into logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The author divides al-murakkab al-lisani into two types: muhmal and musta'mal. Locate these terms in the Arabic passage.
2
Define muhmal
Muhmal is a composite utterance with no conventional meaning, like delirium (al-hadyan). The author emphasizes its existence, contradicting al-Razi.
3
Define musta'mal
Musta'mal is a composite utterance that has a meaning by convention. It is the opposite of muhmal.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a sample utterance, decide whether it is muhmal or musta'mal based on whether it has a conventional meaning in Arabic.
For example, 'أقيموا الصلاة' is musta'mal; 'hbkj' is muhmal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردت استيفاء ضبط الأقسام، فاعلم: أنَّ المركب اللساني :
- إما مهمل كالهذيان ، وهو : لفظ مركّب لا معنى له بالوضع، وهو موجود، خلافاً للإمام الرازي وليس موضوعاً.
-
أو مستعمل؛ بأن يكون له معنى.
And if you wish to complete the control of the divisions, know that the linguistic composite: either it is insignificant like delirium, and it is a composite utterance with no meaning by convention, and it exists, contrary to al-Imam al-Razi, and it is not conventional; or it is used, meaning it has a meaning.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of composite speech (al-murakkab al-lisani). The author divides it into two types: muhmal (insignificant, like humming or delirium) which has no conventional meaning, and musta'mal (used) which does have meaning. He notes that muhmal exists, contrary to the view of Imam al-Razi. This is a standard logical division: speech is either meaningful or not. The passage is typical of a sharh, expanding on the matn's division of utterances.

Key Terms
المركب اللساني
المهمل
المستعمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) It is a type of command (amr).
D) It does not exist, according to al-Imam al-Razi.

Key Takeaway
Composite speech is either muhmal (meaningless) or musta'mal (meaningful), and only the latter is relevant for logical and legal analysis.

Is Superiority Necessary for a Command?
Critique of the condition of superiority in defining command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether the condition of superiority (isti'la') is necessary for a command (amr) using linguistic evidence from Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Command does not inherently require superiority; usage and poetry prove otherwise.

In usul al-fiqh, the definition of command (amr) is hotly debated. Some scholars insist that superiority (isti'la') is a necessary condition. The author of our passage challenges this claim, citing a verse by the Companion Amr ibn al-As to Mu'awiya. In this lesson, we will examine the argument and its implications for legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about superiority
Find the statement that asserts certainty that the word form in supplication and equality is not called a command. This implies superiority is essential.
The claim is attributed to some scholars; the author questions its certainty.
2
Examine the counterexample
Read the verse by Amr ibn al-As: 'I commanded you a decisive command.' Note the context: Mu'awiya was the commander, not inferior. The word 'amr' is used without superiority.
The author explicitly says 'there is no superiority here' (إذ لا استعلاء هنا).
3
Assess the objection to alternative interpretation
Some claim the verse is a metaphor for consultation. The author argues this is far-fetched because of the emphasis 'decisive command'.
The author says تأويل الأمر بالمشورة بعيد.
4
Conclude the author's position
The author states the correct view is non-requirement of superiority, following the majority.
The phrase الصواب عدم الاشتراط shows the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للقطع بِأَنَّ الصِّبْغَةَ فِي التَّضَرُّع وَالتَّسَاوِي لَا تُسَمَّى أَمْراً . اهـ . وَكَذَا تَجْرِي هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامُ فِي طَلَبِ الكَفِّ أَيْضاً عَلَى هَذَا القَوْلِ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ . قصارة. ووجه الرَّدِّ : أنَّ الأمر هنا مجاز عن المشورة؛ يعني : أنَّه طلب منهم الإشارة ببعض الرأي؛ فالمعنى حينئذٍ : بأيِّ شيءٍ تشيرون عليَّ مِنَ الرَّأي في أمر موسى ، ولا أمر هنا بهذا المعنى. قلت : الصَّوابُ عدم الاشتراط كما هو مذهب الأكثر ؛ لوروده صريحاً في قول عمرو بن العاص الله تعالى عنه : رضي أَمَرْتُكَ أَمْراً جَازِماً فَعَصَيْتَنِي وَكَانَ مِنَ التَّوْفِيقِ قَتْلُ ابْنِ هَاشِمِ إذ لا استعلاء هنا ؛ لأنَّ معاوية كان أمير إذ ذاك، وتأويل الأمر بالمشورة هنا بعيد؛ لأنَّ في تأكيد أمرتك» بقوله : أمراً جازماً تأييد الإرادة الحقيقية ومبعد لإرادة «أشرت»، فليتأمل. والمراد بـ ابن هاشم في البيت رجلٌ من بني هاشم خرج من العراق على سيدنا معاوية رضي الله تعالى عنه، فأمسكه، فأشار عليه سيدنا عمرو رضي الله تعالى عنه بقتله، فخالفه وأطلقه لحلمه، فخرج عليه مرّةً أخرى، فأنشده سيدنا عمرو رضي الله تعالى عنه البيت، فلم يرد بـ «ابن هاشم»: سيدنا علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله تعالى عنه . قوله : (للقطع) قال الكمال عقيبه دعوى القطع : تبع فيه المولى سعد الدين، وهو محل توقف. قوله : (وَكَذَا تَجْرِي هَذِهِ الأَقْسَامُ فِي طَلَبِ الكَفِّ هي وإن كانت جاريةٌ فيه، لكن لا يسمى مع الاستعلاء أمراً، بل نهياً ؛ اللهم إلَّا إذا بنينا على المجاز؛ أخذاً من قولهم : «النَّهي عنِ الشَّيء أمرٌ بضده»، فيكون الشارح كأنَّه أشار إلى جواب ثانٍ عن المصنّف وهو أنَّه أدرَجَ النهي في قسم الأمر . وحاصل ما أشار إليه النَّاظم في البيتين: أنَّ الكلام ينقسم إلى الخبر وإلى الطلب فقط، وأنَّ الثَّاني ينقسم إلى الثلاثة الأقسام.
For the certainty that the form (of the word) in supplication and equality is not called a command. End. And likewise these divisions apply to the request of cessation (prohibition) also according to this saying, and Allah knows best. Summary. And the face of the response: that the command here is a metaphor for consultation; meaning: he asked them for an opinion of some views; so the meaning then: 'With what do you advise me from the view concerning the matter of Moses?' and there is no command here in this sense. I say: The correct view is the non-requirement of the condition (of superiority) as is the school of the majority; because it occurs explicitly in the saying of Amr ibn al-As, may Allah be pleased with him: 'I commanded you a decisive command, and you disobeyed me, and of the success (of Allah) was the killing of Ibn Hashim.' There is no superiority here; because Mu'awiya was the commander at that time, and interpreting the command as consultation here is far-fetched; because in emphasizing 'I commanded you' by his saying 'a decisive command' is a confirmation of the true intention and a distance from intending 'I advised', so let one reflect. And what is meant by Ibn Hashim in the verse is a man from Banu Hashim who came out from Iraq against our master Mu'awiya, may Allah be pleased with him, so he seized him, and our master Amr, may Allah be pleased with him, advised him to kill him, but he opposed and released him due to his forbearance. Then he came out against him another time, so our master Amr recited the verse to him, and he did not mean by 'Ibn Hashim' our master Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him. His saying: 'For the certainty' – al-Kamal said after it: the claim of certainty: he followed in that the master Sa'd al-Din, and it is a place of hesitation. His saying: 'And likewise these divisions apply to the request of cessation (prohibition)' – it is, even though they apply to it, but it is not called a command with superiority, rather a prohibition; unless we build upon metaphor; taking from their saying: 'Prohibition of a thing is a command for its opposite.' So the commentator, as if he indicated a second response to the author, which is that he included the prohibition in the division of command. And the summary of what the poet indicated in the two lines: that speech divides into khabar and talab only, and the second divides into three divisions.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn discussing the division of speech. The author first addresses a claim that the form of words used in supplication and equality is not called a command. He then notes that the same divisions apply to the request of cessation (prohibition). A summary (قصارة) is given. Then a response to an objection is presented: the word 'amr' in the Quranic context of Moses is actually a metaphor for consultation, not a real command, because the Prophet asked for advice. The author then argues that the condition of superiority is not necessary for a command, citing a verse by Amr ibn al-As where he says 'I commanded you a decisive command' to Mu'awiya, even though Mu'awiya was the commander and not inferior. This shows that the word 'amr' can be used without superiority. Then there is a clarification that 'Ibn Hashim' in that verse is not Ali ibn Abi Talib but another man. The commentator then criticizes the claim of certainty by al-Kamal (Ibn al-Humam) who followed Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani, considering it hesitant. Finally, he notes that prohibition can be included in the division of command metaphorically, and concludes with a summary: speech divides into khabar (statement) and talab (request), and talab divides into three divisions. The passage guards against misunderstanding that command always requires superiority, and shows that other dimensions like supplication and equality also produce forms that are not commands but requests.

Key Terms
الأمر
الاستعلاء
الطلب
النَّهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the verse by Amr ibn al-As demonstrates that:
A) command always requires superiority
B) command can be used without superiority
C) command is a metaphor for consultation
D) command is only used by equals

Key Takeaway
The condition of superiority for command is not universally required; the majority of scholars, supported by linguistic evidence, do not stipulate it.

The Division of Speech into Khabar and Talab
Summary of the division of speech into khabar and talab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any utterance as either khabar (statement) or talab (request) and identify the three subdivisions of talab.

Core Idea
All speech is either informative or directive; directive speech includes command, prohibition, and other request forms.

At the end of our passage, the commentator summarizes a fundamental classification derived from the poet's two lines: speech divides into khabar (statement) and talab (request). Talab further divides into three types. This binary division is crucial for understanding how Muslims interpret the Quran and Sunnah. In this lesson, we will unpack this classification and see its application.

Guided Path
1
Understand the binary division
The passage states that speech (الكلام) divides into two: khabar (خبر) and talab (طلب). Khabar is informative and can be true or false; talab is directive and requests an action or cessation.
This is a foundational division in logic and usul al-fiqh.
2
Define khabar
Khabar is speech that can be evaluated as true or false. Example: 'Zayd is standing' is khabar.
The truth value is a key feature.
3
Define talab and its subtypes
Talab is a request. The passage says it divides into three divisions: command (amr), prohibition (nahy), and others (such as supplication, interrogation, entreaty). The passage also notes that prohibition can be considered a type of command metaphorically.
The three divisions are not explicitly listed, but inferred from 'الثلاثة الأقسام'.
4
Apply the classification to Islamic texts
Take a Quranic verse: 'أقيموا الصلاة' – this is talab (command). 'وما خلقنا السماء والأرض' – this is khabar.
Practise classifying verses to internalize the division.

The Nine Types of Composite Utterance: A Complete Classification
Author enumerates the nine types of composite utterance based on meaning and request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the nine-type classification of composite utterance and correctly place any given utterance into its proper category based on meaning, completeness, request, and truth-likelihood.

Core Idea
Every composite utterance falls into one of nine types, determined by meaning, completeness, request, and truth-likelihood.

In this lesson, we unpack the detailed classification of composite utterance as presented by the author. The passage provides a systematic tree of divisions, distinguishing meaningless from meaningful, complete from incomplete, request from non-request, and within request, four subtypes, and within non-request, statement vs. performative. Mastering this taxonomy is essential for understanding how logic and Islamic jurisprudence treat different types of sentences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division
The passage divides composite utterance into meaningless (muhmal) and used (musta'mal). Meaningless has no conventional meaning, like raving. Used has meaning.
Contrary to Imam al-Razi, meaningless utterances exist.
2
Subdivide used utterances
Used utterances are either complete (tamm) or incomplete (ghayr tamm). Incomplete utterances cannot stand alone to convey a full meaning.
Incomplete is not further classified here.
3
Distinguish complete utterances by request
Complete utterances either convey a request or do not. Those that convey a request are further divided into four types based on what is requested and the relationship with the addressee.
Request types: interrogation, command, prohibition, supplication/entreaty.
4
Identify the four request types
Interrogation asks for mention of an essence; command requests an action with superiority; prohibition requests cessation with superiority; supplication and entreaty lack superiority or equality.
Supplication is from inferior to superior; entreaty is between equals.
5
Classify non-request complete utterances
Non-request complete utterances are either those that do not admit truth/falsehood (exclamation/performative) or those that do (statement/khabar). Performatives include verbs of approaching.
The passage says exclamation and performative are two names for the same category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أردت استيفاء ضبط الأقسام، فاعلم: أنَّ المركب اللساني : - إما مهمل كالهذيان ، وهو : لفظ مركّب لا معنى له بالوضع، وهو موجود، خلافاً للإمام الرازي وليس موضوعاً. - أو مستعمل؛ بأن يكون له معنى. والمستعمل : إمَّا تام، أو غيره . والتَّامُّ : إمَّا أن يفيد بالوضع طلباً، أم لا . والمفيد للطلب : - إما أن يفيد طلب ذكر الماهية وهو الاستفهام؛ نحو: «ما هذا؟». - أو طلب تحصيلها الاستعلاء وهو الأمر؛ نحو: «قم». مع - أو طلب الكفّ عنها كذلك، وهو النَّهي؛ نحو: «لا تقعد». أو مع التّساوي والخضوع فهما : دعاء والتماس. وغير المفيد للطلب : - إن كان لا يحتمل صدقاً ولا كذباً فهو : تنبيه، وإنشاء؛ أي: يسمَّى بكلِّ مِنَ الأمرين . - وإن كان يحتمل ما ذكر فهو خبر؛ فهذه تسعة أقسام. وسمِّي الطَّلبُ المجرَّد عن القيدين: «التماساً ؛ لأنَّ الالتماس هو الطلب، وقد وُجد مطلق الطلب. وسمِّي الذي لا يحتمل صدقاً وليس فيه طلب : «إنشاء» ؛ لأنَّ الإنشاء في اللغة: «الإيجاد»، ومنه : وإنَّا أَنشَأْتَهُنَّ إنشاء [الواقعة: ٣٥] ، ومما يدخل في الإنشاء: أفعال المقاربة، كما صرح به السَّعد والرَّضي ؛ لأنَّ معناها : استقرابُ الوقوع ، لا الإخبار بالقرب، وإنَّما الإخبار المذكور لازم لمعناها .
If you wish to fully grasp the control of the divisions, know that composite utterance (al-murakkab al-lisani) is either: - meaningless, like raving, which is a composite utterance with no meaning by convention, and it exists, contrary to Imam al-Razi, and it is not conventional. - or used, in that it has a meaning. And the used is either complete, or other. And the complete is either that it conveys by convention a request, or not. And what conveys a request: - either it conveys a request for mention of the essence, which is interrogation, like 'What is this?' - or a request for its acquisition with superiority, which is command, like 'Stand up.' - or a request for cessation from it likewise, which is prohibition, like 'Do not sit.' Or with equality and supplication, so they are supplication and entreaty. And what does not convey a request: - if it does not admit truth nor falsehood, it is exclamation and performative; i.e., it is called by both of these terms. - And if it admits what was mentioned (truth/falsehood), it is statement (khabar). So these are nine divisions. And the request stripped of the two conditions is called 'iltimas' (entreaty), because al-iltimas is the request, and the absolute request has been found. And what does not admit truth nor falsehood and has no request in it is called 'insha'' (performative), because insha' in language means 'bringing into existence', and from it: 'And We have produced them as a production' (Quran 56:35). And among what enters into insha' are the verbs of approaching (af'al al-muqaraba), as declared by al-Sa'd and al-Radi; because their meaning is the approach of occurrence, not a report of nearness, and the mentioned report is a necessary consequence of their meaning.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of composite utterance into a hierarchy of nine types. The author begins by noting that composite utterance is either meaningless (مهمل) like meaningless speech, or used (مستعمل) with meaning. The used is either complete (تام) or incomplete. The complete either conveys a request or does not. Request-conveying types are further divided based on what is requested: (1) request for mention of essence (استفهام), (2) request for acquisition with superiority (أمر), (3) request for cessation with superiority (نهي), and (4) request with equality (التماس) or supplication (دعاء). The categories that do not convey request are (5) those that do not admit truth/falsehood, called تنبيه (exclamation) or إنشاء (performative), and (6) those that do admit truth/falsehood, which is خبر (statement). Counting carefully: meaningless (1), used incomplete (2), used complete non-request: exclamation/performative (3), statement (4), used complete request: interrogation (5), command (6), prohibition (7), supplication (8), entreaty (9). The passage then explains the naming of 'iltimas' as request without the two conditions (superiority/equality), and 'insha'' as bringing into existence, giving Quranic example and mentioning that verbs of approaching (like 'kada') are considered insha' because they indicate approach, not reporting nearness. This taxonomy is fundamental for understanding how logical analysis treats different types of sentences.

The nine-type classification of composite utterance is a foundational tool for analysing language in Islamic logic and jurisprudence, distinguishing utterances by meaning, completeness, request, and truth-likelihood.

Division and Classification
intermediate

Statement (Khabar) vs Performative (Insha'): The Truth-Condition Divide
Author enumerates the nine types of composite utterance based on meaning and request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khabar (statement) and insha' (performative) based on their truth-conditional properties and provide examples from Islamic law and rhetoric.

Core Idea
Khabar can be true or false; insha' cannot — it brings about a state of affairs.

One of the most crucial distinctions in Islamic logic is between utterances that can be evaluated as true or false (khabar) and those that cannot (insha'). The passage defines insha' as that which does not admit truth or falsehood and is not a request, and it includes performative acts like divorce and verbs of approaching. This lesson dives into this distinction and its application in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two non-request complete utterances
The passage tells us that complete utterances not conveying a request are either those that do not admit truth/falsehood (exclamation/performative) or those that do (statement/khabar).
These are the last two categories in the nine-type classification.
2
Define khabar (statement)
Khabar is a complete utterance that does not convey a request and admits truth and falsehood. For example, 'The sky is blue' can be true or false.
In Islamic theology, khabar is used for reports about Allah and the unseen.
3
Define insha' (performative)
Insha' is a complete utterance that does not convey a request and does not admit truth or falsehood. Its meaning is performative: it brings about a change in reality, like 'I divorce you' or 'I sell you this'.
The passage also includes verbs of approaching (kada, asbaha) as insha' because they indicate approach, not a report.
4
Examine the etymology of insha'
The author notes that insha' literally means 'bringing into existence', quoting Quran 56:35. This reinforces that insha' is not about describing reality but creating it.
This is a key insight for understanding performative utterances.
5
Test the distinction with examples from Islamic law
In Islamic law, a declaration of divorce 'You are divorced' is a classic insha' because it changes the marital status. A report like 'The sun has set' is khabar. The truth of the report can be verified, but the divorce declaration is neither true nor false; it is effective.
This distinction is crucial in validating legal acts.

Three Meanings of 'Kull' and Their Opposites
Author explains the three meanings of 'كل' and their counterparts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of 'كل' (al-kull) and correctly match each with its opposite term from 'كلية', 'جزئية', and 'جزء'.

Core Idea
The term 'whole' has three distinct meanings; each has a unique opposite.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary clarifies the technical terms around 'whole' (كل). In logic and Islamic sciences, the word 'كل' can mean different things depending on context. Understanding these three senses is crucial for interpreting arguments and definitions correctly. The author explains each meaning and its opposite, then cites a poetic summary from his teacher Sayyid Hamdun.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of 'كل'
From the passage, list the three distinct usages: 1) judgment upon all individuals (الحكم على مجموع الأفراد), 2) judgment upon some individuals collectively (الحكم الثابت لبعض الأفراد البعض المجموعيّ), 3) the composite essence from parts (الماهية المركبة من أجزاء).
2
Match each meaning with its opposite
The passage states that 'كلية' (wholeness) opposes the first meaning, 'جزئية' (partiality) opposes the second, and 'جزء' (part) opposes the third. Write down each pairing.
3
Understand the definition of 'جزء'
The author gives a clear definition of 'جزء': 'that from which and from something else a whole is composed.' Identify this as a definition of the part in the sense of a constituent element.
4
Recognize the poetic summary
The passage ends with a line from Sayyid Hamdun's 'Khurida' that encapsulates the three opposites. Note that the 'three' refere to the three meanings of 'كل'.
This helps memorize the relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان الكل والكلية والجزء والجزئية
ويقابل الكلّ بمعنى الماهية المركبة من أجزاء: «الجزء»، وأشار إليه بقوله : و (الجزء) معرفته جليّة) أي : واضحة ، إذ هو : ما تركّب منه ومن غيره كلّ»، فتبيّن ؛ أنَّ كلًّا من الكلية والجزنية والجزء مقابل للكلّ باعتبار أحد إطلاقاته الثلاثة.
قوله : (فتبيّن أنَّ كلًّا من الكلية والجزنية ... إلخ) بيانه : الكلية تقابل الكل؛ بمعنى : الحكم على مجموع الأفراد والجزئية تقابله باعتبار إطلاقه على الحكم الثابت لبعض الأفراد، البعض المجموعيّ، والجزء يقابله باعتبار إطلاقه على الماهية المركبة من أجزاء، وإلى هذا المعنى أشار شيخنا سيدي حمدون في خريدته بقوله :
وقابل الثلاثة المرعية بالجزء فالكلية الجزنية
ومرادُهُ بـ «الثلاثة»: المعاني التي للكلِّ، كما تقدَّم.
Chapter on explaining the whole, wholeness, the part, and partiality. And the whole in the sense of the composite essence from parts is opposed by 'the part', and he indicated it by his saying: 'and the part, its definition is clear', i.e., obvious, for it is that from which and from something else a whole is composed. So it became clear that each of wholeness, partiality, and part is opposite to the whole according to one of its three usages. His saying: 'So it became clear that each of wholeness, partiality, etc.': its explanation: wholeness opposes the whole in the sense of the judgment upon the totality of individuals, and partiality opposes it according to its usage upon the judgment established for some individuals, the collective part, and the part opposes it according to its usage upon the essence composed of parts. And to this meaning our master Sayyid Hamdun alluded in his 'Khurida' by his saying: 'And oppose the three observed by the part, so wholeness, partiality.' And by 'the three' he means the meanings of the whole, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms related to the concept of 'whole' (كل) and its opposites. The author begins by stating that the whole in the sense of a composite essence from parts is opposed by the term 'part' (جزء). He then references the text saying that the definition of part is clear, as it is that from which and from something else a whole is composed. The discussion then clarifies that each of the three terms—wholeness (كلية), partiality (جزئية), and part (جزء)—is opposite to the whole according to exactly one of its three distinct usages. Wholeness (كلية) means the judgment upon the totality of individuals; it opposes the whole in that sense. Partiality (جزئية) opposes the whole when the whole is used for a judgment established for some individuals, specifically the collective part. The part (جزء) opposes the whole when the whole means the composite essence. The author concludes by citing a poetic line from his teacher Sayyid Hamdun that summarizes these three opposites.

الكلية
الجزئية
الجزء

Key Takeaway
The three opposites of 'كل' are 'كلية', 'جزئية', and 'جزء', each corresponding to one precise sense of the whole.
