Three Types of Hujja: Qiyas, Istiqra, Tamthil
Author classifies hujja into three types based on inclusion relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given hujja as qiyas, istiqra, or tamthil based on the inclusion relation between the proof and the conclusion.

Core Idea
Proof is classified by whether it includes the conclusion, is included by it, or shares a common element.

In classical Islamic logic, proof (hujja) is not a monolithic concept. The author divides hujja into three types based on the logical relationship between the evidence and the sought conclusion. This classification is foundational for understanding how arguments work in Islamic sciences, from usul al-fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof and the sought conclusion
In any argument, locate the statements serving as evidence (al-hujja) and the statement being proved (al-matlab).
2
Check the inclusion relation
Determine if the proof includes the conclusion (qiyas), the conclusion includes the proof (istiqra), or neither includes the other but they share a common element (tamthil).
3
Apply the classification to examples
Classify given arguments: e.g., 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, every intoxicant is forbidden' is qiyas because the conclusion 'Nabidh is forbidden' is included in the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا نِزَاعَ فِي ذَلِكَ، وَلِهَذَا كَانَتِ الحُجَّةُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَنْوَاعِ، وَهِيَ : القِيَاسُ، وَالِاسْتِقْرَاءُ، وَالتَّمْثِيلُ . وَوَجْهُ الِانْقِسَامِ إِلَيْهَا : أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَنَاسُبٍ بَيْنَ الحُجَّةِ وَالمَظْلُوبِ؛ إِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ الحُجَّةِ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ، وَيُسَمَّى : قِيَاساً كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ ، وَلِهَذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالكُلّي عَلَى الجُزْئِيِّ ، وَإِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ المَظْلُوبِ عَلَى الحُجَّةِ وَيُسَمَّى: «اسْتِقْرَاء»، وَلِذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالجُزْنِي عَلَى الكُلّيّ ، وَإِمَّا بِغَيْرِ اسْتِمَالٍ مِنْ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الآخَرِ، لَكِنْ لَا بُدَّ مِنِ اشْتِرَاكِهِمَا فِي أَمْرٍ يَشْمَلُهُمَا ، وَتُسَمَّى الحُجَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : «تَمْثِيلاً، وَلِذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالجُزْئِي عَلَى الجُزْئِيِّ . وَمَعْنَى كَوْنِ القِيَاسِ يُفِيدُ القَطعَ : أَنَّهُ يُفِيدُ لُزُومَ النَّتِيجَةِ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ عَلَى فَرْضِ تَسْلِيمِهِمَا، وَلَوْ كَذَبَ الجَمِيعُ ؛ لِأَنَّ القِيَاسَ كَالقَالِبِ تُفْرَعُ فِيهِ المَوَادُّ، فَمَنْ أَفَرَغَ فِيهِ الحَقَّ وَجَدَ النَّتِيجَةَ حَقًّا ، وَمَنْ أَفْرَغَ فِيهِ البَاطِلَ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ لَهُ حَقًّا ؛ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ فَرَسٌ، وَكُلُّ فَرَسٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَالنَّتِيجَةُ حَقٌّ مَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَبِمَا ذَكَرَ يَنْدَفِعُ مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ النَّظْمِ مِنْ كَوْنِ القِيَاسِ المَنْطِقِيِّ لَا تَكُونُ نَتِيجَتُهُ إِلَّا يَقِينِيَّةٌ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، كَمَا سَيَتَبَيَّنُ فِي أَقْسَامِهِ. قوله : (إِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ الحُجَّةِ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ) مثالُهُ: «النبيذ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام»، فالمطلوب الذي هو : النَّبيذُ حرام جزء بالنسبة إلى قولنا : كل مسكر حرام . : فإن قيل : كيف جعلوا هذا القسم الذي هو القياس المنطقي من الاستدلال بالكلّي على الجزئي، أنَّ الأوسط قد يكون مساوياً للأصغر ؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق حيوان»، وأحد المتساويين لا يكون جزئيا إضافيًا من الآخر، فلا يندرج فيه . أجيب : بأنَّ مرجع القياس إلى استفادة الحكم على ذات الأصغر من ملاحظة مفهوم الأوسط وهذا أعم قطعاً. مع قوله : (أَنَّهُ يُفِيدُ لُزُومَ النَّتِيجَةِ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ) فإذا قلت : «النَّبيذُ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام»، فهو قياس منطقيٌّ ؛ لأنَّ النتيجة مقطوع بلزومها، وإن كانت بعد القطع بلزومها ظنيَّة؛ لأنَّ مادة القياس ظنِّيَّة، فإنَّ قولك : وكلُّ مسكر حرام قضيَّةٌ ظنّيّةٌ .
And there is no dispute about that. Therefore the hujja (proof) is of three types: qiyas, istiqra, and tamthil. The basis of the division is that there must be a relation between the proof and the sought conclusion: either the proof includes the sought conclusion, which is called qiyas (as previously), hence it is said: reasoning from the universal to the particular; or the sought conclusion includes the proof, called istiqra, hence it is said: reasoning from the particular to the universal; or neither includes the other, but they share something that encompasses both, then the proof is called tamthil, hence it is said: reasoning from particular to particular. And the meaning of qiyas yielding certainty is that it yields the necessity of the conclusion from the two premises assuming they are conceded, even if both are false. Because qiyas is like a mold into which materials are poured; whoever pours truth into it finds a true conclusion, and whoever pours falsehood does not produce a true conclusion, unless due to the specificity of the material, e.g., 'Every human is a horse, and every horse is rational,' the conclusion is true despite the false premises. With this, the misunderstanding from the poem that logical qiyas only yields certain conclusions is refuted, as will be clarified in its divisions. His statement: 'Either the proof includes the sought conclusion' example: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' The sought conclusion 'Nabidh is forbidden' is a part of 'every intoxicant is forbidden.' If it is said: How did they make this section (logical qiyas) as reasoning from universal to particular given that the middle term may be equal to the minor, e.g., 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal'? One of two equals is not a relative particular of the other, so it does not fall under it. The answer: The essence of qiyas is to derive the ruling on the subject of the minor by considering the concept of the middle, and this is absolutely more general. Along with his statement: 'It yields the necessity of the conclusion from the two premises.' So when you say: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden,' it is a logical qiyas because the conclusion is certain in its necessity, although after certainty of its necessity it is probabilistic if the material is probabilistic. For your statement 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is a probabilistic proposition.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage classifies the three types of proof (al-hujja) based on the logical relation of inclusion between the proof and the desired conclusion. Qiyas (categorical syllogism) is when the proof includes the conclusion, i.e., deduction from universal to particular. Istiqra (induction) is when the conclusion includes the proof, i.e., from particular to universal. Tamthil (analogy) is when neither includes the other but they share a common element that encompasses both. The passage then clarifies that qiyas yields certainty only in the sense of formal necessity: if the premises are conceded, the conclusion necessarily follows, even if the premises are materially false. The example given: "Every human is a horse, every horse is rational" yields a true conclusion "Every human is rational" despite false premises. This refutes the misunderstanding that logical qiyas always yields certain truth; material truth depends on the premises. The commentary addresses an objection about the middle term being equal to the minor, explaining that the reasoning still proceeds from a universal (the middle concept) to the particular (the subject). Finally, it notes that even in a valid qiyas, the conclusion may be probabilistic if the premises are probabilistic.

القياس
الاستقراء
التّمثيل

Key Takeaway
Hujja is classified into qiyas (deduction), istiqra (induction), and tamthil (analogy) based on whether the proof includes the conclusion, is included by it, or shares a common element.

(b) 'Zayd is rational, Amr is rational, Khalid is rational, therefore all humans are rational.' (c) 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; beer also intoxicates, so beer is forbidden.'

Division and Classification
beginner
10 min
The Three Types of Proof: Qiyas, Istiqra', Tamthil
Author defines three types of proof based on inclusion relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given argument as qiyas, istiqra', or tamthil based on the inclusion relation between evidence and conclusion.

Core Idea
Proofs are distinguished by whether the evidence contains the conclusion, the conclusion contains the evidence, or they share a common element.

The author divides proof (hujja) into three types based on the relationship between the evidence (hujja) and the sought (matlub). This classification is fundamental in Islamic logic and helps in identifying the logical structure of arguments used in various Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sought (matlub) and the proof (hujja)
Read the argument and determine what is being concluded (the sought) and what is used as evidence (the proof).
2
Check if the proof includes the sought
If the evidence contains the conclusion as a part (i.e., evidence is more general), it is qiyas (syllogism). Example: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant; every intoxicant is forbidden' – the sought 'nabidh is forbidden' is a particular of the universal 'every intoxicant is forbidden'.
This is described as inference from universal to particular.
3
Check if the sought includes the proof
If the conclusion contains the evidence (i.e., conclusion is more general), it is istiqra' (induction). Example: observing multiple particular instances (like some animals are rational) and concluding a universal (all animals are rational) – the sought includes the evidence.
This is inference from particular to universal.
4
Check for shared attribute without inclusion
If neither includes the other but they share a common property that encompasses both, it is tamthil (analogy). Example: inferring that a new drink is prohibited because it shares the intoxicating property with wine – the evidence (wine is forbidden) and sought (new drink is forbidden) are both particular, linked by the shared attribute 'intoxicant'.
This is inference from particular to particular.
5
Apply to real Islamic examples
Practice by taking arguments from usul al-fiqh or kalam and classifying them. For instance, the argument 'The world is contingent, every contingent has a cause, therefore the world has a cause' is qiyas.
This reinforces the classification and prepares for deeper analysis.

بَيْنَ الحُجَّةِ وَالمَظْلُوبِ؛ إِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ الحُجَّةِ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ، وَيُسَمَّى : قِيَاساً كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ ، وَلِهَذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالكُلّي عَلَى الجُزْئِيِّ ، وَإِمَّا فَمَنْ أَفَرَغَ فِيهِ الحَقَّ وَجَدَ النَّتِيجَةَ حَقًّا ، وَمَنْ أَفْرَغَ فِيهِ البَاطِلَ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ لَهُ حَقًّا ؛ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ فَرَسٌ، وَكُلُّ فَرَسٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَالنَّتِيجَةُ حَقٌّ مَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَبِمَا ذَكَرَ يَنْدَفِعُ مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ النَّظْمِ مِنْ كَوْنِ القِيَاسِ المَنْطِقِيِّ لَا تَكُونُ نَتِيجَتُهُ إِلَّا يَقِينِيَّةٌ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، كَمَا سَيَتَبَيَّنُ فِي أَقْسَامِهِ. قوله : (إِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ الحُجَّةِ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ) مثالُهُ: «النبيذ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام»، فالمطلوب الذي هو : النَّبيذُ حرام جزء بالنسبة إلى قولنا : كل مسكر حرام . : فإن قيل : كيف جعلوا هذا القسم الذي هو القياس المنطقي من الاستدلال بالكلّي على الجزئي، أنَّ الأوسط قد يكون مساوياً للأصغر ؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق حيوان»، وأحد المتساويين لا يكون جزئيا إضافيًا من الآخر، فلا يندرج فيه . أجيب : بأنَّ مرجع القياس إلى استفادة الحكم على ذات الأصغر من ملاحظة مفهوم الأوسط وهذا أعم قطعاً. مع قوله : (أَنَّهُ يُفِيدُ لُزُومَ النَّتِيجَةِ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ) فإذا قلت : «النَّبيذُ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام»، فهو قياس منطقيٌّ ; لأنَّ النتيجة مقطوع بلزومها، وإن كانت بعد القطع بلزومها ظنيَّة; لأنَّ مادة القياس ظنِّيَّة، فإنَّ قولك : وكلُّ مسكر حرام قضيَّةٌ ظنّيّةٌ .
There is no dispute about that. Therefore, the proof (hujja) is of three types: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). The reason for division into these is that there must be a proportionality between the proof and the sought; either the proof includes the sought, and it is called qiyas, as previously, so it is said about it: inference from the universal to the particular; or the sought includes the proof, and it is called istiqra', so it is said about it: inference from the particular to the universal; or neither includes the other, but they must share in something that encompasses both, and then the proof is called tamthil, so it is said about it: inference from the particular to the particular. The meaning of qiyas yielding certainty is that it yields the necessary implication of the conclusion from the two premises, assuming them to be conceded, even if all were false; because qiyas is like a mold into which materials are poured; whoever pours truth into it finds the conclusion true, and whoever pours falsehood into it does not produce truth for him, except due to the specificity of the material; like: 'Every man is a horse, and every horse is rational', so the conclusion is true despite the falsehood of the premises. By what has been mentioned, the misunderstanding from the [poetic] text is repelled, that logical qiyas only yields a certain conclusion, while it is not so, as will become clear in its divisions. His statement: (Either the proof includes the sought) its example: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden', so the sought, which is 'nabidh is forbidden', is a part relative to our statement 'every intoxicant is forbidden'. If it is said: How did they make this division, which is logical qiyas, from inference from universal to particular, while the middle term may be coextensive with the minor term; like: 'Every man is rational, and every rational is animal', and one of two coextensives is not relatively particular to the other, so it does not fall under it? It is answered: The reference of qiyas is to derive the judgment on the essence of the minor term from observing the concept of the middle term, and this is absolutely more general. With his statement: (That it yields the necessary implication of the conclusion from the two premises) when you say: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden', it is a logical qiyas; because the conclusion is certain in its implication, even if after certainty of implication it is probable, because the matter of the qiyas is probable; for your statement 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is a probable proposition.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the classification of proof (hujja) into three types based on the relationship between the proof (evidence) and the sought (conclusion). The author explains that there must be some proportional relation. First, if the proof includes the sought, it is qiyas (syllogism), described as inference from universal to particular. Second, if the sought includes the proof, it is istiqra' (induction), inference from particular to universal. Third, if neither includes the other but they share a common property, it is tamthil (analogy), inference from particular to particular. The author then clarifies that qiyas yields certainty in the sense of formal entailment: given the premises, the conclusion necessarily follows, regardless of the truth of the premises. The example 'every man is a horse, every horse is rational, so every man is rational' shows that the form alone guarantees the consequence, though the premises are false. The author also addresses a potential misunderstanding from the poem that logical qiyas always yields certain conclusions; in fact, the material (premises) may be probable, so the conclusion may be probable. He then provides a detailed example involving nabidh (date-wine) to illustrate the first type: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, every intoxicant is forbidden' – here the sought 'nabidh is forbidden' is a particular instance of the universal premise. An objection is raised that the middle term may be coextensive with the minor term, as in 'every man is rational, every rational is animal', where man and rational are coextensive, so man is not particular relative to rational. The response is that the essence of qiyas is to derive a judgment on the minor term by observing the middle term concept, which is more general. Thus the classification stands.

Division and Classification
intermediate

The Formal Certainty of Qiyas: Form vs. Matter
Author clarifies that qiyas yields formal entailment, not material truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why qiyas yields necessary entailment regardless of the truth of premises, and distinguish between formal validity and material truth.

Core Idea
Qiyas is like a mold: the conclusion necessarily follows the premises in form, but the content may be false.

The author clarifies a key point about qiyas: it gives certainty in the sense of logical entailment, not in the sense of true content. This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope and limits of logical reasoning in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'mold' analogy
The author says qiyas is like a qalib (mold) into which materials (premises) are poured. The mold guarantees the shape (consequence) but not the quality of the material.
2
Analyze the example of false premises with true conclusion
Consider: 'Every man is a horse; every horse is rational; therefore every man is rational.' The premises are false, but the conclusion is true. This shows that the logical form alone guarantees the consequence, not the truth of the premises.
The form is valid (Barbara), but the argument is unsound because premises are false.
3
Recognize that material certainty comes from premises
If the premises are certain (yaqini), the conclusion is certain; if premises are probable (zanni), the conclusion is probable. Qiyas only transmits certainty or probability from premises to conclusion formally.
This counters the misconception that logical qiyas always yields certain knowledge.
4
Distinguish formal validity from soundness
Formal validity is about the necessary connection between premises and conclusion. Soundness requires true premises. In Islamic logic, a qiyas may be valid but the conclusion may be false if premises are false.
This distinction is vital in evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Source Text

وَلَا نِزَاعَ فِي ذَلِكَ، وَلِهَذَا كَانَتِ الحُجَّةُ ثَلَاثَةَ أَنْوَاعِ، وَهِيَ : القِيَاسُ، وَالِاسْتِقْرَاءُ، وَالتَّمْثِيلُ . وَوَجْهُ الِانْقِسَامِ إِلَيْهَا : أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَنَاسُبٍ بَيْنَ الحُجَّةِ وَالمَظْلُوبِ؛ إِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ الحُجَّةِ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ، وَيُسَمَّى : قِيَاساً كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ ، وَلِهَذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالكُلّي عَلَى الجُزْئِيِّ ، وَإِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ المَظْلُوبِ عَلَى الحُجَّةِ وَيُسَمَّى: «اسْتِقْرَاء»، وَلِذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالجُزْئِي عَلَى الكُلّيّ ، وَإِمَّا بِغَيْرِ اشْتِمَالٍ مِنْ أَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الآخَرِ، لَكِنْ لَا بُدَّ مِنِ اشْتِرَاكِهِمَا فِي أَمْرٍ يَشْمَلُهُمَا ، وَتُسَمَّى الحُجَّةُ حِينَئِذٍ : «تَمْثِيلاً، وَلِذَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : الاسْتِدْلَالُ بِالجُزْئِي عَلَى الجُزْئِيِّ . وَمَعْنَى كَوْنِ القِيَاسِ يُفِيدُ القَطعَ : أَنَّهُ يُفِيدُ لُزُومَ النَّتِيجَةِ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ عَلَى فَرْضِ تَسْلِيمِهِمَا، وَلَوْ كَذَبَ الجَمِيعُ ؛ لِأَنَّ القِيَاسَ كَالقَالِبِ تُفْرَعُ فِيهِ المَوَادُّ، فَمَنْ أَفَرَغَ فِيهِ الحَقَّ وَجَدَ النَّتِيجَةَ حَقًّا ، وَمَنْ أَفْرَغَ فِيهِ البَاطِلَ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ لَهُ حَقًّا ؛ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ لِخُصُوصِ المَادَّةِ ؛ نَحْوُ : «كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ فَرَسٌ، وَكُلُّ فَرَسٍ نَاطِقٌ، فَالنَّتِيجَةُ حَقٌّ مَعَ كَذِبِ المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ، وَبِمَا ذَكَرَ يَنْدَفِعُ مَا يُتَوَهَّمُ مِنَ النَّظْمِ مِنْ كَوْنِ القِيَاسِ المَنْطِقِيِّ لَا تَكُونُ نَتِيجَتُهُ إِلَّا يَقِينِيَّةٌ، مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، كَمَا سَيَتَبَيَّنُ فِي أَقْسَامِهِ. قوله : (إِمَّا بِاشْتِمَالِ الحُجَّةِ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ) مثالُهُ: «النبيذ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام»، فالمطلوب الذي هو : النَّبيذُ حرام جزء بالنسبة إلى قولنا : كل مسكر حرام . : فإن قيل : كيف جعلوا هذا القسم الذي هو القياس المنطقي من الاستدلال بالكلّي على الجزئي، أنَّ الأوسط قد يكون مساوياً للأصغر ؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق حيوان»، وأحد المتساويين لا يكون جزئيا إضافيًا من الآخر، فلا يندرج فيه . أجيب : بأنَّ مرجع القياس إلى استفادة الحكم على ذات الأصغر من ملاحظة مفهوم الأوسط وهذا أعم قطعاً. مع قوله : (أَنَّهُ يُفِيدُ لُزُومَ النَّتِيجَةِ لِلمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ) فإذا قلت : «النَّبيذُ مسكر، وكل مسكر حرام»، فهو قياس منطقيٌّ ; لأنَّ النتيجة مقطوع بلزومها، وإن كانت بعد القطع بلزومها ظنيَّة; لأنَّ مادة القياس ظنِّيَّة، فإنَّ قولك : وكلُّ مسكر حرام قضيَّةٌ ظنّيّةٌ .
There is no dispute about that. Therefore, the proof (hujja) is of three types: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). The reason for division into these is that there must be a proportionality between the proof and the sought; either the proof includes the sought, and it is called qiyas, as previously, so it is said about it: inference from the universal to the particular; or the sought includes the proof, and it is called istiqra', so it is said about it: inference from the particular to the universal; or neither includes the other, but they must share in something that encompasses both, and then the proof is called tamthil, so it is said about it: inference from the particular to the particular. The meaning of qiyas yielding certainty is that it yields the necessary implication of the conclusion from the two premises, assuming them to be conceded, even if all were false; because qiyas is like a mold into which materials are poured; whoever pours truth into it finds the conclusion true, and whoever pours falsehood into it does not produce truth for him, except due to the specificity of the material; like: 'Every man is a horse, and every horse is rational', so the conclusion is true despite the falsehood of the premises. By what has been mentioned, the misunderstanding from the [poetic] text is repelled, that logical qiyas only yields a certain conclusion, while it is not so, as will become clear in its divisions. His statement: (Either the proof includes the sought) its example: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden', so the sought, which is 'nabidh is forbidden', is a part relative to our statement 'every intoxicant is forbidden'. If it is said: How did they make this division, which is logical qiyas, from inference from universal to particular, while the middle term may be coextensive with the minor term; like: 'Every man is rational, and every rational is animal', and one of two coextensives is not relatively particular to the other, so it does not fall under it? It is answered: The reference of qiyas is to derive the judgment on the essence of the minor term from observing the concept of the middle term, and this is absolutely more general. With his statement: (That it yields the necessary implication of the conclusion from the two premises) when you say: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, and every intoxicant is forbidden', it is a logical qiyas; because the conclusion is certain in its implication, even if after certainty of implication it is probable, because the matter of the qiyas is probable; for your statement 'every intoxicant is forbidden' is a probable proposition.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the classification of proof (hujja) into three types based on the relationship between the proof (evidence) and the sought (conclusion). The author explains that there must be some proportional relation. First, if the proof includes the sought, it is qiyas (syllogism), described as inference from universal to particular. Second, if the sought includes the proof, it is istiqra' (induction), inference from particular to universal. Third, if neither includes the other but they share a common property, it is tamthil (analogy), inference from particular to particular. The author then clarifies that qiyas yields certainty in the sense of formal entailment: given the premises, the conclusion necessarily follows, regardless of the truth of the premises. The example 'every man is a horse, every horse is rational, so every man is rational' shows that the form alone guarantees the consequence, though the premises are false. The author also addresses a potential misunderstanding from the poem that logical qiyas always yields certain conclusions; in fact, the material (premises) may be probable, so the conclusion may be probable. He then provides a detailed example involving nabidh (date-wine) to illustrate the first type: 'Nabidh is an intoxicant, every intoxicant is forbidden' – here the sought 'nabidh is forbidden' is a particular instance of the universal premise. An objection is raised that the middle term may be coextensive with the minor term, as in 'every man is rational, every rational is animal', where man and rational are coextensive, so man is not particular relative to rational. The response is that the essence of qiyas is to derive a judgment on the minor term by observing the middle term concept, which is more general. Thus the classification stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'qiyas yields certainty'?
A) The conclusion is always true.
B) The conclusion necessarily follows from the premises, regardless of their truth.
C) The premises must be certain.
D) The qiyas is always sound.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas guarantees logical consequence, not material truth; the certainty of the conclusion depends on the certainty of the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every act of disobedience is sinful; every sinful act deserves punishment; therefore every act of disobedience deserves punishment.' Is this argument formally valid? Are the premises certain or probable? What does that imply for the conclusion's certainty?
?
Construct a valid qiyas with false premises that yields a true conclusion, using Islamic subjects (e.g., 'Every believer is a prophet; every prophet is infallible; therefore every believer is infallible'). Explain why the conclusion is true despite false premises.
